THE RED PAGE .
PRAYER IN TIME OF RECKONING.
y y JS'iJ.E' again, Twenty-fifth of December !
§ # we laugh through our tears at
M M the date.
-M. We 're a good thing or two to remember,
As we wipe our regrets off the slate.
Though Pluto may starve us or skimp us,
A fig for the chances we miss !
Who cares, while we 're free of Olympus,
And Hebe laughs low to our kiss ?
0, Mother of Loves, the Almighty,
The ageless, and faultless, and siveet !—
0, exquisite lithe Aphrodite,
Whose slaves are as gods at your feet /
Uphold us, preserve us, befriend us,
Who, save for your strength, were so weak,
Let our lives (from mean virtues defend us !)
Be spacious and fragrant and Greek !
Stand by us, and hold us, and guile us
Each day by your woods and your streams l
At the heart of each night sit beside ns,
Your lips on the lips of our dreams !
Fair heights that are goodly and pleasant !
Soft scents of wild thyme and the sea !
With Love in the exquisite present ,
Who recks of the Things that May Be !
We still have some splendid illusions ,
Some prospects divine and anblurred ;
We laugh at the stupid confusions
And trumpery hopes of the herd.
Well armed ’mid earth's pestilent rancour
We wedded Olympians are,
With the wife’s morning kiss for sheet-anchor,
And the eyes of the baby for star.
And our friends, “without capitulation
Are loyal and staunch, and we know
That at need, without great trepidation,
We can still spin a sormet or so.
Once again, Twenty-fifth of December !—
And we laugh with delight at the date....
We ’ve some very good things to remember,
As we wipe our regrets off the slate !
TAS.
F.M.
M RMORANDA
Prof. Allbutt states the results of two thorough
Amer. experimenters on the energy-value of foods.
It appears that an excess of food does not escape
mechanically and cheaply from the body, but is
elaborately absorbed, distributed, and excreted :
to this circular process no little energy is uselessly
diverted. Energy is chiefly wasted by the nitro-
genous foods, which are relatively difficult to get
rid of ; excessive starches and sugars are burned
off in the lungs pretty directly, and at far less
cost. By an adjustment of food to work required,
the sum of work mav be greatly increased. And,
once more, “ the ordinary man eats too much.”
C. A. Pearson’s purchase of Bond. Standard,
the old-fashioned Conservative organ that is to be
converted into an exoonent of Chamberlain’s
Brummagem policy, did not issue without heart-
burning. The old-fashioned Conservative editor
disappeared in a wave of indignation ; and
Winston Churchill, taking up the cause of the
journalist, penned some warm sentences in the
controversy that followed. Counting ten papers
controlled by Pearson, he adds: “Surely this
formidable list of allied publications scattered all
over the country, assuming local character, pro-
fessing unbounded independence, but all workec
like marionettes by the chairman of the Tariff
Reform League, will excite some disquieting re-
flections. Such combinations, so far as they are
effective, must inevitably degrade journalism from
the status of an honoured profession like medicine
or law to a sordid and irresponsible traffic in words
and phrases.”
The advertised “additions” to Swinburne’s
Heptalogia, now reprinted in his collected Poems
prove to be unimportant. There is a new couplet
for Tennyson ; three new stanzas for Browning
and for Lytton is added a “specimen from the
speaker’s original poems”—a mixture of theft anc
nonsense. The complete essence of Tennyson now
reads thus (with the new distich in italic):
The Higher Pantheism in a Nutshell.
One, who is not, we see ; but one, whom we see not, is :
Surely this is not that: but that is assuredly this.
What, and wherefore, and whence ? for under is over anc
under:
If thunder could be without lightning:, lightning- could be
without thunder.
Doubt is faith in the main : but faith, on the whole, is
doubt:
We cannot believe by proof : but could we believe with-
out ?
Why, and whither, and how ? for barley and rye are not
clover :
Neither are straight lines curves : yet over is under and
over.
Two and two may be four: but four and four are not
eight:
Pate and God may be twain: but God is the same thing
as fate.
Ask a man what he thinks, and get from a man what he
feels:
God, once caught in the fact, shows you a fair pair of
heels.
More is the whole than a part: but half is more than the
' whole:
Clearly, the soul is the body: but is not the body the soul 1
One and two are not one : but one and nothing is two :
Truth can hardly be false, if falsehood cannot be true.
Once the mastodon was : pterodactyls were common as
cocks:
Then the mammoth was God : now is he a prize ox.
Parallels all thing are: yet many of these are askew :
Ifou are certainly I: but certainly-1 am not you.
Springs the rock from the plain, shoots the stream from
the rock:
Cocks exist for the hen : but hens exist for the cock.
Tv°jj’i W^oni we se . e not >’ s : and God, who is not, we see :
riddle, we know is diddle : and diddle, we take it, is dee.
THE LIGHT GREKS
IN a limited and self-centred society like
a University’s, it is natural that aca-
demic exploits should be extolled and
cherished somewhat beyond their merits.
Yet one need not complain of the friendly devo-
tion that collects, with an account of his brief life,
the Cambridge verses of A. C. Hilton. Chas. Whib-
ifU h- good. judge, calls Hilton s The Light Green
perhaps the most brilliant periodical which the
University of Cambridge has produced.” Apart
from the parodies, there is little for general readers
to profit by; but these are so happily touched that
after thirty years they are delightful. It was
Hilton who wrote for “Bret Harte” “The
Heathen Pass-ee,” with the felicitous verse:
In the crown of hits cap
Were the Furies and Fates,
And a delicate map
Of the Dorian States,
And ive found in his palms, which were holloiv,'
What are frequent in palms,—that is dates.
To Hilton, also, we are indebted for what seems
on the whole the brightest parody of Swinburne’s
‘Dolores.” Seaman’s clever “ Song of Renunci-
ation” is heavily weighted with satire :
Panatheist, bruiser and breaker
Of kings and the creatures of kings,
I shouted on Freedom to shake her
Feet loose of the fetter that clings ;
For rolling my ravenous red eye,
And lifting a mutinous lid,
To all monarchs and matrons I said I
Would shock them—and did.
Hilton’s “Octopus” ripples more spontane-
ously :
Strange beauty, eight-limbed, and eight-handed,
Whence earnest to dazzle our eyes'!
With thy bosom bespangled and banded
With the hues of the seas and the skies;
Is thy home European or Asian,
0 mystical monster marine ?
Part molluscous and partly crustacean,
Betwixt and between...
0 breast that’t were rapture to writhe on 1
O arms’t. were delicious to feel
Clinging close with the crush of the Python
When she maketh her murderous meal !
In thy eight-fold embraces enfolden,
Let our empty existence escape,
Give us death that is glorious and golden
Crushed all out of shape !
Ah ! thy red lips, lascivious and luscious,
With death in their amorous kiss,
Cling round us, and clasp us, and crush us
With bitings of agonised bliss ;
We are sick with the poison of pleasure,
Dispense us the potion of pain ;
Ope thy mouth to its uttermost measure
And bite us again !
[Oar own J. B. Stephens threw his witty address
to the Southern Cross into Swinburne’s metre,
without troubling to preserve the details of
parody :
Next to Hilton’s one would put the “Colores”
of E. C. Lefroy, an Oxford man :
0 love that is redder than roses !
O hate that is whiter than snow !
That blinkedlj / blindedly blazes
When black-blooded blast-blisses blow ;
Desert us, disdaining 0 never !
Still fashion our fatuous fate ;
0 lick us and kick us for ever,
Red love and white hate !...
By the fin-smashing fists that have smitten
The bruises that blacken and bud,
By the tawny-tailed cur that has bitten
When the thong has come down with a thud. ..
Ye bards that make Bedlam your model,
Remove your absurdities hence !
Come down and redeem us from twaddle,
Benign Common-sense /]
Hilton’s “ The Vulture and the Husbandman ”
is a delectable parody of Lewis Carroll ” :
“ The time has come," the Vulture said,
“ To talk of many things,
Of Accidence and Adjectives,
And names of Jewish kings,
How many notes a sackbut has,
And whether shawms have strings."
“ Please, Sir,” the Undergraduates said,
Turning a little blue,
“ We did not know that was the sort
Of thing we had to do.”
“ We thank you much,” the Vulture said,
“ Send, up another two ”...
Hilton’s serious verse is lightly touched : it
expresses little more than evanescent sentiment,
and brings no warrant of individuality. He died
at twenty-six, leaving his gay little bevy of rhymes
to make the world brighter. “ All human joys
are transient”—as “ George Eliot,” to the disgust
of undergraduates, remarked on being asked what
she thought of tne turmoil of University boat-
races.
Hubert Church’s new Poems ask us again to
sympathise with the pioneers. He would adapt
his verse to his new nation, so he uses familiar
words like guilt/ and kauri, and they hamper his
verse: they are not yet woven into literary lan-
guage, are too near yet too remote. Even creek,
in the Australasian sense, is a “difficult” word.
But gully :
If ever horn of hunter sivayed an echo
On thy dark gully p 001 5...
gully is almost “impossible.” And how different
it is, in poetry, to fell an oak and fell a kauri !
By dint of usage our minds will incorporate the
impression ; but in the meantime valiant verse-
writers lose the fine flavour of fitness —precisely
because their language is literally befitting.
Not quite what the shape of a cross is —
A little lop-sided , l own—
(Confound your infernal proboscis,
Inserted well-nigh to the bone !)
Queen-lights of the heights of high heaven,
Ensconced in the crystal inane—
(Oh me, here are seventy times seven
Moapdtoes again!)
THE BRIGHT BEACH
FROM The Native Tribes of South-East Aus-
tralia, by A. W. Howitt (Lond., Macmillan;
21s. net), one sees that the great Australian
Aboriginal Game is losing its interest. For
years and years ethnologists have been playing
hide-and-seek among the customs of the most com-
plicated race on earth, and chasing fascinating
hypotheses through the gaps in the evidence.
Now the fun is growing cold. The Australian
aboriginal has been explained over and over again;
and though we have still to learn about many
tribes of him, there is little doubt that these will
exhibit merely another pattern of the same stuff.
The blackfellow is literally an open book. Howitt,
dealing with S.E. Australia, joins hands with Roth
on the N.E. and Spencer and Gillen in the centre,
and only the West remains to be brought up to
modern date. So passes the mystery of the abo-
riginal. -
Howitt, whose collection of observations is last
to be published, was one of the first to observe.
This weighty volume is the work of a lifetime,
and earns “ Well done !” for a good and faithful
servant of science. Here is the gathering of forty
years in the Australian wilderness, from time to
time recorded partially, now written down fully
and methodically as a lasting contribution to
humanity’s knowledge of* humanity. The author
and Australia are heartily to be congratulated.
**
Howitt says that most of his material was col-
lected before 1889; and owing to the decay of the
aboriginal race many of his sources of information
have now disappeared. He stresses Dr. Lorimer
Eison’s connection with his earlier inquiries, and
says “much of what I have done is equally” Dr.
Fison’s.
Howitt’s main field corresponds roughly with the
territory of Victoria, N.S. Wall's, and 8. Queens-
land. This territory, at the arrival of the whites,
was parcelled out among scores of tribes, often
differing widely in language and customs. With
patient labour Howitt has plotted out all the in-
formation he'could obtain respecting every tribe,
and ranged it under such headings as tribal and
social organisation, systems of marriage arid magic,
and ceremonies and usages. He has been helped
greatly by correspondents in obtaining details in-
accessible to himself personally, and in the result
we have another complete picture of aboriginal
life —here and there vague in outline, but full of
colour, and doubtless in the main a truthful recon-
struction of the fact.
Necessarily the work is rather for the student
than the general reader, but the range of Howitt’s
observations and the plan of his book give it
especial interest. Compared with those of other
investigators, Howitt’s pages have more variety.
Spencer and Gillen are fuller ; Roth is - more sys-
matie ; but Howitt gives the easiest access to the
subject : his book is less a document than a series
of stories. For a compact survey we still go to
Mathew’s Eaglehawl; and Crow, which contains an
interesting exposition of the theory of Malayan
mixture. Howitt will have none of the Malays in
their definite historical form. He suggests that
the Tasmanians were an offshoot from the primi-
tive Negritos of S.E. Asia, while the Australians
represent a later Melanesian offshoot. The subsi-
dence of Bass Strait segregated the Tasmanians as
the subsidence of Torres Strait segregated the
Australians.
S
LTpon the anthropological and mythological pro-
blems of the pundits it is needless here to venture.
This brief notice of an indispensable book may
close with a song from Howitt’s interesting section
dealing with music. Many performers, it appears,
compose the “words and songs” of the aboriginal
opera when they are asleep or dreaming : Umbara
was different. Umbara was a sea-fisherman, and
owned a good Sydney-built boat, which he
managed with the aid of his wife. This is the
song he composed when going down the coast in
his boat:—
Winbelow is a grafted English word, really “the
wind blows.” Freely translated, the song means :
“Between the furious wind and the dashing
waves of the long-stretched sea I was nearly
upset.”
Homeric, is it not ?
In a material shape, the “ printer’s devil ” exists
no longer. The boy who blackened Gutenberg’s
wooden types with an ink-ball, and smeared him-
self into a symbol of the Lord of Darkness, has
become a literary figment, an exhibit in the vast
collection of fossils that we call language. Yet
there is still a manifest devil in a printing-office—
a tricksy imp who delights in setting the best-laid
lines agee. Frank Morton’s script m iddens him.
Not long ago he altered a verse of amorous regret—
Her twining limbs that hold me and are sweet
into
Her twining limbs that hold me and are sweat.
The reading was not without realistic merit, but
seemed unlikely.to win approval from a conven-
tional community. In the verses printed on this
page the clever imp would have two lines thus :
Let our wives (from mean vultures defend us !)
Be spacious and fragrant and Greek.
There is only an adjective for demurral.
In George Moore’s reprinted Confessions, “edited
and annotated by G.M.,” he says the book con-
tains the germs of all his writings, all his pro-
phecies. “Those whom I have brushed aside,
where are tbev ? Stevenson, so well described as
the best-dressed young man that ever walked in
the Burlington Arcade, has slipped into nothing-
ness, despite the journalists and Mr. Sidney
Colvin’s batch of letters.”

RECEIVED
The Career of Harold Endsleigh, by W. B.
Harris (Load., Blackwood ; 2s. 6cL). The author
is apparently a beginner, but he has a natural
storytelling gift. His imagination is weaker than
his experience.
Cricket Drill and Training, by A.O.D. (Lond.,
Long; Is.). The author urgessomewhat quaintly that
not cricket makes the cricketer, but preliminary
practice of attitudes—used as a means of training
judgment, mind, and muscles. The booklet
describes and pictures the chief hits and guards.
In foul-weather England the theory may have
value : otherwise the best cricket rehearsal is per-
formance.
The League of the Leopard, by H. Bindloss
(Loud., Long; 2s. (id.). Love and adventure in
Scotland and West Africa, not badly managed.
II a, Idhisin, No. 4 (Rangoon, International
Bud lhist Society; 35.) A delayed and weak
number : the editor, one regrets, is sick, still sick.
An assistant editor with the portentous name of
J. F. McKechnie has arrived : one views him not
without trepidation. Our friend Ananda M.,
who pierces the cloud of the Mystic Doctrine with
arrays and brings the last discoveries of science
in witness to the truth of dogma, is this time in-
decisive ; and the maga. is occupied with the
testimony of Chinese astrology that ‘.‘ this country
of Tibet rests on a Srinmo (goblin) lying bn her
back. The lake of Ho-thang contains her blood,
the two hills that stand near it are her bosom . . .
It is necessary to erect a saneituafy on the lake
after filling it up with earth conveyed' to the spot
on goats, so as to close up the passage to hell which
exists underneath it.” One sympathises with
Khri-tsun, the Nepalese princess, who “became
disheartened,” and exclaimed, “ How many mil-
lions of goats will be required to carry earth for
tilling up the lake of Ho-thang, and over what a
number of years the work will extend ! ”
The novels of Greening’s “Colonial Library”
are a class by themselves. They seem to be
written by clever young Oxford men, with a lean-
ing towards epigram, the arts, and the altar of the
Anglican “ High Church.” C. Ranger-Gull, who
won something like a success of scandal with The
Hgpocrite, has the most obvious air of sparkling.
In his new book, .Pontalone (3s. 6d.), he is greatly
serious, and contrasts high idealists with low
artists in an original fashion. The scene is laid in
Cornwall, where A. G. Folliott-Stokes seems to
have shared a holiday and matured A Moorland
Princess (2s. 6 d.) The princess moves in a new
robe to the old sacrifice —witness “ The End ” :
Strong arms clasped her in a warm Lips met
lips in passionate understanding, and sealed in sileht
contact their eternal vow.
Inexhaustible ‘ ‘ arms ’’ and ‘‘ lips ” L “The End”
of Portalone, you perceive, is distinctly on another
floor :
“Are you there, heart of my heart?” she whispered.
“ Are you there, noblest gentleman, tenderest lover,
truest and most honourable knight?”
When it Was Dark (L\), by Guy Thorne; differs
again. Simply :
Christ is risen !
The story is Oxford at its youngest and quaintest.
Sensational religionists might swoon to read how
a Bad Man forged an inscription at Jerusalem to
ruin a cherished dogma. When the dogma fell,
everything fell-even from Australia “ the fore-
most prelate” wrote of “the utter overthrow
of a communal moral sense.” The farrago is
funny, and the expression of High Church disgust
with Mrs. Humphry Ward is very funny. Now
that the picture of When It Was Dark is cheaper
at Is., it ’s a poor heart that will never rejoice in
the purchase.
“ Everything’s in attitude,” says a philosophic
Yankee. “Adam, William Tell, and Isaac
Newton all had business with an apple, but how
differently they took it! —and how different were-
the results ! ”
George Gissing, as a novelist, was a kind of
literary embalmer of the living: in Veranilda he
tries vainly to resuscitate the dead. The book is-
laboured and tedious. It requires the vitality of a
Scott or a Dumas to recreate a vanished age by
dint of imagination : weak-pulsed men must thrust
their hands into' the side of-life to bring assurance
of existence. —We see Gissing left an estate of
959 Z., beside his novels.

my tie.”
“ Beer,” he said dreamily, “is a strange thing. It kills
you, but then it makes you live.”
“ What, going to church ! ” “ Yes, my doctor has pre-
scribed all the bromides.”
“ I think men' are disgusting. They ’re vulgar, dirty,

different.”
“ He saves others ; himself he cannot save.”
“The Lond. publishing trade is in a very bad way.
Another publisher has just gone under; two more are

mirror tell you that your hair is gray
when you are only thirty or fifty 1

age. Why not look as young as you
are, or even younger ?
Ayer’s Hair Vigor

always. Brings back all the deep,

hair, scraggly hair. Your hair seems
weak, not well nourished. Then give
it Ayer’s Hair Vigor, a true hair-
food. It stops falling hair, makes
the hair grow, and keeps it soft and
silky.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Violin.
O beautiful, slender, sensate thing
In the Master's hand —-
Awakel Awake!
My heart must throb with each throbbing string
And yearn till it break—
Till it break for yearning—or understand ;
Must thrill with a pain that .is ecstasy
Seeking, for ever baffled, the key
To interpret each subtle symphony.
For oh ! a Spirit within the strings
Wakes and weeps at the Master’s will.
It has tears and laughter—it sighs and sings—
It wails or whispers “Be still! Be still ! ”
And it sobs unutterable things ;
Low and tender, or sweet and shrill,
Or sad as a bird with broken wings ;
And the dulcet, dreamy melodies flow
Like moonlight falling on rippling seas,
Or the tremulous music, soft and slow,
Of the south wind in the blossoming trees,
In dying cadences, hushed and low.
What soul of woman emancipate,
A thrall for an age of penance and pain,
Would make her sorrows articulate
With a sound of tears like the drip of rain,
And a storm of sobs on the passionate •chords—
Of sobs and of sighs that can find no wards,
That can only murmur and moan and shriek ?
Speak ! Ah speak !
Or the heart must break with the will to know
The theme of thy wrongs and thy wordless woe
That rises for ever. Ah God t Speak ! Speak !
What poet a-dying before his time,
His theme unuttered, his songs unsung,
Breathed into thy void his soul sublime,
Bequeathed thee the gift of liis silver tongue ;
His tears and his laughter exquisite?
O heart !to follow and fathom it— •
The broken, beautiful, wild refrain ’
Soaring again
To heights of rapture, or making moan
In a troubled and tender monotone,
All love and joy, all passion and pain.
What revellers burst from some merry hell,
Wanton and wayward, a laughing throng,
That reel in a quivering tarantelle,
Have edged the strings with shrill mirth and
song?
Have quickened the chords to gusty glee—
As fleet as the feet of the dancers ilee,
With loose lips laughing and eyes a-shine,
And crowned with vine ;
With wild locks tossing and hands flung high,
And bosoms heaving and feet that fly ;
All mad with music and mirth and wine ?
What martial spirit, re-animated
From the dust of forgotten ages, came—
On its lip 3 a battle-cry of the dead,
And seized thee and-swept thee like a flam e ?
A storm of music that shook the strings,
From a soul a-fire with remembered things,
Of the screaming bugles, high and shrill,
Crying “ Kill! Kill ! Kill ! ”
And the roll and beat of the Deathless Drum,
The ranks that waver, t'te hosts that come,
And the faces, savage and stern and still ?
0 ! beautiful, slender, sensate thing
In the Master's hand —
Be still! Be still !
The wanton sweetness in every string,
The quiver and thrill —
I hear, but I may not understand.
M.L.
MAUD PEACOCKE.
Visions of the Rain.
Last night I lay awake and heard the rain—
In that dark hour before'the break of day,
When life burns low —when phantom fingers play
A sad, soft tune upon the window-pane.
The moon was like that sweet drowned virgin face
That floated down the Tiber’s current slow
When Nero reigned, long centuries ago,
All dim with grief, yet glorious with grace.
The wind went moaning through the trees below,
And like a lost child cried, then wildly laughed ;
The grateful lily in the garden quaffed
The wine of Heaven from her cup of snow.
Yea, far away, beyond the Mountains Blue,
On many an ample field, on many a plain,
Made glad by the rich succour of the rain,
The harvest, like the gourd of Jonah, grew.
But I was gazing on another sight;
I saw the gaslit silent streets that shone
Like Hell’s sad streets by Heaven’s tears rained
upon,
When God was merciful on Calvary’s night.
And, at lane-corners where the gaslight gleamed,
I saw wan faces flushed with haggard mirth—
Alas ! poor devils they had found on Earth
A Hell more terrible than monks have dreamed.
And then the scene was changed, and glasses rang
At midnight in a little tavern room
Where no man dared to think about the tomb,
While Bunster spun his yarns and Morgan sang.
Then we went forth a merry caravan—
And down a dismal deluge came n:n .in,
And, lurking in an archway from the rain,
We knew what caves meant to the Primal Man.
Bunster and Morgan and the rest are gone.
Upon their graves, with this good rain, I ween
Tho grass is springing fresh and lush and green.
I pulled the blind down, and the rain rain d on.
N.S.W,

THURSDAY, JANUARY 19, 1905.
TWO STRIKES.
The New Year opened for New South Wales
laden with industrial misfortunes. To the damage
caused by hot winds and bush fires was added a
paralysis of the two most important primary in-
dustries—those dealing with wool and coal —by
rash and foolish strikes. The “ wheelers ” in the
Newcastle district —lads and young men employed
in wheeling the trucks in the coal pits—dissatisfied
with a reduction of \fages, left their work, in spite
of the fact that the Industrial Arbitration Court
was hastening to judge their cause, with the power
to restore the old wage rate and the inclination to so
act if justice demanded. This folly on the part of
the wheelers had the effect of throwing idle the
majority of the coal pits in the Newcastle district,
though the wheelers themselves form but a small
minority of the workers concerned. The complex
processes of modern industrialism are so inter-
related that the failure of one small department
involves sometimes the stoppage of a great enter-
prise. In the Newcastle district, before this, a pit
has been closed down and great suffering caused
by the pit-boys—lads employed to sharpen the
picks and do other such small jobs—going on
strike ; and there is a legend of a manager, enraged
by the idea of such a boys’ strike stopping his col-
liery, driving the youngsters to their work with a
birch, they just fresh from school submitting
from a sense of custom to this summary method of
breaking a strike. The wheelers, who have caused
the present trouble, are more responsible than the
pit-boys ; a proportion of them are adults ; all are,
so to speak, apprentices to the mining trade. But
they have nothing like the feeling of responsi-
bility which the miner and the trades-unionist
has, and the rashness of youth has led them to a
strike which may have far-reaching political, as
well as industrial, consequences. The second
strike, which paralysed the wool trade, was that
of the members of the Woolbuyers’ Association—a
very closely-organised trades-union which is so
strong that it is never troubled by “black-
leg ” competition, and which uses its giant
strength so tyrannously that it declares a
strike on the smallest provocation. Because
of the new Commonwealth Shipping Act the
shipping co.’s have alleged a necessity to
raise freight rates by the imposition of a special
“primage” charge on all shipments. The
necessity is more imaginary than real. For years
past the shipping co.’s have, by little and little,
been victimising consignors by contracting them-
selves out of their liabilities as carriers. Things
have come to such a pass that one case is cited
when a deck cargo of fruit was broached on voyage,
looted by officers, crew, and passengers, and the
shipping co. disowned ail responsibility, advancing
its bill of lading as a defence. The new Common-
wealth legislation had no other purpose or effect
than to bring the shipping firms back to the control
of the common law as carriers. Seemingly out of
mere spitefulness, the co.’s responded with the new
primage duty, just as that big monopoly, the Aus-
tralian Gas Co., when it was made liable to muni-
cipal rating, replied with a big increase in the price
for lighting the municipal streets. Against pay-
ment of this primage on wool cargoes the wool-
buyers went on strike. They decided to consign
no more wool and to buy no more wool until the
imposition was abandoned. As a result, the wool-
export industry was stopped. Vessels, stevedores,
carriers, wharf-laborers were kept idle, and an
economic waste involved, not so great certainly as
in the case of the coal trouble, but still consider-
able. With those two strikes the New Year
opened.
* • * *
It is effective proof of the oneness of human
nature, the “fidelity to type” of man, whatever
his occupation or education, that two such widely
separated classes as the wheelers of Newcastle and
the woolbuyers of Sydney should have, under the
same stimulus, acted in precisely the same way.
Each had a grievance. In both cases there was a
means of redress by reason and argument. But
both elected to use the same means of violence and
disorder. The pit lads, but little educated, rough
diamonds when diamonds at all, and the travelled,
urbane wool-buyers showed themselves influenced
by the same passions, the same impatience, the
same crude love of a row, the same selfish disre-
gard for the rights of others. It mattered as little
to the wheeler in Newcastle that his grudge
against the coal-owner was being mostly worked
off against the miner, the sailor, the coal-con-
sumer and their families, as to the wool-buyer
that his strike against the ship-owner was keeping
thousands of innocent workmen idle, and was
tending to close down mills and generally disturb
a world-wide industry. The average wheeler
lives in a hut or cottage, has had no school but the
primary State one, and that availed of for the
shortest possible time. The average wool-buyer
lives at a club, a fashionable hotel, or an elegant
suburban villa, has had the advantages of
liberal education, and knows the cultivat-
ing benefits of travel. But beneath the skin —
perhaps, indeed, no farther than beneath the
clothes —wheeler and wool-buyer show, under
exasperation, exactly the same lack of the civilised
feeling; they are alike stupid, unreasonable, and
careless of the community rights when they
imagine their interests to be touched. If there is
any choice between the two sets of strikers, the
wheelers must have the advantage of it, for their
grievance was greater. A material reduction of
wages is a galling thing when wages are already so
low as to be below the subsistence point if the
wheeler, as is often the case, has listened to the
exordium of the Birth-rate Commission and mar-
ried young. The wool-buyer had to face no re-
duction of his fat income; at the worst the action
of the shipping companies only involved him in the
necessity of re-casting his prices and re-adjusting
his calculations. Admitted that the wool-buyer had
a vexation to complain of, still it was not so great as
that thrust home to the wheeler. And the wool-
man’s superior education, his more mature age,
his greater experience should have restrained him
from a violent and extreme step. But “woolie”
and “ coalie ” both acted alike, careless absolutely
of the effect of their action on others.
• • • •
This sameness of action was not matched, how-
ever, by a sameness of consequence. As soon as
the wheeler went on strike, the daily press, the
“ respectable classes,” and all the other people to
whom the community is taught to look for light
and leading, savagely denounced him for his
folly, his wickedness, his reckless disregard of
consequences. The denunciation was, of course,
justified, though not the measure of it. There is no
excuse for the wheelers rushing into a strike when
a court of arbitration was open to them. But for
the wool-buyer, the wealthy, influential wool-
buyer who ought to know better, there has been
no word of anger, only a gentle murmur of expos-
tulation. He illustrated “the tyranny of trades-
unionism ” in its most extreme form by absolutely
shutting up a business because of some slight
grievance, and, instead of meeting angry denun-
ciation, he has been coaxingly urged like a good
fellow to think it over again, and show himself
the awfully jolly chap that he is by giving way
and going back to work, and letting the wheels of
industry go round again. The Government en-
trusted with the administration of the law
has, too, shown its capacity for making
differences. Action has been taken to fine
or imprison the contumacious strikers of the
coal pit, but there is not a whisper of a warrant
against any man of the wool pit. Yet one is
almost as much within the shadow of the law as
the other. To match the Arbitration Law, under
which the coal-man may be punished, there is
an old Act before this invoked in Aus-
tralia against trade-unionists of a humbler
type—which might be directed against the wool-
men for combining to act in restraint of trade.
But it seems to be quite freely accepted as a prin-
ciple that a gentle exhortation is the proper thing
for the rich, well-dressed offender, and gaol for
the miscreant in blucher boots.
THE LACK OF AN EFFECTIVE SAVAGE.
In spite of the moderate degree of interest to
which the selection of a cricket Eleven arouses
him, the Australian collectively must be convicted
of a don’t-care attitude on most vital points. Indi-
vidually the Australian does not “ lean up against
a post ” —despite all that may be said to the con-
trary. He is, to a very marked extent, a “ hustler.”
As a mechanic or a laborer his output is far greater
than that of his -British confrere; even in
fast-living America he holds his own, whilst
in S’Africa, where popular prejudice links
instinctively “Australian” and “criminal,” those
of our citizens who have won to employment in
the face of prejudice are recognised as the best of
workers surpassing Afrikander, Britisher, or
Yankee. Nor does the Australian, individually,
withhold full meed of admiration from energy in
any form ; indeed, he acclaims it furiously—in
politics, in sport, in every department of effort.
John Norton, of the N.S.W. Parliament, for
example, is something of a deity to a large class,
and nothing that John Norton can do will
outweigh with them the fact that he is a
meaty man, full of energy and fire. For the
same reason, Conroy, of the Federal Parlia-
ment, is looked upon with fondness by many a
back-country man and city Radical. That
Conroy votes on the Tory side, as a rule, counts
for little ; he “ hustles,” makes a row, is vigorous,
and workers in the far north of Queensland and
the far sand of Westralia, who know few other
Federal members by name, acclaim him for that
reason. The curious phenomenon is thus pre-
sented of the Australian, individually,being capable
of great energy, admiring great energy, and yet
collectively suffering from that tired feeling. The
same people which produces champions in almost
every branch of sport; which can boast as
to the rank and file of its workers that they
will hold their own against the world ; which is
ever sending away pioneers to the remote places
of the earth; which has always a welcome for the
energetic man in politics—is content, nevertheless,
to allow its institutions of government to drift
into the hands of weakling time-servers and poor
mediocrities; its legislation to be mostly of the
neuter gender; its industrial life to languish
under a blight of indolent carelessness. The
Australian man is a virile type, capable of becom-
ing a champion in sport, a pioneer in explora-
tion, a captain in industry. The Australian
nation has been, so far, shiftless, lazily tolerant,
incapable of organising to utilise, as a means to
great power, the wonderful chances put into its
hands by fortune.
* * * *
Perhaps the explanation is that the Australian
type—of great potential energy, sometimes need-
ing strong stimulus to- call out his good quali-
ties, but always capable of effort—is yet cursed
with a grave doubt of appearances, and is
fearful generally that enthusiasm should show as
ridiculous. He lets his doubts take refuge in a
cynical attitude of “don’t-care.” The man who
“doesn’t care a darn” is familiar to the city
and the back country. As a matter of truth he
does care, and cares very much, for a good deal
of what he professes to disregard. But his
shyness suggests that not to care is a
safe stand to take, and he therefore hides his
emotions and his hopes. That is the spirit which,
to a very large extent, governs Australian public
life. It is in a way the antithesis of the spirit of
blague, the pose of overweening national conceit,
which is found so offensive in other peoples ; and
yet it can and does exist side by side with a
very marked degree of vanity. The Australian
citizen is not by any means an over-modest man :
he has a quite sufficiently good opinion of himself.
But in his community life he suffers, without effort
at remedy, under the ban of being a helpless, inferior
sort of creature. His assent is tacitly given to
various propositions most hurtful to national
pride—that he cannot manufacture things like
the European or Japanese ; that he cannot defend
himself but must hire defence from outside ; that
he cannot pay his way like the ordinary man but
must exist on debt; that he cannot expect in his
public life any high standard of ability or honesty,
and that “ statesmen ” of the type of G. H. Reid,
Thomas Bent, Jenkins and Joseph Carruthers
are good enough for him.
* * * *
From these various, seemingly contradictory
facts—that the Australian individually is a tough,
skillful, resourceful worker, but collectively is
content to be classed as a mere rouseabout for
civilisation ; that the Australian as a man is keen-
witted, well-educated, ambitious, and a good deal
conceited, but as a unit of a nation is content to be
bamboozled by ignorant and unworthy men into
acquiescence with a humiliating state of debt
and defencelessness —it is difficult to trace any
general conclusion. But what seems to be
true, is that the Australian is good enough
stuff to make a nation of, but that so far
he lacks the community feeling. He has not
been taught how to effectively extend his personal
pride into a national pride, his individual desire
for supremacy, and his individual capacity to
hustle, into a collective dignity and vigorousness.
What would have been of priceless value to Aus-
tralia is an effective savage.
* * * *
If, on the foundation of these States, the
aborigines holding the country had been bold and
warlike, sufficiently powerful to make the acquisi-
tion of territory a matter of some fighting, a
different bent would have been at the outset given
to the Australian character. The necessity for
community action would have been from the first
impressed, a tradition not of individual but of
collective prowess established. Settlement, of
course, would have spread more slowly, and that
would have been another advantage. Instead of
a thin sprinkling of population over an enormous
area, as at present, thickly clustered groups would
have gathered around the more fertile coast lands,
gradually extending as the pressure of population
became stronger than the fear of the savages’
weapons. How much the United States of Ame-
rica owe to their Red Indians it would be difficult
to over-estimate. The Red Indian not only saw
to it that settlement proceeded on natural lines by
groups, but he, from the first, hammered in the
lesson of solidarity, and gave to the American
people that national self-respect which comes of
battles fought and dangers surmounted together.
Communities are best cemented with blood.
* * * *
But, unhappily, aboriginal Australia lacked
altogether an effective savage. The thin tribes
of blacks melted away before the white man, leav-
ing no records except those of mean murders on
either side. Tasmania boasts a “ Black War,” but
the chief weapon of the white in that disgraceful
campaign was poisoned flour. Not in any district
or any State was the aboriginal population power-
ful enough to make a fair stand against the white
man’s settlement, or to drive home the lesson that
a people can live only by mutual help and reliance.
England threatened once or twice to make up
for the lack of an efficient savage, but the
threat, or promise, was never fulfilled. At
Eureka, at Circular Quay, when the convicts
were sent back, again when the Chinese
were barred out, there seemed a chance of a
new nation coming to life with bloodshed. But
always at the pinch, England was too wise for its
own interests, too humane, and gave way.
Deplorable as it would have been for Australia to
have won, as the Americans did, a baptism of
blood at the swords of kindred, there is little
doubt but that even a fratricidal war would have
had an ultimate national advantage. It often
seems that things have been too easy for the
Australians, that before they can learn to think
as a nation, and to put a proper value on their
responsibilities, they must be called upon to fight
together—not in some filibustering raid on another
country, undertaken in mere delight of battle, but
at the call of national necessity, in stern, almost
despairing defence of their own territory.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
What the White Can Do.
The Kanaka crowd is flooding Sydney and Mel-
bourne again with a steady supply of lie-literature
in the interests of the monopolists who have
flourished on Cheap Black Labor. “The white
man can’t stand the tropic heat; he can’t exist in
a sugar field ” —this the burden of it all. The
writers picture a cane-brake and ask, “Can a
white stand that?” They don’t picture other
things and say, “The white stands that.” Yet,
consider what he does stand. In N.Q. the writer
has personally seen white men screen auriferous
tailings with the thermometer at 110 degrees. He
has also seen lengthsmen relay a running road in
the humid coastal district at 102 degrees. Both
these sets of men worked amid dust and other
nuisances, week in week out, for months on end;
and they do it even when the heat runs up to
llOdeg. At Charters Towers, thousands of white
men toil in deep workings, where the temperature
of the smoke and fracteur-filled air reaches 35deg.
higher than the surface temperature. Lately,
these same white men didn’t have to be called
on to go underground amongst the poisonous
fumes from the Brilliant fire; it was a greater
job to restrain them from going; and they
grumbled when they were ordered up. Writer,
m a mine further north, worked in a cross-cut
where the temperature was 113°. Thousands of
white men stand these conditions,—fracteur fumes
machine dust, and risk of accident thrown in— and
never stop to think that they are doing anything
special. Then, leaving the goldfields, consider
the old-style reverberatory copper furnaces, where
the heat of the molten slag and metal was so
great that men had to wrap old felt hats or such
like round their shins to protect them from scorch
White men stood that. In the “ gullet ”of a 60-
feet cutting in mid-summer, when 110° is the
signal for knock off-white men stand that. For
from £3 to £4 10s. a week the N.Q. miner will go
on standing such things, the same as the Flinders
shearer stands as much or more, handling hot,
stinking sheep, in a foetid atmosphere in a blister-
ing galvanised shed, for £1 a hundred. Or, along-
coast, who has yet heard of any white steamboat
line, that wasn’t black and mean, being laid up
for want of white stokers and trimmers, bar
strikes ? Eight pounds to £9 a month is a good
reason why. And that suggests the reason why
the white man “ can’t ” do lighter labor up north.
One shocked sugar farmer stated that he offered
such generous pay that it was possible to earn
from 7s. to Bs. a day, yet only two men offered in
Brisbane. Fare up, £4 ; fare back, ditto. Dura-
tion of season variable in different areas; but if a
man got three months’ work he would do well.
Cost of living, say, 30 per cent, dearer than the
city. Because white men wouldn’t take it on at
the price, the southern papers are told that the
white man “can’t.” And when they go, it is
further stated that as soon as they earn a few shil-
lings they clear out. Well ? The miner or stoker or
smelter sees money before him and stays. But if 255.
was all these same men could handle after a week’s
work, they would desert those indutries ; at least
the best of them would. That the white man
can’t do the work is a lie. It is proved a lie by
the fact that he is every day doing worse work.
The whole truth is that he wants to be paid for it;
and the mean white of the north, because he
won’t pay white’s wages, says that the white
won’t work.
No Money for Sydney.
Sydney Telegraph seems hurt to find that the
newest N.S.W. loan of two millions “will bring
hardly any money to Sydney.” The Telegraph
ought to tell now when a loan did bring money to
Sydney. From the middle of 1900 to the middle
of 1903, N.S.W. borrowed for purposes other than
redemption £12,435,147. Except a small amount
scraped up locally, the whole of this was raised in
England. Yet, so far as money is concerned, this
is what we got from England -
Gold. Silver. Total.

1901 .. .. Nil 90,000 90,000
1902 .. .. £lOO,OOO 40,000 140,000
Three years’imports.. £lOO,OOO £270,000 £370,000
Meantime this is what N.S.W. exported to Eng-
land in the shape of hard coin :
Gold. Silver. Total.
1900 .. ~ £488,806 nil £488,806
1901 .. .. 524,293 nil 524,293
1902 .. .. 680,585 nil 680,585
Three years’ exports £1,693,654 nil £1,693,684

actually received from Cohen, in money, only
Australian Engineers
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Three boys, from 15 to 18, recently applied to a well-
known public official for “ a start in something." Each
said he wanted to be an engineer. Several schoolmasters
have recently asked boys of the higher classes what they
would like to be. Carpentering and engineering seemed
the favorite callings.— News par.
Ah, well! but the case seems hopeless, and the
pen might write in vain :
The people gabble of old things over and over
again.
For the sake of the sleek importer we slave with
the pick and the shears,
While hundreds of boys in Australia long to be
engineers.
A new generation has risen under Australian
skies,
Boys with the light of genius deep in their dreamy
eyes—
Not as of artists or poets with their vain
imaginings,
But born to be thinkers and doers, and makers of
wonderful things.
Born to be builders of vessels in the Harbors of
Waste and Loss,
That shall carry our goods to the nations, flying
the Southern Cross ;
And fleets that shall guard our seaboard—while the
East is backed by the Jews—
Under Australian captains, and manned by Aus-
tralian crews.
They long for the crank and the belting, the gear
and the whirring wheel,
The stamp of the giant hammer, the glint of the
polished steel.
For the mould and the vyce and the lathe—they
are boys who long for the keys
To the doors of the world’s Mechanics and
Science’s mysteries.
They would be makers of fabrics, of cloth for the
continents—
Makers of mighty engines and delicate instru-
ments ;
It is they who would set fair cities on the western
plains far out,
They who would garden the deserts—it is they
who would conquer the drought !
They see the dykes to the skyline, where a dust-
waste blazes to-day,
And they hear the lap of the waters on the miles
of sand and clay ;
They see the rainfall increasing, and the boundless
sweeps of grass,
And all the year on the rivers the strings of barges
pass.
But still the steamers sail out with our timber and
wool and gold,
And back with the costly shoddy stacked high in
the foreign hold ;
With the cardboard boots for our leather, and the
Brummagem goods and the slops
For stunted and white-faced Australians to sell in
our sordid shops.

State not only handed Cohen its I O U for 12 a

its I O U and its money was manufactured goods.
Broadly speaking, N. S. Wales went 121 millions

and then, having made them weak dependents,
and barely able to keep themselves going, much less
to pay up back bills, a grateful parent bequeathed
to them the I O U he gave to Cohen. This is like
a man, who has had an arm and a leg bitten off bv
a bear, getting the bear for a present, with strict
instructions to feed it well for the rest of its life.

“ To Let.”
There is an anguished cry in Sydney prints
that the suburbs thereabout are studded with
empty houses ; and in the interests of Property it
is demanded that “ the burden of taxation ” shall
be lightened. The Telegraph seeks to prod the
tenant to pull Property’s nuts out of the fire by
telling him that it is he who has to pay the tax on
the empty house as well as upon the house he lives
in. Incidentally, the allegation is valuable, be-
cause, if the tenant is the real ratepayer, in respect
of both the occupied and the unoccupied house, by
what right is the owner a voter, except in
respect of his own residence ? But when
the Telegraph asks us to believe that it is
filled with compassion for the tenant, because
he has to pay rent for the unoccupied as
well as the occupied house, it asks us to swallow
too much. For one thing the theory runs too
violently against the Telegraph’s other pet theory
with regard to supply and demand. An over-
supplied market means cheapness ; the Telegraph
asks us to believe that, where houses are con-
cerned, it means dearness. The workman knows
that when there are three of him struggling for
one job, he has to take low wages ; now it seems
that when there are three landlords struggling for
one tenant, the law works the other way,
and the tenant has to pay high rents. It’s
that sort of argument which gives people the
fleeting suspicion, sometimes, that the Telegraph
isn’t quite as frank and consistent as it might
lie. Meantime, however, there is no doubt that
empty houses are bad for somebody. They are a
waste and a drag ; they are mostly built with bor-
rowed money, and the country is the poorer for
having to pay interest which isn’t earned. That
it is very largely foreign debt which these pre-
mises represent is pretty well proved by the fact
that a boom in building always occurs during a
borrowing boom. At such times we borrow money
to build premises here, and we provide the work
for people who live in foreign parts. Then,
when we have the premises and nobody to
occupy them, all that the Telegraph can
suggest is that taxes shall be reduced.
At the most that will save Proputty a few shillings
a year, and what it saves in that way it loses
through the local goat being allowed to wander
unmolested, and the local jam tin to heap up in
front of the “ To Let ” board. The tax-reduction
will do no more than that; it will not add a single
tenant; it will not bring in a penn’orth of rent.
The idea of getting people to fill the premises—of
employing workmen here instead of abroad—of
making the place attractive to the world—of these
things the Telegraph doesn’t think. It only
thinks of encouraging the accumulation of the
foreign sardine tin in public places, so that the
man may trip over it as he goes in search of work
at the factories that don’t exist.
The Labor Leagues and Protection.
There is a chance that the platform of the Federal
Labor party may be amended before long in such
fashion as to preclude any Freetrader from re-
ceiving a Labor nomination. N.S.W. Political
Labor League will hold its annual conference in
Sydney very shortly ; and a number of delegates
will carry commands from their leagues insisting
on the introduction of a prohibitive Federal tariff.
N.S.W. Political Labor League is responsible to
a very large extent for the character of the Federal
Labor platform ; and, once that body plumps for
Protection, the Labor party will practically be won
for the cause of Australian nationalism. The Vic-
torian Trades Hall inquiied into the working of
the tariff on its own account a while back. As a
consequence, the Trades Hall now knows what
most perceptive Australians recognised a couple
of years ago. Stephen Barker, as secretary of
the United Trades Tariff Conference, submitted
a report
(1) That serious ravages have been made upon Austra-
lian industries by the present Federal tariff.
(2) That some of the trades affected are absolutely
standing still; and, where prior to the introduction of
the present tariff hundreds of skilled artisans were
engaged, the machinery is silent, the capital used in them
heavily discounted, and the managing staff and every
kind of worker under them completely idle.
(3) The spectacle of thousands of pounds worth of
machinery laid aside, with hundreds of technically
trained artisans out of work, and, crowning summit of
the iniquity, the foreign sweated-labor products dumped
into our markets, duty free, demonstrated the pernicious
effects of the tariff upon the workers of the Common-
wealth.
As a remedy the Trades Hall conference
submitted: —■
As we ask in the interests of a White Australia that
the colored races shall not be permitted to engage in
unequal competition with our own people, so their wares
should also be excluded from our warehouses, factories,
shops, and homes. If we sincerely desire a White
Australia, both alien races and their products, BY
MEANS OF A PROHIBITIVE TARIFF, must be dis-
carded, as a means to the encouragement of Australian
manufactures and productions. By such means Aus-
tralian enterprise and inventive genius would he stimu-
lated, with the natural sequence of educational impetus,
mechanical progress, and a number of other improve-
ments within the scope of Federated Australian national
development and prosperity.
It is a long while since any Australian Labor
League or Council brought in such a common-
sense report and recommendation as that. Let the
Labor leagues in the other States make similar
inquiries impartially, and they must come to
similar conclusions.

The Scandal of the Nor’-West.
“G.R.F.”: “ Vigilans’” pen-picture of Beagle
Bay, Nor’-west Australia (8., 5/l/’O5), is true to
life, and should galvanise Australians into taking
drastic action to protect the miserable offspring of
.the natives from further contamination by the
crews of the pearling luggers, which consist of the
rakings of Asia. The Bevans, Careys, and other
rev. and enthusiastic admirers of the yellow
and black men, should see the festering Asiatic
sore that is spreading along the west and
northern coast line of the continent to realise the
horrible possibilities of ill that follow on the
influx of the mongrel Asiatic races. These
: reverend gentlemen would be especially inter-
ested to note how their darling Asiatics
spend their Sundays when ashore, in the hurricane
season, which lasts from November to the end of
March. During the week-days they are kept at
work from daylight till dark, but from 3 o’clock on
Saturday afternoon till Monday they are allowed
to occupy their time as they please. The men on
credit with the “slop-chest’’’buy extensively of
rice, at 10s. a bag of 561 b., flour at 155., gaudy
blankets at £1 each, tobacco, and other things dear
to the blacks, and hurry away to the blacks’ camp-
ing places in the scrub. There they barter away
their purchases to the old men of the tribes for
the women and children, who are handed over to
the Japs, Malays and Manila men. Occasionally,
grog gets ashore, and then the fierce hatred that
slumbers in the Malay for the Manila man, and
in the Japanese for both, awakens, and it takes all
the persuasive power of the Colt revolvers in the
hands of the white men to reduce them to their
normal condition of cowed and snarling beasts.
But, so long as they don’t knife each other or
injure his property, the master pearler cares little
what else they do ; and this apathy renders the
task of saving the young children from these
Asiatic fiends the more difficult for the few
mounted police, who, to their credit, do their best
to detect and punish offences against children.
But bush telegraphy in the black Nor’-west is in
the highest state of perfection, and the movements
of white men (police particularly) are known in
blacks’camp hours before the visitors arrive, and
by that time the injured women and children are
out of the way. There is a heavy penalty for
allowing lubras or their children on board luggers,
but it is common enough all the same, and the
police are powerless to prevent it.

Sporadic Philanthropy.
Every calamity of great proportions is made the
text of an appeal to the pockets of the public.
The charity thus evoked is too often diverted into
morasses of waste and futility. There have been
many such exasperating experiences. Everybody
knows the history of the Bulli Colliery
Sufferers’ Fund, and many other funds since
that date. It is not alone the fact
that money charitably subscribed is lavishly
wasted in the machinery of its distribution;
that ridiculous extravagance is indulged in
—costly stationery, highly-paid clerical staffs and
palatial offices. There is something more annoy-
ing still. The public in its generosity frequently
subscribes much more than is needed. The money
is vested in a trust for administration for the
specific purpose, and that prevents the application
of the surplus funds to the amelioration of any
other national calamities that may subsequently
take place. Then, when the next fund comes
along, the public, perhaps, subscribes much less
than is needed. The remedy for this condition of
things would be the creation of a State officer of
Public Charity. It is not desirable, as yet, to
propose that the State should take over the
responsibility of relieving all such distress out of
taxation, and thus discouraging private generosity.
But the calamity appeals should be systematised—
all money paid in to the State trustee and disbursed
by him for the disaster which specially evokes it,- or
for any subsequent need of the same type. It is a
matter of common observation that a “ thrilling ”
accident, with but a small amount of disaster in
its train, causes a bigger flow of charity than a
more prosaic happening, causing much more poverty
and suffering. By systematising the matter of
appeals this would be no longer an evil to be
regretted. But as things are the stupidity of
sporadic philanthropy needs to be modified by
humanitarian wisdom.

By the Wool.
The lead mining and smelting business illustrates
how the modern nation builds up its industries,
and how the country that won’t learn the same
lesson has to be merely a hewer of wood and
drawer of water. One Broken Hill mine which
does its own lead-smelting in Australia employs
one man for every 130 tons of ore, while another
co., with richer ore, employs only one man
for every 235 tons produced per annum ; but, the
latter co. sells its concentrates, which are shipped
to Germany, so that Germans not only have the
actual work of smelting these concentrates,
but they also supply the coal, coke, etc.
They have deliberately set themselves oat to get
all this trade. First, they put a heavy duty on
lead bars, which shut the Australian producer
out of one of the biggest markets in the world.
Having built up no manufacturing industries of
his own, the Australian has to export to the
countries which have built up such industries;
and they are just the very countries—Germany
and the U.S. notably—which have the stiffest
duties. With no market of his own, the Aus-
tralian found half the world’s markets shut.
Germany, having thus got one leg in, proceeded
to put in another. It shut out lead bars, but it
openedits ports to concentrates and crude ores; and
by means of shipping subsidies, it made the freights
on these goods very low to the German purchaser.
The result of all these things, working together,
is that upwards of 30,000 tons of lead ore go away
unsmelted every year—Australia doesn’t even have
the job of getting the dirt away from the metal
which it exports. And in return it gets back
manufactured lead goods—white lead, paint, elec-
trical articles, ammunition, &c.,-in the production
of which the foreign workman has been employed
instead of the Australian. Now Australian zinc
is going precisely the same way. There was a
time, not so long ago, when even America ex-
ported lead ores to Germany ; but the U.S. soon
saw. the absurdity of that, and, through fostering
its own industries, it has itself become a large
purchaser of Mexican and Canadian ores, which
are treated in bond, the product being either used
at home, or sent abroad to the open markets and
sold at surplus-stock prices ; and that is the metal
with which our metal has to compete. Then we
wonder how it is that protected countries have got
us by the wool and are shaking us, and why our
products are often low priced, and our population
almost stationary !

The Year Ahead for Tasmania. -
The year 1905 dawned dully on Tasmania, and
the prospect ahead is anything but cheering. The
State with neither honesty nor grit enough to
come in out of the mud, is practically bankrupt;
and the present sorrv Government is mainly con-
cerned to postpone the public examination so long
as possible. With a population well under 200,000
—the population of a second-class English
provincial town—the State has, during many years
of misgovernment by monopoly, heaped-up such a
ghastly incubus of public debt that the interest-
bill now amounts in round figures to £lOOO a day.
This sum has to be scooped out of the little
treasure-chest every morning, before the days
actual needs can be attended to. The average
Tasmanian legislator has so many class-exemptions
rooted in his thick head that it is really very diffi-
cult to get any considerable amount of money by
direct taxation. The island, with great tracts of
alienated land lying waste, submits rebelliously to
a halfpenny land-tax, and talks darkly all the
time of the “grievous burdens ’ born by Land.
Land (with the capital L) is one of those fetishes
that badly needs strangling, and the fetish Capital
might profitably be exposed to derision on the
same gibbet. Thanks to this form of fetishism,
the owning of a sufficiently large quantity of
land in Tasmania—provided always that it is
kept closed as completely as possible from settle-
ment and usefulness —is, in ordinary, accounted a
positive virtue in any man. The State further
loves to “ encourage capital,” and it encourages it
most habitually by letting it escape all responsi-
bility of its just obligations. If land were
properly taxed, the land-grabbers would be
knocked-out speedily, settlement would be
fostered along legitimate lines, and there
would be some reasonable hope of eventually
shifting the bloated public debt. But
while the Evans Government remains in power
there is no hope, Evans being especially promi-
nent as a buttress of the House of Dodery, which
has the one set purpose in life of enabling the
plutocratic minority it represents to dodge legiti-
mate and reasonable taxation. Holding that the
working man is a very impudent fellow for daring
to be alive, the House of Dodery believes in in-
creasing the taxation of the working man at every
possible opportunity. There, according to Dodery,
you have in a nutshell the secret of State
finance. Unfortunately for the little treasure-
chest, the worker in Tasmania already bears
so disproportionately large a share of the
taxation of the country that he practically cannot
be made to bear more. The workers of the State
are very poor. Wages sre lower and some neces-
saries dearer than in any of the larger Australian
States. Also, over a good part of Tasmania, work
is more uncertain. There is no great pastoral in-
dustry ; no great agricultural industry even. The
fruit industry is substantial; but it only employs
a certain fixed number of people, mostly women
and children, during a certain fixed period of each
year. The mining industry is substantial, but it
is depressed—wages, in regard to the conditions
and cost of living, are low—and there is no opening
for fresh labor on the fields. The hope of Tas-
manian democracy in 1905 is, first of all, that
Evans and his awful crowd will be ejected
speedily; after, that, at the election ensuing, an
Assembly will be returned to deal with the House
of Dodery. Failing that, Tasmania looks like
being about the first State of the Commonwealth to
file its schedule.

The Undesirable Immigrant.
There is so much idiotic blather about the iniquity
of Australia’s Immigration Restriction Act th.it it
cannot be too often pointed out that, in compari-
son with legislation on similar lines in other
countries, the Australian Act is mild and gentle.
The United States Government exacts full in-
formation from intending immigrants upon every
one of the following questions ; and the immigrant
who supplies a false statement is returned to his
starting place without further argument:—
Age : In years and months.
Sex, and whether married or single.
Occupation, and whether able to read or write.
Nationality, and last place of residence.
Seaport for landing in the U.S. A., and also final destina-
tion. Whether immigrant lias a ticket right through to
final destination, also who paid for same?
Whether immigrant possesses any money ; and, if so,
does the amount exceed $3O, and by how much?
Whether immigrant has ever been in the United States
before ; and if so when and where ?
Whether immigrant intends to join a relative in the
U.S. ; and if so that relative’s name, occupation and
address ?
Whether the immigrant has ever been in gaol or has
ever been supported by charity ; full details to be given in
either case ?
Whether a disciple of Brigham Young or not ?
Whether under contract of labor in any shape or form,
and what prospects, if any, of employment ?
Finally, the U.S. asks every immigrant to pro-
duce a comprehensive health bill, mental as well
as physical; and requires a full account of all
debilities and deformities. A satisfactory account
has to be given on all these points before the
immigrant to America can be taken on board any
ship ; and Uncle Sam provides a rigorous set of
punishments for even the smallest deviation
from the immigration laws. And it is the
right way to do it. No decent man would
admit a total stranger into his home with-
out obtaining the fullest satisfaction as regards
his character ; and a nation has the same right to
be particular. But, the main point to be stressed
is that the stern care with which U.S.A. excludes
the unsuitable immigrant does not frighten away
but encourages the suitable one. It is not the
Australian Immigration Act which keeps immi-
grants away from our shores. It is the Tory land
monopolists and Freetraders, who destroy the
hope of work, without which the stranger will not
come.
The Chambers of Small Men.
A comparison of the membership of the Federal
Convention with that of State Parliaments drives
home one argument for wiping the latter bodies
out of existence. That argument is simply that
there are not enough capable public men in Aus-
tralia to supply State Parliaments as well as the
Australian Parliament, and that the capable men
prefer the Australian Parliament. The Federal
Convention no doubt might have been better ; yet
it was a collection worthy of Australia, and there
were not half-a-dozen large, or fairly large, men in
Australian public life who were out of it. A few
small men got in, but, for all that, it was a gather-
ing branded by Australia as the best it had. Now
look for members of the picked flock in the State
Parliaments. N.S.W. has two, which is more
than the average. Wise is one ; Carruthers the
other. Victoria doesn’t number a single one in
its House of Landlords, and only Peacock in the
other House; and Peacock is far too busy now“
making money to trouble much about politics.
S. Australia hasn’t one Conventioner in its
Assembly, and in its Council it has only J. H.
Howe, who got into the Convention unexpectedly
as ninth or tenth man, and is now mostly sick.
The Tasmanian Assembly hasn’t one Conven-
tioner, and its Council only one, who is never
heard from. Westralia, where there wasn’t a
popular election, may be left out of the reckon-
ing ; and Queensland only came in afterwards.
Thus of the 40 men upon whom Australia set its
first-class brand, only two, Joey Carruthers and
the played-out Peacock, are in the State Assem-
blies ; and sick-man Howe is the only one now in
Australia who is in any Legislative Council, Wise
being out of Australia. Yet when it is suggested
that Australia should ask those whom it has itself
put into the first class to do some more of Austra-
lia’s public work, the provincial papers shriek
“State Frights,” and the public seems to agree
with them to a great extent anyway, the public
doesn’t actively protest against the shrieking.
And the result is that the. admitted second and
third-raters, in whose interest the “State Frights ”
cry is raised, make as bad a mess of things as
might be expected.
Putting Them on the Land: A New Phase.
Even England, driven to it by Freetrade, begins
to babble about “putting the unemployed on the
land.” Witness this extract from the London
letter of an Australian daily:—
In one patch of the East End alone there are more than
9000 men out of work. Manchester has 10,000 unem-
ployed, a large proportion of them married. W ith their
families and dependents these workers make, according
to an .estimate supplied to the city council, between
40,000 and 50,000 “on the verge of starvation. At
Leicester 5000 men are receiving outdoor relief, and twice
as many are barely existing independently. There are
6000 houses “to let” in the town. The poor there are
said not to have been in so miserable a plight since the
Crimean war. At Sheffield, which should he one of the
busiest spots in England, there are 2030 persons in receipt
of outdoor relief, 1728 indoor paupers, and several thou-
sands who cannot get sufficient employment to keep
them in moderate comfort.
It is officially stated that all the London workhouses
are crowded to their last inch, though the winter cannot
yet be said to have begun. At Poplar (from which an
appeal has been made to the King) many of the dockers
are “ living on one day’s work a week,” and 3890 persons
are receiving outdoor relief. . Thousands of the same
class of workers are reported to be out of employment at
Liverpool and Cardiff. There is also much suffering
among the colliers at the latter port in consequence of
the damage which the Russo-Japanese war has done to
maritime trade. The export of pig iron from Middles-
brough of late is described as the smallest for ten years,
and employment there has decreased accordingly. At
Leeds 3000 men are registered in the corporation bureau
as unemployed. These are a few of the outstanding
facts of the position, which seems likely to become much
worse as the winter advances.
Labor leaders suggest that the Government should
acquire tracts of land for cultivation by the unemployed,
the settlement and immediate control to be left to local
authorities. “ Whatever is done,” says Mr. Crooks,
“ not a single sixpence should be expended on charity.
It is work that is wanted.” He points out that sufficient
could be raised to give the poor a start in agricultural
work on a small scale if they had the land. The expense
of getting the land is so great that only the State could
meet it.
The men on the land in England—the men who
understand agriculture—can’t make a living now
by reason of high rents, Ereetrade and foreign
competition. So there begins to be a feeble talk
about replacing them with men who don’t know
anything about agriculture, and who have been
driven out of their trades by Freetrade and foreign
competition. The Freetrader always ‘‘kids him-
self” that England’s vast imports are imports of
raw material to be made up in the vast factories
that have grown up under Freetrade —but they
aren’t. In 1902 Britain imported £132,479,875
worth of manufactured goods, and in 1903 it
imported £134,059,090 worth—mostly manufac-
tures of iron and steel, machinery, cotton and
woollen fabrics, leather goods, chemicals, earthen-
ware, glass, &c. This is taking no account of such
more or less manufactured products as flour,
cheese, refined sugar, butter, spirits, and manu-
factured tobacco. Every year the imports of
manufactured goods into Britain grow larger, and
the local artisan’s chance of a job fades propor-
tionately. And Freetrade has no remedy to offer,
unless Cobden’s bones can be called a remedy.

“ G., ” from S. Aus., which is to have a General
Election in a few months :
As an ardent democrat, I urge the Labor party not to
call itself the Socialistic party. I know the cocky
farmer’s mind well, and I know his political aspirations
are not wide of the L.P.’s ambition to burst up monopoly
in land, to get at the absentee, to tariff local industries
into healthy existence, to throttle the Trust Octopus ;
but I also know that the word Socialism sends a shiver
through him. The L.P. must check its impetuosity,
effect a cure of social and industrial evils by legislative
potions bearing a less obtrusive label ; and then say to the
applauding cocky, “ You have been swallowing Socialism.
The railways with their cheap carriage, the State bank
with its cheapening of money, the Agricultural College,
and travelling instructors, the Public schools —all these
things are Socialism. How do you like it?” He’d ask for
more. I know a couple of ‘ 1 Socialists,” adherents of the L. P.,
who want to be M.’sP. They hurl hunky definitions like
this at the staggered farmer—“ On this point all
Socialists agree. Our aim, one and all, is to obtain for
the whole community complete ownership and control
of means of transport, the means of manufacture, the
mines and the land.” It takes his breath away. He
thinks about his little bit of land and the money he’s put
into it. His leanings to the L.P. unbend, and he passes
his vote on to the fat Conservative to be on the safe side.
The fact that the farmer has a good deal of Socialism
already, and likes it, doesn’t make him any less shy of
Socialism. He doesn’t, in fact, believe that these things
are Socialistic at all, and how can he be expected to
believe it, seeing that they’ve nearly all been nominally
introduced by alleged anti-Socialists ? The Labor party
must therefore get a chance to show him that it would
only give him just the same sort of stuff as he has been
taking and relishing. Above all things the party wants a
chance. And it will never get one while it tries to catch
the farmer, who is absolutely essential to him in agricul-
tural S. Australia, by holding up to him the thing that he
dreads most.
— »
The worse the bargain Australia made over the
Naval Tribute, the greater the need to rectify the
mistake. The mere fact that we are paying an
annual £200,000 doesn’t, of course, protect us. A
man might have paid 10s. for a Christmas turkey,
but if the turkey wasn’t there when he wanted it,
the fact that he had paid 10s. wouldn’t give him
a dinner. He had made a bad bargain oyer
the turkey and the 10s. ; but the need of something
for dinner would be as great as ever. Australia is
paying £200,000 for a protection which doesn’t
guarantee to be there when we want it; yet, in
spite of the payment, the need is as great as ever.
From the days when he commanded 8. Australia’s
little fleet of one small gunboat to the present,
when he is the Commonwealth’s naval director,
Captain Cues well has strenuously advocated the
creation of an Australian navy ; and he is advocat-
ing it still. Luckily, he is now in a position to
speak where he can be heard ; and the matter will
soon be before the Federal Govt. Clearly we can
be sure of protection in no other way. If Aus-
tralia wants vessels, it must own them. The
existence of the Naval Tribute should possibly
influence us as to the class of boats to be acquired
—they should be strictly for harbor and coastal
defence : torpedo boats, submarines, and the like.
But beyond that, the fact that we are paying
dearly for a protection which doesn’t protect
should not count.
—
Before the inrush of t’otliersiders the ’Groper ad-
ministrators of Westralia did some peculiar
things. One was to give a shipping co. the
nor’-western mail service without tendering,
and the co. has hung on to it for 20 years. Just
on the eve,,: of Federation a fresh contract was
entered into with the same co. to carry the mails
for another six years for an annual £4500, the
co. to have the free use of the telegraphic
service, and also exemption from pilot, har-
bor, and other dues. No tenders were invited,
and the whole business was carried through
as though it were a deal in butter. Only one
mail steamer is run monthly, and much of the
postal matter and passengers and cargo are sent
forward by the Singapore boats which leave every
fortnight. Of course, the Postal Department has
to pay poundage to the owners of the Singapore
steamers, in addition to the thumping subsidy
presented to the contracting co. ; but the Common-
wealth Government can do nothing in the way of
annulling the contract. Meanwhile, the mail
service is costing close on £lO,OOO a year ; and
the arrangement with the co'. .is binding for
another three years. . - j
