SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “ H.M.S. Pinafore.”
Theatre Royal “A Desperate Game.”
Lyceum “ Blind Man’s Buff.”
Tivoli Riokards’ Co.
Queen’s Hall Williamson’s Bio-Tableau.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “ Romeo and Juliet.”
Her Majesty’s “The Geisha,”
New Theatre Royal.. “Sinbad the Sailor.”
Opera House Hackensohmidt.
Bijou “A Soldier and a Man.”
Princes Court “ Shooting the Chute,” &c.
It has been a conservative week among the
Sydney theatres. At Her Majesty’s, “Pinafore”
has continued to charm and please : there’s no
better proof of the very high quality of Gilbert
and Sullivan’s work than the fact that the “ olio ”
interpolated into the second act in this production
to spin out time (the public likes to be amused
until 11 p.m.) seems tame compared with the
opera itself. Yet it is a very bright patch of “olio,”
with Miss Eileen Boyd as chief singer, and an ex-
cellent hornpipe. “The Pirates of Penzance” is in
naration. At the Royal “A Desperate Game ”
, lyed out nightly to a cheery and happy con-
clusion. No change is contemplated until Satur-
day week. The Tivoli has found no reason to
change a strong programme, and is advertising its
present bill-of-fare as better than a pantomime for
the children ; which is probably a fact. The
average pantomime of these days is taken ad-
vantage of by the leading ladies to undress more
than usual, and by the leading comedians to
parade jokes with hardly any clothes on them at
all. At the Tivoli just now the show is clean and
wholesome and has many features attractive to
the youngsters.
** * *
These are the last hours of Peg Woffington
at the Lyceum ; Saturday night Nellie Stewart
will cease to enter upon a donkey in the furbelows
of 150 years ago, and will be somebody’s sweet
sister in the frocks of to-day. She will still be
pursued by two lovers, but they will step out of
something as modern as Wall-street, New York ;
one of them will be a pork-butchering sort of
millionaire, of course, and the other, who is an
otherwise nice young man, will go away to shoot
perfect strangers in a far land. “ Blind Man’s
Buff,” in spite of the pork-butchering person and
its domestic interest, will, it is promised, prove
just as dressy and ornate as “ Pretty Peggy.”
** * *
Mr. J. C. Williamson’s new concert co.
opens a Sydney season on Feb. 14. The combi-
nation is headed by Foldesy, a Hungarian ’cellist,
who has already won fame on Continental concert
platforms as an artist of rare technique and ex-
pression, and Miss Elizabeth Parkina, a young
American soprano with a Covent Garden reputa-
tion. Miss Margaret Thomas (contralto), Mr.
Whitworth Mitton (tenor) and Mr. Victor Mar-
mont (pianist) will complete a strong co.
** * *
Some of the battles between the Russians
and Japanese have been the greatest, in point of
numbers, in which white men have engaged,
since the Goths and Huns fought over the loot
of the Roman Empire at Chalons. And no inci-
dent of it has been more sensational than the
siege of Port Arthur. Captain Scott Harden’s
illustrated lecture at the Centenary Hall, York-
street, Sydney, to-night should therefore arouse
interest. He has just come from the historic
siege (he was London Chronicle and Daily
Graphic war correspondent), and should be able
to tell a moving tale.
** * *
Although Hackenschmidt is evidently the
most respected of the muscle-men who have ap-
peared at Rickards’, and although nobody questions
the genuineness of his champion title or denies
that he is a sportsman in his exhibition work,
still public sympathy for the most part is with his
opponents, provided they be white. The other
fellow is invariably the underneath dog in the
tussle, and he labors under the indignity of being
offered “£lOO to nothing” if he can throw the
Russian. The overthrow of Hackenschmidt would
be a startling novelty, and the public are looking
for startles. That’s the situation where white
challengers are concerned. But if Hackenschmidt
tackles the Indian wrestlers —Buttan or Gunga—•
he will find himself a popular idol all the time.
That’s where the white Australia policy will come
in. Even the stout, respectable gentlemen with
“a stake in the country ” who write letters to the
Argus, protesting that a White Australia is an
abominable invention of the Labor party —even
those blatant old capitalists will shout with the
mob if they go and see the black man flattened
out by Hackenschmidt at Melbourne Opera House
or the Exhibition-building. Fatman is human
when he gets his blood up.
sle * * *
The Tituses—Madame Lydia Yeamans-T.
and her spry husband—completed their short and
sweet engagement with Rickards last week, and
are now off to America again. From America
they go to England, and their present determina-
tion is to return to Australia within 18 months for
settling-down purposes. This is Mrs. Titus’
nearest approach to a native land. Her people
are mostly Australian, and she —born at sea—was
only a fortnight old when she first saw Melbourne.
As for the jaunty Titus, he is a citizen of the
world, with strong leanings towards the newest
half of it.
* # *
The revival of “ Florodora ” in Melbourne
reminds that Irene Outtrim, sauciest of the ori-
ginal Six Pretty Maidens, lately scored her first
success as a leading lady in ’Frisco. A good many
Australian girls have had to go abroad to realise
their importance, and Miss Outtrim is another of
them. ’Frisco notices of her first appearance at
the Tivoli in “ King Dodo ” were good but rather
conflicting as to details. The Call described her
as “an English beauty of graceful figure and the
prettiest of faces, having personality and charm to
spare.” The ’Frisco Bulletin spoke of her as
“an English prima-donna, who danced gracefully
and sang with a distinctive charm and a rather
light voice.” The Chronicle said, “ Clever
actress, personal magnetism,” &c., &c. ; and the
Evening Post delivered an instructive judg-
ment on our young friend: “Miss Outtrim,
who was captured on her way from Mel-
bourne to New York by the Tivoli management,
won much favor with the audience. Her dancing
lacks flexibility and grace . . . Australia evi-
dently does not give to her dancers the finish that
is noticeable in English artists. It is as an actress
that Miss Outtrim last night won her honors.
She has evidently had great experience in the
tricks of the stage, and, as far as acting is con-
cerned, can teach some of the listless female stars
of the Tivoli many valuable lessons.” A com-
paratively brief Australian experience seems to
have gone a long way with the Ballarat girl on the
American stage. She had been coached by Fred
B. Norton before she joined Williamson’s co., but
with the exception of her prominent chorus part
in “Florodora” the bright girl was given very
little chance to show her mettle.
** * *
Andersonian melodrama droops and lan-
guishes somewhat at Melb. Bijou. The house was
not built for deeds of blood and thunder. A little
more stage room is required to give the virtuous
characters a chance of expressing their feelings in
the broadest possible manner without falling over
the furniture, or infringing upon the landscape in
the far distance. As for the villains and the
comic characters, they probably feel a sense of
insecurity in being too near to the audience.
They almost invite a thunderbolt from a wrathful
“god” when they come before a curtain. It
recalls the story of Binks, the questionable
tragedian, tolling a brother pro. that he had
played Hamlet for one consecutive night in a
rugged provincial district. “ I suppose you got
a call at the end of every act?” inquired his friend.
“My boy,” said Binks, “ they did more than call
me. They dared me.” This by the way.
* * * *
“A Soldier and a Man,” staged at the
Bijou on Saturday, is a domestic trouble before it
breaks away to S’Africa and presents crowded
spectacles of British conflict with the Zulus. The
tall hero restores himself to public favor by de-
feating the enemy with great slaughter, but in the
first two acts he brings his wearisome parents to
woe, and almost drives his wife to drink. He
talks so freely about drowning his despair in
brandy that his wife grabs it from him and pro-
poses to seek the bubble consolation even at the
bottle’s mouth, whereupon he spills the good liquor
on the floor rather than let it be her only comfort
instead of his. Then they wander back to Devon-
shire, and come in at the death of his aged mother-
in-law, whose dear old home has been sold up
owing to his bad conduct. It is just here that the
young waster’s wife, remembering his selfishness
re the brandy, forbids him to assist at mother’s
funeral, and casts him loose upon the world, after
which he washes his sins away in Zulu blood, as
previously stated .s, “ A Soldier and a Man” is a
better sort of melodrama than some of the more
modern specimens, although it is handicapped
with the puerility of a military she-male named
Sergeant Jane, who appears to have sole com-
mand of a demented father. Miss Duggan catches
her breath at brief intervals in the leading-lady
part, where Miss Dora Winton figured very effec-
tively in former days ; and the rest of the co. in-
cludes Vivian Edwards, Dunbar, Stanford, Willard,
Harrison, Nunn and others, apart from Anderson’s
auxiliary forces.
* * * *K-
Both of J. C. Williamson’s shows will be
changed on Saturday. “ L’Aiglon ” has reached
its fourth week at the Princess’s, and four weeks
is a long innings for play of purely French
national sentiment, which never breaks into a
loud smile, nor touches the tender subject of
matrimony. All things considered, the fictitious
story of Little Buonaparte and his distressing
cough has been a retnarkable success. “Romeo
and Juliet ” follows. Melbourne, being warmly
disposed towards Miss Tittell Brune in top-boots,
will wish to see how she deals with the familiar
character of Juliet, the young woman who made
three different appearances in a nightgown prior
to committing suicide; in her shroud.
*. * * *
The “Florodora ” revival at Her Majesty’s
clashed, during the first week, with a spell of hot
weather that seemed to take all the brightness out
of the performance. Deterioration set in where
improvement, under cooler circumstances, might
have been fairly expected. Cyrus Gilfain and
Tweedlepunch played football with one another’s
hats, and the ladies, some of them, went about
their duties in a perfunctory manner as though
they resented the rising temperature. Friday of
this week is to be a Lauri complimentary night,
to celebrate the 14th anniversary of G.L.’s associ-
ation with the Royal Comic Opera Co., says the
advt. A benefit night nowadays is usually marked
by the intrusion of sundry items borrowed from
previous entertainments, and Lauri’s 14th anni-
versary will be no exception to this rule. On
Saturday a further revival of “ The Geisha ” will
fill the bill.
** # *
The panto, at Melbourne Royal is still
happening eight times a week. Also, it continues
to be an unduly long panto., and its dullest
moments, as before, are due to the ballets and the
“ variety ” turns. A red and green ballet in the
2nd act needs to be twice as active and only half
as long. At the Athenaeum Hall, a short farewell
season of Grossi has displaced the Biograph.
Grossi, who begins to look rather tired of his job,
is an interesting exponent of mental subjective-
ness up to a certain point, but his range of
obedience seems to be confined to pin-
finding and little things like that. One would
like to see him mentally induced to a wrestle with
Hackenschmidt, the “controller ’ standing by
and ordaining that Grossi should throw the
Russian on his back and sit on him whilst the
referee counts him out. Doughty men are im-
plored to come along and challenge Hacken-
schmidt without delay. Next week will be his
last at the Opera House, and, in the words of his
Prophet Rickards, the amiable monster is ready,
anxious, and eager to upset anybody he can catch
hold of. Three of the minor lights of the Opera
House programme are going out this week, which
means that other luminaries of corresponding
candle power are about to arrive.
#* # *
Adelaide shows have been prejudiced by
the fact that the powers below, after a little pre-
liminary skirmishing, fairly took the lid off.
Rickards closed up hastily, and the Tivoli was
pluckily invaded by an Andrew Hodge co. (from
Innamincka and Wattle Plat), which tackled the
sorrows of Little Eva. Dampier, at the Royal,
evidently agreed with a fellow-dramatist that you
cannot hold a fire in your hand by thinking on
the frosty Caucasus. At any rate, he gave up
“The Exiles of Siberia,” which had been billed,
and has relied on a mere “Black Flag.’ Per-
haps he thought the expensive Siberian scenery
would melt.
The Wild Cat Column.
N.S.W. is offering over 4 per cent, in London
for a £2,000,000 redemption loan, and, even then, doesn’t
venture to ask for the money for more than 10 years ; in
other words, it is to come in well ahead of the £12,820,200
which falls due in 1918, and the £16,695,065 which has to
be provided for shortly after that. It’s well enough to
make the new loans short dated provided that the State
resolutely sets itself forthwith to live honestly, and pay
its way, and wipe out the black smudge against its name,
because, in that case, the sooner it gets the chance to
benefit by the better terms that will then be available the
better ; but, if the State is going to drift on into debt, as
hitherto, then the short-dated loan, and the multiplica-
tion of appeals to the public, simply increase the chances
of a failure and a smash some day.
But the loan troubles are small troubles this
year compared with what are coming in the next three.
This list shows the position :

N.S. W. Treasurer Carruthers says he was advised by cable
in November that unless expenditure was reduced the
new loan would be a failure. ' Joseph’s reply to that was
the figure-juggling referred to last week. When the fact
soaks in that there has been juggling, and that the
Reform crowd hasn’t reformed worth two cents, and that
the improvement in the position is almost entirely due to
improved railway returns, the result of an extra-good
harvest, the future Treasurer will have to argue hard
with Cohen before the bill is renewed. Cohen isn’t a
person whom you can rely upon catching more than once
with a juggle and a broken promise.
» * * *
Of the 280 pastoral leases in the N.S.W. hack-
country, rentals of which the Western Lands Commission
has just reduced wholesale, about 140 are held absolutely
by the big mortgage and land co.’s—most of them
absentees. Over 60 are held by the banks. How many
of the balance of 80 are mortgaged up to the hilt or there-
abouts is not stated ; but practically the whole of the
Western division has fallen into the hands of the mort-
gagees, and most of the mortgagees wish it hadn’t. For
the last couple of years all these lessees have been paying
a provisional rent, which totals about £65,000 more than
the rent now fixed ; so that not only will the State in
future get £65,000 less than hitherto, but many lessees
w-on’t have to pay anything at all for some years, having
overpaid largely during the provisional rent period.
Treasurer Carruthers didn’t make any provision or allow-
ance for this ; he took the rents as they came in, and put
them all to the credit of the year in which they were paid.
Now he will refer to the great falling off in revenue and
the awful difficulties over which he had to triumph,
whereas the fact is that hitherto the revenue was inflated
by stuffing it with rents which covered the future as well
as the present. Still, that’s a small thing to a State which
habitually stuffs the proceeds of land sales into its revenue.

The New Zealand wrote off £4354. The Colonial
Mutual shareholders have actually subscribed only
£37,500 ; the other £62,500 consists of profits capi-
talised. On the amount actually subscribed the
dividend was at the rate of 214 per cent. The
Australian Mutual divided £4290 as a bonus to
policy-holders ; the amount paid to shareholders was 10
p.c. and £4290 as bonus. On the amount actually paid
up (£6250) the Aus. Mutual’s div. was 1321 p.c. The
Mercantile Mut. paid as bonus to polity-holders, £6067 ;
on the £22,500 actually paid up the div.' was 27 p.c. The’
Australian Alliance wrote off £1225. This co. has capital-
ised £75,000 of profits ; on the £50,000 actually paid the
div. was 25 p.c. The Commonwealth, a new office,
charged nothing in profit and loss account to cover unex-
pired risks ; wherefore the reserves stated here are not
true reserves.

Generally speaking, the offices which do a large marine
business show a low expense but a higher loss rate. The
Australian Alliance shows its departmental figures ; the
marine business cost 21’3 p.c. of premiums and the fire
business 357 p.c., while the marine losses were 71 p.e.
and the fire losses only 38‘4 p.c. Loss rates are also re-
duced by increasing expense rates in order to secure and
watch only selected risks. The Merc. Mutual, Aus. Mutual,
and Mut. of Tas., with the highest expense rates, have the
lowest loss rates. Those offices also show the highest per-
centage of premiums available as profits and to cover
extra unexpired risks. It. looks as though 1 per cent,
spent in supervision saves a good deal more than 1 per
cent, in losses.
In addition to underwriting profits, there are
profits from investments. In comparison with capital,
reserves are usually high, taking the similar figures with
regard to banks as a standard. But the insurance
company has always an enormous contingent liability to
which the ordinary bank is a stranger. It knows approxi-
mately what in ordinary circumstances it will have to
pay out; but it must also be prepared for extraordinary
circumstances, and if it isn’t so prepared it is a fraud of
an insurance office. Australia has had tremendous luck
so far in the way of escaping conflagrations ; but the risk
is still here. To a very great extent, therefore, the office
which does its duty best to the insuring public is the
office which puts most aside to meet the future shock
which it has taken money to provide against. The next
list shows the proportion of profits thus reserved last
year ; also the interest earned on the average of all assets •

The most remarkable feature of this table is the high rate

detail ; it is obvious, however, that in Maoriland a large
and profitable mortgage business is done, while in Aus-
tralia many of the co.’s have sunk large sums in offices
which are designed as advertisement as well as invest-
ment. But the fact that they are a good advertisement
doesn’t add much to their value as a more or less liquid
reserve against, say, a conflagration hazard.

Nothing in Queensland makes much progress nowadays;
still the North Queensland certainly isn’t moving back,
but, instead, is strengthening itself by putting aside, in
proportion to its profits, more than -almost any other
bank in Australia. On the total of shareholders’ funds
(capital £lOO,OOO and reserves), its earnings for some time
past have been :
June, 1901 .. £4 7 6 p.c. June, 1903 .. £3 14 8 p.c.
Dee., 1901.. 410 8 „ Dec., 1903.. 4 3 0 „
June, 1902 .. 312 1 „ June, 1904 .. 316 11 „
Dec., 1903.. 313 7 ~ Dec., 1904.. 4 2 0 „
These are not high figures ; undoubtedly they could be
made higher if the bank’s conservative policy were eased
a bit. In coin and bullion, which isn’t earning anything
(except, probably, public confidence), it maintains no less
than 23 per cent, of all liabilities to the public and the
Treasury ; in liquid assets, including,coin, it holds over 42
percent. When things show a turn’in Queensland, it
certainly won’t be necessary to hold this proportion in
coin, however desirable it may still be to maintain a high
proportion of other liquid assets ; and it’s just a question
whether things haven’t taken the necessary turn already.

* * * *
A.M.P. Society runs now also something
between industrial and ordinary life insurance—a monthly
payments policy, the society collecting the premiums at
t he insurer's work-place. But the extra cost is heavy. The
policy which would cost £2 16s. Sd. per year, premiums
payable quarterly, costs £3 Is. under the new scheme ;
what would cost £lO Os. Bd. with quarterly payments
costs £lO 18s. with monthly payments. The man who
can’t “ save up ” for a quarter no doubt wants insurance
more than anybody, but the poor beggar has to pay
heavily for his incapacity, and then it’s doubtful whether
the extra premium will recoup the society.

The interest earnings (apparently net, after paying inter-

assets the gross earnings were only just 2 per cent. Pro-

have been written'down only £4348. In this respect the
balance-sheet is very unsatisfactory, and it’s astonishing
that so progressive a co. doesn’t have a cleaning-up.
There is talk of changing the name of Burns,
Philp and Co., Ltd., to James Burns and Co., Ltd. But
Philp, though he lias been out of it for years, still has
some warm friends inside, and they are fighting the sug-
gestion. It isn’t much to struggle for, but, then, there
isn’t much left to Philp, these days.

This remarkably prosperous little co. shows a profit at the
rate of £l4 11s. sd. per cent, per annum on shareholders’
funds ; the only trustee co’s which show better figures are
the Perpetual of Sydney, Perpetual of Dunedin (M.L.), and
Westralian. The trust estates in hand are valued at
£1,016,733 (this co. has begun to adopt the good practice
of showing the position in this matter), and the co.’s
expenses were at the rate of only ss. 2d. per cent, per
annum., of the aggregate value of the trusts. Certainly
this is a higher rate than the largest of the metropolitan
offices show ; but it is low, nevertheless, considering the
work which the trustee co. does. All the capital and
some of the reserves are in Government stock, and that is
a solid thing to be able to say about a trustee co.

This is a remarkably fine improvement, mainly due to an
increase in commissions; it isn’t explained, however,
whether this co. adopts the bad plan of taking credit for
the whole corpus commission as it is received, allowing
the ordinary running commission to provide for sub-
sequent cost of administration, for which it is often quite
inadequate. One could be surer about the real value of
trustee co.s’ balance sheets if corpus commission were
abolished in favor of a slight increase in the running com-
mission. The co. appears to have very profitable invest-
ments ; on its total average assets, the return was 51- per
cent., less a small amount spent on improvements, &c.
Of its funds £lO,OOO is in Vic. stock, £6OOO in offices, and
about £9OOO on mortgage. The profit was at the rate of
£l2 7s. Id. per cent, on shareholders’ funds, which brings
the Sandhurst and Northern well up to the front in the
list of profitable trustee co.’s. The amount of estates in
trust is not disclosed, as it is desirable it should be.

The co.’s capital (£30,000) and reserves, as well as £21,000
debentures, are all represented by the Exchange premises,
which gtand at £70,000, nothing having- been written off
that item-for years. In addition- to the debenture lia-
bility, there is also a pretty constant item of unspecified
“ sundry creditors,” now £4764 ; but the former small
overdraft has disappeared. The co.'is doing somethino-
more, though, than earning a not too certain 6 per
cent.; it has lately promoted wool and dairy exhibitions on
its premises, both of which have been educationally
valuable ; and a mining exhibition is mentioned for the
future. In these movements, Mr. O. H. Hayes, the secre-
tary, a man of rare public spirit, has slaved unselfishly
There is room now for a Shocking Example Exhibition—
an exhibition of the things that are manufactured out of
the raw products which we own, and either don’t use at
all or send to others to make up for us. There really
isn’t any reason why the Exchange Co. should be prouder
of a pound of cheese than a yard of calico, and there’s
more to jubilate over in a yard of flannel than a bale of
wool.

The North-Western Farmers’ Association,
Ltd. (Tas.), a co-operative concern which seems to have
found out lately that things were not what they seemed
(the association began with a deficit of £6o,' flotation
expenses):—

The directors say, with regard to the writing off, that
“it has not been forced upon them as a result of this
year’s working, but solely to render the balance-sheet
true in every detail.” This is a fairly plain way of saying
that some previous alleged profits have been really bogus
profits—bogus to the extent of £1719. In addition to
the absurdly high dividends paid by this bantling, a bonus
was several times declared, the amount being issued not
in cash but in fully paid shares. Presumably, though the
profit was bogus, being only represented by bad debts,
the shares issued in respect thereof won’t be also reckoned
as bogus, and cancelled, though it is fairly clear they ought
to be. The position now seems to be pretty bad ; the
paid capital is only £3831, including the capital issued
in respect of the bogus profits, and the co. has an over-
draft of £7664, for which the directors have had to give
their personal IO IJ as well, as the co’s. By far the
largest asset is “sundry debtors,” £6666 ; it was appar-
ently from this item that the £1719 was written off ; and
this is always a doubtful asset. The cocky co-operator
nearly always wants big dividends as well as big rebates ;
he wants things at cost price, or a little under, and he
also wants to subsequently draw a 10 p.c. dividend on the
transaction. He wants to score, in short, both as buyer
and as seller; and he can’t do it. And the co. which
professes to show him that he is doing it always runs up
against the hard fact as this one has done.
* * * *
The P. and O. Co. last year received £337,840 in
various Govt, subsidies. The dues paid to the Suez Canal,
of which the British Govt, is the largest shareholder,
reached £332,091 for same period. The dues paid for the
use of the canal far exceeded the subsidy received from
the British Govt. ; so instead of spending money fostering
the mail steamers, the British Govt., as far as the P. and
O. Co. goes, makes a handsome profit, and still poses as
broad-minded and open-handed. Australia could be
extravagantly generous and pay the P. and O. 2d. per
letter to England, provided it charged the Co. 3Jd. per
letter every time the mail-boats passed the Leeuwin.
* * * *
Mount Drysdale, Cobar, is in the warm place
prepared for the co. which pays extravagant dividends
and lets a reserve fund slide. It now proposes to recon-
struct, issuing 5120 shares (one for every 10) carrying a
liability of ss. per share, to be paid up in gradual three-
pences, notwithstanding that there is a standing liability
of some £3OOO. Also, it’s tolerably certain the only
chance of working the mine at a profit is to treat the low-
grade ores, and as this can’t be done without replacing
the old Huntingdon mills with a battery, there seems to
be trouble ahead in this direction. Still, new shares
are likely to be absorbed by old holders ; and if nothing
serious happens to the benevolent scheme, there’s always
the chance of another rich pocket arriving to straighten
out the position.
* * * *
Queen Bee, Cobar, which was lately much dis-
turbed in its mind whether to pay a Xmas dividend or
use the money to duplicate the reverberatories, wisely
decided in favor of latter. December saw' two furnaces
running only half-time through accident, and a return of
just on 20 tons copper ; but January started with a clean
bill, and, bar accidents to machinery, this month’s return
will probably exceed 35 tons. As working expenses don’t
exceed £BOO a month, and there isn’t any large octopus
to grab the profits, it’s quite on the cards that annual
meeting in February will fihd a sixpenny div. waiting.
* * * *
Mount Boppy South, Cobar (N.S.W.), in 8000
shares of £1 each, hasn’t much to show for 12 months’
expenditure beyond opening up to the 200 ft. level, and a
little indefinite exploratory crosscutting. Shareholders
lately decided to 'have mine thoroughly overhauled, and
James Hocking, of Myalls and Peak Hill, who undertook
it, reports that operations .have been carried out irregu-
larly, and evidently without any definite object. He
advises sinking a new shaft to water level, 180 ft. north of
main shaft, and crosscutting to eastern formation at
intervals of 100 ft. “ The value, extent, and character of
the lodes fully warrant a vigorous policy of systematic
development, particularly in depth and to the north.”
Alleged Hockingjs now negotiating for an option over the
property, and is likely to get it.
* * * •
Report of Sulphide Corporation, Broken Hill, to
the middle of 1904 showed a year’s profit of £33,324, before
charging anything- for depreciation or sinking fund ; net
profit after these deductions, £43,507. Co.’s movements
have been erratic
1897 .. Loss' £18,842 1901 .. Profit £73,049
1898 .. „ 10,935't 1902 .. „ 2,927
1899 .. Profit 115,154 1903 .. „ 24,935
1900 .. „ ’ 178,002 1904 .. „ 43,507
Ore treated at mills, 201,541 tons ; profit per ton, 4s. 4d.
This, however, doesn’t show what the co. could work at
if the worst came to the worst. The writings-off, &c.,
are made to equal the expenditure on capital account,
which was heavy ; charging the output with only out-of-
pocket expenses incurred in current production, the pro-
fit was Bs. 3d. per ton. Some of this was, no doubt, made
from treatment of purchased gold ores ; some also from the
sale of “zincs.” This is the only co. which has tackled Aus-
tralian production of metallic zinc ; but the business must
so far have cost the co. dearly. An extensive plant,
erected near Newcastle, has been several times more-or-
less reconstructed, and very little of it is used. The mag-
netic concentrators at the mine produced 22,838 tons of
“ zincs,” but only 1475 tons of them w'ere smelted at the
co.’s works—all the rest were sold to be smelted in
Europe, which, so far as this business goes, means Ger-
many and Belgium. Year after year the co. says it hopes
that it will soon be able to set the industry fairly going ;
year after year it spends thousands, and sometimes tens
of thousands, in experiments and new plant; and year
after year it finds that the less it treats in Australia the
less money it loses. And still a zinc bonus, or State help
in any other shape, isn’t needed !

The increase is partly due to the increase in the amounts
written off and applied to capital account. The big creep
of a couple of years ago made the main shaft unsafe, and
the cost of the new shaft which became necessary is being
provided by a fixed charge per ton of ore, added to work-
ing charges. In another respect the movement is unsatis-
factory. In 1902 it took 495 tbns of ore to make 100 tons
concentrates; in 1903,593 tons; in 1904,523 tons. Meantime
there has been no corresponding improvement in the
quality of the concentrates. Still, the co. has big possi-
bilities. Without deductions for depreciation or sinking
fund, profits w'ere equal to £3 10s. 6d. per ton of the total
lead output in the concentrates. Lead averaged £ll9s. 9d.
per ton during 1 the co.’s year ; so that, it lead and zinc
remain stead 3% the co. would have to realise less than £8 for
its lead before it would be out of pocket. That leaves a
good margin. As to ore reserves there is no need to worry.-
At present rate of out-turn there is 15 to 20 years’ supply
proved, even allowing for hardly anything below 800 ft.;
and average ore has been struck in an exploratory cross-
cut 200 ft. below that. Then there are already about
1,000,000 tons of zincy middlings, &c., at surface ; and the
co. is attacking these heaps now with an enlarged mag-
netic plant. It is a huge mine, in fact; but it has to carry
a very big capital (550,000 £1 pref. shares and 550,000 15s.
ordinary shares, besides £lOO,OOO in 5 p.c. debentures);
and it handles an ore in the treatment of which, after all
these years, metallurgists are still only groping. Follow-
ing figures show the extraction of the metals by concen-
tration for some time past at the Sulphide Corporation’s
mill and the neighboring B.H. South mill, between which
there is keen rivalry :

It is upon the slimes, middlings and tailings, which con-
tain more than half the silver, a third of the lead, and
practically all the zinc, that the magnetic separating
plant is operating in a small way—so far with compara-
tively microscopic results in the matter of profits.
B.H. British output shows some improvement
in values.- But there has been practically m change in
the value of the faces of ore. The improvement in mill
reports results simply from dodging the places where the
refractory rhodonite shows heaviest.
* * * *
A while ago, when the Broken Hill boomkin
was being engineered, there was extra-special snorting at
the suggestion that North shares especially were being
rushed up. The North, it was said, was the very mine
which warranted a rush. To-day men who bought at 365.
are looking round for somebody to buy at 225. 6d. Hardly
a development since that time has been satisfactory ; and
the mill is now hung up a third of its time for want of
ore, though mining is rushed along three shifts daily.
* * * »
J. R. Godfrey, inspector of mines, will take the
management of Block 14. The Melbourne shareholders
and directors have regained control of the co., and this is
one of the early results ; during the years the mine was
under Adelaide control it had a managing director, who
had spent a lifetime qualifying for the post by dealing
in hides and sheepskins. The Mines Inspection branch
can’t keep its good men, and the recent, selection of a
chief inspector from outside the staff w'ill make matters
worse. A while ago another inspector left to take a
Broken Hill management at just double the salary the
State paid ; and since then the co. has thought his
services worth still further promotion and salary.
Godfrey, a Cambridge graduate, had been 14 years in the
service, 12 years as inspector. At Block 14 he will have a
pretty hard row to hoe, for want of funds, for one thing.
* * -Jr *
Statistics re lead production are not too com-
forting to the people who have lately been putting a
price on some Broken Hill shares twice as great as the
co.’s have paid altogether in dividends during the past 17
years. The total production (1903) was 910,000 long tons,
an increase of nearly 50 per cent, in ten years. German
statistics place Australia fifth among lead producing
countries, with an output of 93,000 tons. Mexico has
95,000 tons, Germany 141,000, and Spain 172,000. U.S.A.
leads with 266,000 long tons. N.S.W. Mines Department
gives the lead contents of Australian concentrates shipped
as 29,706 tons ; if this were deducted from the German
production and added to the Australian, the latter would
exceed the former.
* * * *
Latest re the “ influential European ” co. formed
to take up diamond-mining at Bingera (N.S.W.) is that
registration (in 300,000 £1 shares, 200,000 issued) has been
shifted from England to Guernsey—the place where a co.
can go and subsequently do pretty well anything. A
£300,000 co. to prospect for diamonds is a piece of humor
in itself. The worst of this sort of humor is that it
generally means tragedy for some poor fools.

Ballarat compares with Bendigo for last year :
Yield. Dividends.
Ballarat .. .. 51,5250 z. .. £21,600
Bendigo .. .. 248,0000 z. . 367,000
Ballarat never gives the total calls made. If it did it
would show the inwardness of the mining position. What
kills the district is (1) the proprietary co.’s that exact
tribute even from call-making lack of scien-
tific methods ; (3) the lack of support given by residents
to local shows ; and (4) the readiness to “ take in ” out-
siders by local mining men. Last cause has operated
more to bring about a withdrawal of capital than any-
thing else.

Victorian gold yield for 1904 was 821,0170 z.,
against 822,4240 z. in 1903. These figures bring the total
yield of the State to 67,557,4530 z., worth, roughly,
£270,000,000.

* * * *
Star of the West, Mack’s Creek (Vic.), has a
reputation built up on crushings of 349 tons, from which
6670 z. were obtained. This return put co. in funds ;it
also sent shares to over 20s. and furnished cash for the
No. 3 tunnel. Some of the originals did well out of the
scrip. They got it for a few pence, and, like sensible men,
sold out anywhere up to top rates. Shoot of gold appears
to be under 150 ft. in length at No. 2 tunnel, where it
splits into eastern and western formations, latter ajjpa-
rently carrying best gold. There are signs that the reef is
narrowing in western leg. Merriri, C.E., estimated that
about 2000 tons are proved above No. 2 tunnel. As he is
very careful, this estimate may be taken as correct,
though wiseacres double the' figures. Winze from
No. 1 tunnel went down in rattling good stone,
as 130 tons gave 2980 z., and shot shares forward.
Concern, then, has promise, but it is folly to put it at
more than the prospecting stage ; 300 tons do not make
a mine, and the battery wants to do a lot more work
before the stock should fascinate. Public will be invited
to buy at an early date, but had better wait. Half-a-
dozen pup floats have been promoted round the Star, but
have not yet done anything.
* * * *
Black Horse, Egerton (Vic.), had a good repu-
tation, for, like its neighbor, which it has since swallowed,
it paid good divs. ; but to-day it is a sink for calls. Chief
supporter is one Vickery—not Ebenezer, of Sydney, but
a cute old Bendigo and Melbourne speculator, who now
owns a couple of stations up Tamworth way. Co. is
practically abandoning work at Black Horse mine to try
and pick up gold in the Egerton lease. Quartz is plenti-
ful, but, as is the case with so many mines in the Ballarat
district, gold does n’t seem to carry down to depth.
Mining man of the Argus has got on the soft
side of Rickard, of the Mixing and Engineering Journal,
of New York. Latter’s latest kindly word is to allude to
him as “that eminent mining authority.” Now, what
can we do for you ?
* * * «
The Vic. cyanide royalties should cease this
year. Four years ago the Govt, bought the Vic. patent
for £20,000, and let it out to the mining co.’s, with the
understanding that royalties should cease when they
totalled £20,000 plus interest. So far they have returned
about £16,266.
* * * *
Westralian gold yield for 1904 was 2,373,0200 z.,
which wa563,3000z. less than in 1903. This is only the second
time since Coolgardie was discovered that a retrograde
movement has taken place. Curiously, the shortage was
almost exactly the same in each case, the output for 1900
being 63,0000 z. below that for 1899—the fireworks year.
A dozen of the bigger mines show a heavy falling off'
while very few show any increase ; had it not been for
the widening of the area of production, the shortage
would have been much larger. Divs. for the year tot up
£2,050,559, nearly £9OOO ahead of 3903, and only £15,500
behind 1899, when L.V. Consols-distributed £625,000. So
far as can be judged, the retrogression is temporarily
checked, and it is most likely the output will slightly
increase.

The British Australasian (London) July 14,
1904 : “ The strong position of the Colonial Mutual Life
Assurance Society is sufficiently illustrated by the figures
in the report for 1903. The addition to the funds during
the year, was £107,965, bringing the total funds up to

* * * *
The No. 1 Centrifugal Pumping Plant on the
pioneer tin mine, Tasmania, has made another excellent
year’s record, the reportss hewing 1,016,300 cubic yards of
drift pumped for a return of 1064 tons of tin at a total
cost of 6 fid. per yard, raised 85 feet high to the sluice
boxes. This plant was constructed by the Austral Otis
Engineering Company, South Melbourne, who have also
lately completed the No. 2 plant of larger size. This
plant should be in operation in a short time, when the
output of the mine will be considerably .increased. The
plant has worked splendidly throughout the period, the
delays for stoppages of machinery amounting to less than
1 per cent, of the total working hours of the year, which
reflects credit both on the builders and management.*
* * * *
The electric plant of the Federal Palace Hotel,
corner Collins and King-streets, Melbourne, furnishes a
good example of a thoroughly successful electric installa-
tion, in technical as well as in commercial respects. This
plant generates all electric current required for the hotel
lighting; it consists of a twenty horse-power gas-engine
belted to a “ Ganz ” twelve kilowatts dynamo, a “ Ganz ”
switch-board containing all the measuring and controlling
instruments, and a storage battery with 62 cells. The
generating machinery runs continually during the day
time at its full capacity and highest efficiency, the surplus
current being stored into the battery ; the latter supplies
all necessary electricity required during the night; and it
forms also a substantial reserve in case of accidents.
Compared with the previous method of working, a saving
of £4OO per annum has been effected by this plant, the
concentrators for which were the Austral Otis Engineering
Company, Ltd., corner Hanah and Kavanagh-streets,
South Melbourne, as sole Australian agents for Ganz and
Company, Budapest, Hungary.*

The Borough Council, Wanganui, N.Z., passed
the following resolution on Nov. 29th; “ That this
Council desires to compliment Mr. Mestayer, C.E., on the
successful completion of the Wanganui-Okehu Water-
works scheme, at a cost of £60,000, the work being com-
pleted within the estimate. The council also desires to
express its appreciation of the Mephan Ferguson steel
pipes, and notes with satisfaction that all the joints are
sound, although upwards of 4600 were used over a length
of eighteen miles, in some places having to withstand a
pressure of 2881bs. to the square inch.”*

* * * *
Economical Gold Mining.—Low grade ores
and high cost in fuel and wages have made it imperative
that managers and engineers should reconstruct upon
more economical lines. Heretofore the boiler plant was
given little consideration, but the introduction of the
Hornsby “upright” water tube boiler having demons-
trated great economy, and with safety under indifferent
conditions of feed water, it is now superseding other types.
In W.A. these boilers are worked with an economy not
heretofore known on those fields, and in Queensland over
2500 h.p. is on order for the Mount Morgan Gold Mining
Co. It is cleaned in one fourth the time of other boilers
and has no tube troubles. Boilers may be seen in opera-
tion in Sydney and Melbourne. R. Hornsby and Sons,
Ltd., Sussex-street, Sydney, and Spencer-street, Mel-
bourne.*
* * * *
The Babcock Boiler seems to be favored by-
mining engineers, over 50 mines in Australia using these
boilers. Mount Morgan has 20, Waihi Gold Mining Co.
24, Mount Lyell 12, Tasmanian Gold Mining Company 7,
and at Broken Hill there are 30 distributed amongst the
mines, water works, and electric supply, while in
Western Australia they are looked upon as a necessity,
there being now 68 in the principal mines and Kalgoorlie
Water Supply. It will be noted that coal is expensive at
Broken Hill, and wood fuel is expensive at W.A. These
figures speak for themselves. Evidently the ease of
transportation, the economy in fuel and maintenance and
the adaptability to any class of fuel, that are such con-
spicuous points in the Babcock Boiler, are much
appreciated in Australian mines.*

Hannan’s Star, Kalgoorlie, should have a better
year ahead, if official reports can be relied on. “At 575 ft.
main lode has been driven on 350 ft. ; ore runs from 3ft.
to 7ft. wide, while values range from 25dwt. to over 30z.”
Such developments should make the mine one of the best.
Unfortunately many of the reports issued as to this mine
have been exaggerated. For instance, official estimates
of reserves a year ago figured out gold contents at 12dwt.,
whereas returns have only averaged 6 to 7dwt , and
November’s figures were only sdwt. Morrison, or Boulder
Deep Levels, lode is about the same width as the main lode,
but values, on the whole, are very poor and unpayable.
Morrison shaft has been fitted with new surface equip-
ment, and exploring is to be energetic. Mine has always
been patchy, but lode seems to be more regular in
settled country.

Society
While the Australian during Xmas was
putting water on his whiskers to keep himself
from catching fire, the Maori lander was looking
round for-his heaviest rug with which to guard
against an Arctic blast. Fires were in the same
request as whisky hot. Snow fell at Hanmer
(out of Christchurch) on Xmasday.
In a recent fatal case of snakebite in South
Gippsland, the sufferer drank a bottle and a half
of whisky during his 17-miles drive to the doctor’s
surgery, where ho was lifted from the buggy in a
comatose state from which he never rallied. The
medico had to first tackle the deadly whisky with
a stomach pump ; and strychnine injections with a
plentiful supply of electric battery followed, but
there was never the faintest sign of returning
consciousness. The man, of course, died of the
whisky. He might have survived the bite, but
the bottle made him a sure corpse.
♦ * * *
The great siege of Port Arthur closed with
a small horse incident—not with Stoessel’s body
under the ruins of the last wall, his face towards
the gun-flash, the last remnants of his force lying
in bayonet-torn heaps. The humorless vanity of
fighting generals is amazing. Nogi and Stoessel
bowing in a human shambles, talking airily of
each other’s mokes, is a theme for a Voltaire. It
suggests the pea-nut man carrying on business
with energetic fortitude on Judgment Day.

MAY: “Ever been jilted, Edith?”

From Groperdom :
Compare Hyde Park in Sydney with Queen’s Park in
Perth—each in the heart of its respective city. Hj'de
Park, full of frowsy loafers of unclean aspect and evil
blasphemy; the flower-beds surrounded with iron-spiked
railings : uncouth notice-boards projecting at each corner,
threatening dire penalties should one dare to pluck a
flower. Queen’s Park, the flower-beds masses of gorgeous
bloom, unprotected by even a modest shingle; no
threatening notices, not even the customary “Keep off
the grass.” The city folk, conspicuous by the absence of
the loafer element, lie about smoking and reading ; if
they feel inclined for a “ button-hole,” there is no one to
say them nay. There is one notice exhibited : “ This
park is the property of the people, and they are respect-
fully requested to protect their heritage from wanton
damage.”
Queensland’s Agent-General, Horace Tozer,
has allowed the British worship of the stray nigger
potentate to get on his nerves, and burst out the
other day :
During the seven years he had been the accredited
representative of Queensland in this country he had never
been introduced either to the present or to the late Prime
Minister, and he had not been more than a dozen times
to the Colonial Office, one of those occasions being when
he rushed there to offer the help of stalwart Queenslanders
to fight the battle of the Empire in Soutli Africa. When
a black king, ruling over fifty or sixty thousand people,
came to England, however, he was received with a marked
amount of etiquette and attention, which contrasted
strangely with the treatment he and his colleagues had
obtained.
It is a fact that London makes more fuss over a
Zulu or a Dahomey chief, with a picturesque
parrot-tail in his fuzzy hair, than to “the colo-
nies ; ” and it is not a bad thing that it is so, for
fussy interference is the last thing that Australia,
at least, wants. But as regards the Agents-General,
are theynpt contributory causes to the negligenceof
which complaint is made? Tozer artlessly admits
to “ rushing over ” to the Colonial Office to offer
stalwart Queenslanders to burn down farms in
S’Africa. Palpably the rush was for the lime-
light for himself. Otherwise, there was no need
to rush over. A sixpenny telegram would have
fulfilled all the business necessities of the position.
A more or less distinguished Australian who
visited London a while ago tells an illuminating
story of the Agent-General for his State. Ihe
Australian asked the A.-G. to present him to the
Secretary of State for the Colonies, and the A.-G.
joyfully consented, rigged himsa.f up in -some
sort of a uniform, and dragged the visitor along.
Evidently he wished to impress his fellow-Aus-
tralian with a notion of his zeal and importance,
for he opened the interview by “ragging” the
Minister soundly for sending to his office only
three tickets for the opening of the British Par-
liament. “My great state,” he declared, “ has a
population of —. The British Empire s total
population is . The number of seats available
for visitors at the opening of Parliament is -.
Now, on a population basis, my State is
entitled to at least eleven seats. Why,
me lud, Tasmania, with its insignificant
population, got three tickets. I demand, me lud,
fairer treatment for my great State.” The unfor-
tunate English Minister tried to suggest that Par-
liament was not opened on a population basis, but
it did nothing to check the flow of grievance. He
had to finally dole out some more “paper ” for the
free show, and then the Agent-General departed,
feeling that he had vindicated the honor of his
great State.

Melbourne’s “ houses of pleasure ” have
been lately receiving the much-needed attentions
of revenue detectives on the look-out for sly grog-
selling. One of these excise men, who seems to
have had a rorty time while doing the Common-
wealth’s duty, passed himself off as the “Rev.
John Jobseph,” a Methodist clergyman. The
Wussleyans are, naturally, greatly shocked. It is
a pretty grave reflection on “ the cloth ” when a
detective, anxious to put on a disguise that would
be accepted as natural in the haunts of vice, gets
up as a clergyman.
“Robbo ” : Yarned, the other day, with a
police officer who knows the “ Rocks ” (Sydney)
by heart. Asked whether the once notorious
Rocks’ push had passed away. “ Well, they have
grown up,” he told me. “ They are still there, or
near by, but nowadays are mostly decent married
men and good average citizens. They didn’t
arrive at sense early, but they arrived. They
were always more wild than wicked, more riotous
than ruffian. Perhaps the drink is better, too ;
certainly, there’s less of it. The police rarely
have a brick heaved at them now, and when they
do, it generally comes from some casual visitor.
And it’s much the same all round. There are
still a few ‘ pushes ’ in other parts, but they hardly
count in the way of organised ruffianism.”

From the Riverina, where the bush fires
swept the land on New Year’s Day :
The fire started late on Sunday afternoon on Ringwood
station, where the grass was as high as a table. The sheep
couldn’t keep it down ; it grew green and lush with the
late spring rains, and before the fire it was as white as a
tablecloth. The men were all away at Yarrawonga races ;
when they got back on Monday night it was all over. The
homestead was saved, but for the rest—well, it was simply
a wall of fire 40ft. high and sometimes 20 miles wide,
sweepimr along at about 30 miles an hour before the north
wind. Birds, rabbits, snakes—all were swept up in its
red jaws. A sight from the verandah of Kentucky home-
stead was five foxes, breaking from their blazing cover
like five scorched devil 3, with their brushes standing out
like burnt sticks. They came straight past the house,
through a flock of cackling fowls and into some scrub on
the left. Two minutes afterwards the fire swept that scrub,
too. but the foxes never came out. You could not walk
along the track of the Tabletop fences—the fences them-
selves were gone—for a week afterwards, for the stench of
dead rabbits. And the sheep—God have mercy ! I came
away on the Friday after, and some of the poor brutes
were still staggering about —blind, their eyes blistered out,
their hoofs burnt off, their wool a mass of black cinder.
There hadn’t been time to kill them. The lucky ones
were the dead. Curious how partial the fire was. Many
of the vineyards around Corowa were laid in ashes, others
were unscathed. One farmer, a few miles out, had just
cut and bagged 3010 bags of wheat ; he lost all but 300. A
mile away another cultivator had a 30-acre paddock of
wheat standing alongside the road ; next to that was a
strip of fallow, then a 100-acre paddock of wheat; one
“ arm ” of the fire took every ear of this latter ; the other
sweot up the road, clearing the grass as close to the fence
as if burnt off purposely, and left the 30-aere patch un-
touched, save for a slight scorching at the edge.

One result of the £5O fine imposed on a
N.S.W. J.P., for illegally witnessing an affidavit,
and the dictum of the Crown Law Office that a
J.P. must understand the contents of a docu-
ment witnessed by him, will be increased in-
comes for commissioners for affidavits —appointed
by the Supreme Court. Commissioners in the
city are entitled to charge Is. for an affidavit and
6d. for every exhibit, and sometimes there are ten
or a dozen exhibits. In the cquntry districts the
commissioner’s fee is 2s. 6d. The honorary J.P.
doesn’t receive anything for his autograph.

Melbourne analyst Percy Wilkinson on
ptomaines (in Melbourne nowadays most of the
talk is of ptomaines and butter commissions) :
There are many ptomaines, but only a few are poisonous.
They are the product of bieterial work on substances con-
taining nitrogen, and uuv appear or disappear at any
stage. They cannot be detected by the smell. Meat
which is putrid may possibly be innocuous, while meat
that smells quite fresh and sweet may contain deadly
poison. Their effects may di Ter through the personal
idiosyncrasy ; whereas some persons can eat putrid meat
—as high game—without injury, to others it is death.
Tasmania has a memory of the long and
lagging kind. An old man, who had been a
recipient of charity in Launceston some years ago,
had received a sentence of 24 hours for being
impudent to the secretary of the Benevolent
Society and. had escaped imprisonment by walking
out of the city, was pounced upon by the police at
Devonport and forwarded to Launceston to do liis
sentence. The old chap had been working steadily
for the two or three years since his horrible crime,
and was turned out of the Launceston gaol with
the intimation that he could walk back to Devon-
port if he liked. A Good Samaritan, however,
paid his railway fare.
The outback man is not too much troubled
by Faith-belief. A Western cocky, who signed to
deliver 400 bushels of wheat at the end of January,
was approached by an insurance agent, who
wanted to insure the crop, which was still stand-
ing. The cocky declined to do business. “ Why,
if the crop was burned, it would be a Judgment of
Heaven upon you for your stubbornness,” said the
agent, after all attempts had failed. “ I’ll chance
Heaven’s wrath,” said the cocky, “ I’ve ploughed
four furrows round the paddock.”
MARRIEP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My wife and l were best of friends
Before we wed, and now
Our bickering but seldom ends ;
Each simple little thing offends,
And any foolish matter lends
Occasion for a row.
When once a week I met my May,
We thought we were in Heaven.
Alas ! how many hapless stray,
Who might be friends for one good day,
And in the matrimonial way
Are enemies for seven !
E.
A FAILURE OF FAITH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He owned a farm, his name was Biggs,
A German on the border line.
His speciality was pigs.
His crops of hogs were very fine.
From Adelaide to Hay, I’ve heard,
Biggs’ bacon was a household word.
He held the creed of Dowie once,
And much admired that fat Elect
Declaring but a dolt and dunce
Each member of a rival sect.
His faith might save him many bills
For patent medicines and pills.
Last month, the grinning neighbors say
The air at Biggs’ was simply thick
With smells that rolled in proud array—
With odors that would catch and stick.
And Biggs sat where his hams were piled,
Lamenting like a little child.
When men approached him to invite
His confidence, he beat his head.
“ Ach, Himmel! I vos ruined kvite.
Elijah vos a rogue ! ” he said.
“ A liar, too. Ten thousand tamns 1
I tried dot faith cure mit mine hams 1 ”

The British Government Committee that
inquired into the case of Adolf Beck (the unfortu-
nate who did seven years’ hard in an English gaol
for the crime of a man whom he resembled) has
made in its report one highly sensible recommen-
dation :
That the anomaly of “pardoning” a man who never
ought to have been convicted should be abolished, and
that the conviction should be quashed and an acquittal
entered on the record instead.
For “anomaly” read “an insolent addition of
insult to injury” and the paragraph is correct
about this barbarous survival of kingly tyranny.
The old theory was that the King could keep any-
one in prison if he liked, and that it was only out
of his exceeding great mercy that he let anyone
out. And many, such old trappings hang round
the neck of modern democracy.

A beautiful snake story comes from Vic-
toria, where a married couple, formerly of the
1 )owie persuasion, have been introducing a new
Faith-cure religion on their own. Wandering
around in the name of the Lord they captured a
black snake, and the lady put out her bare arm to
be bitten, for the edification of several bystanders,
who doubted whether the new Faith-cure would
work in a test case. She casually remarked that
Elijah Howie had never been “game” to put his
doctrines to a similar proof. Then comes a break
of two days in the continuity of the yarn. When
next heard of the lady was not dead, but convales-
cent, suffering from swollen arm. She had applied
no antidote except Eaith, she told a policeman,
without attempting to explain why the poison
had lieen able to get going pretty strong before
Faith saved the situation. More is likely to be
heard of the couple. If the lady persists in
gathering black snakes by the wayside there may
be a vacancy for a husband presently. Faith for
the goose is not bound to be Faith for the gander.

Sachse, the Vic. Minister for Education,
complains that the department is put to a serious
annual expense in providing substitutes pro tern.
for the many young women teachers who suffer
from anaemia. The anaemic State-school teacheress
grows more frequent every year—especially in
country districts; and Sachse, as the official
parent of all these pallid spinsters, is seeking the
best medical advice on the subject. Matrimony
—the time-honored cure for anaemia—being quite
out of the question, medical science will be called
upon to suggest some other means of providing a
Timely Tonic for Tired Teachers.

“ Mars ” : As an argument on the sending
of missionaries to China :
Here is a special case with a general moral: In a little
known part of Victoria is a valley shut in by rough hills.
Through this, long ago, a stock route led. Here a couple
named (say) Henderson planted themselves and kept an
accommodation house and grog shanty. They reared a
numerous family. When the stock took an easier road,
the little place was left in forgotten solitude. Too listless
to seek new fields, the Hendersons stayed there. The
fledglings gradually left the nest. Charlotte was long-
headed. She selected 100 acres of the richest flat. Jim,
a horse-breaker, married her. Susan got married—married
in court. Jenny started her family first. Then she got
married —to another fellow. Jenny’s husband could live
for three half-crowns per week, which the other man pro-
vided, so he didn’t see the force of staying single with
double expense. George married for love, and took up a
selection near by. Bill, his brother, went to live with him.
When George and Bill quarrelled, it was the missus who
was to blame. She had such a careless way of forgetting
which mat) was her husband. Tom saved a parson’s fee.
He took charge of another man’s cast-off and lived on a
plot of ground held under a miner’s right. The others
lived in similar styles, ekeing out miserable lives by trap-
ping ’possums and shooting wallabies, rearing tribes of
children to a life of squalor, laziness and vice, with an
object lesson in behavior to copy in the shape of the
grandmother of the settlement, who thus late in life,
separated from her husband and topped up a splendid
record of birth-rate with an infant fathered by a stray
miner. Could not some of our parsons leave China to
others and work here ? There is a broad field.

“ O. Fweddy—my poor Fweddy — there’s a mosquito

“ For goodness sake don’t touch him, Algy; I’ve

The Bulletin is constantly hearing now-
adays of erstwhile friendly families in N.S.W.
which have been utterly estranged by the Dill
Macky insectarianism. Mrs. Dooley used to be
good neighbors with Mrs. McDuff. They would
lend and borrow an occasional bit of tea
or half a loaf of bread, and mingle their
children together in amicable measles when an
epidemic was on so that “ they could all get them
together.” Now they don't play speaks—one
takes a weekly gloat over thrippence worth of
malevolent sectarianism ; the other buys a
penn’orth and gets it just as strong if a little
cruder in expression. And when Mrs. Dooley
passes Mrs. McDuff it is with a sniff, and Mrs.
McDuff expresses publicly her opinion that Mrs.
Dooley is benighted. To interfere with the
freedom of the press is a serious matter, but there
is such a profound public danger in the constant
dissemination of slanders and half-truths calculated
to provoke breaches of the peace that the State
may yet have to face the unpleasant duty of
restraining the organs of religious bigotry
“ Caro ” on flower perfumes and the color
sense:—
Pick a violet at sunset, when all flower scents are the
sweetest. Shut your eyes and scent it slowly. It should
suggest a warm, not bright, blue—a bit of windless sky
over a hot sea. The queen variety approaches nearly to
it, but the deep purples are unfaithful utterly. Saunter
on through the garden walk, and lift that deep crimson,
wine-warm rose, with the petals of crushed velvet. If
you held it burdened with its languid scent before a blind,
most prosaic human, he would tell you it was red or wine-
colored. Bury your face in a bunen of carnations and
you will see a prismatic glory of light, and as their keen
scent penetrates your veins you, if your imagination be
not utterly dead, will become slightly and puzzlingly
cognizant of a hidden and alien sense. Mignonette is the
absinthe of flowers, strange green crystals of scent, sooth-
ing and subtle. The old-fashioned wall-flowers are faithful
too, with their intricate smell of spiced wood, suggestive
of cedars of Lebanon ; they are the only flowers to be
picked at noon. Geraniums, hibiscus, sunflowers, nas-
turtiums, are among- those whose scent is wholly color-
less. Hyacinths are too clamorous ; who could mistake
the clammy white bloom for the stained purple? As for
tuberoses, we shudder, remembering our dead. Then
there are the unfaithful among the elect. Camelias,
cornflowers, poppies, pansies (with the scent of running
water), daisies, and most of the bulbs (flowers of vanity).
There are millions of blooms, with their legions of odors,
and the study is a charming one. But before you forget,
drive down a vista of bush when the wattles are blooming
and it is gold, gold, gold, till your senses and your soul
are surfeited with it.
* » * *
Bunny seems to have been responsible for
nearly all the recent bush fires, and yet the land-
holder has primarily himself to blame; only the land-
holder who is to blame isn’t, unfortunately, always
the same landholder who has to suffer the damage.
Any low-waged noodle is reckoned competent
enough to mix the phosphorised pollard used for
rabbit-poisoning, with the result that it is laid
about the paddocks in a semi-dissolved state, only
awaiting an extra hot day or the tread of a beast
to start it into flame. Some day pastoralists and
farmers will recognise that phosphorus isn’t the
kind of commodity that can be handled by all
sorts of incompetents, and that cheap labor isn’t
an unmixed blessing.
* * * *
Now that Melbourne has at last got its
Queen’s Jubilee Fever Hospital open nobody will
use it. It takes in scarlet fever and diphtheria,
yet, though 40 such cases were reported in Mel-
bourne last month as being treated privately, the
hospital had a staff idly twiddling its thumbs and
brushing the dust off 35 vacant beds.
* * * *
Very quaint is the form of apprenticeship
to the sea. After a rigmarole of preamble, it pro-
vides that during his term the apprentice
His masters faithfully shall serve, their secrets keep,
their lawful commands everywhere gladly do ; he shall do
no damage to his said masters, nor see it done by others,
but to his power shall tell or forthwith give notice to h s
s lid masters of the same ; the goods of his said masters he
shall not waste, nor lend them unlawfully to any ; he
shall neither buy nor sell without his master’s leave ;
taverns, inns, or alehouses he shall not haunt; at cards,
dice tables, or any other unlawful games he shall not
play ; matrimony he shall not contract; nor from the
service of his said masters day or night absent himself ;
but in all things as a faithful apprentice he shall behave
himself towards his said master and all his family during
the said term.
Not many Australians are apprenticed to the sea.
Many that have been were scapegraces who did
their country no credit. So much has this been
so that one line—the best fleet of sailing
vessels that comes here —has absolutely forbidden
its captains to receive Australian boys as appren-
tices. Yet, given a fair show, Australians make
splendid sailors.
Bernard Hall has been affirming in Mel-
bourne that the “original intention of Art is to
decorate,” and the Goths have fallen on him with
scorn. Is not, then, Tragedy Art, asks one ; and
what is there decorative in Tragedy ? Which
shows a complete ignorance of the essential facts
of Art. Tragedy is of all things supremely
decorative. It takes the most sordid and miser-
able things of life and transmutes them into a
poetry which is beautiful and, in a true sense, joy-
bringing. To quote Bertrand Russell: —
Of all the arts, Tragedy is the proudest, the most
triumphant, for it builds its shining citadel in the very
centre of the enemy’s country, on the very summit of his
highest mountain. . . . Within its walls the free life con-
tinues while the legions of Death and Pain and Despair,
and all the servile captains of tyrant Fate, afford the
burghers of that dauntless city new spectacles of beauty.
Tragedy not decorative, Why it can take the
battered corpse from the pavement and make it
“a thing of beauty and a joy for ever.”
* * * *
The Australian railway system could be
improved by the adoption of numbers for railway-
stations. A lot of stations are mere wayside
pulling-up places, but their names are sufficient to
strike terror into a Cossack. A few from the
Westralian railway time-table: Moonyoonoonooka,
Nannowtharra, Tuckannarra, Mooliabeenee, Jen-
naccubbine, Hulongongine, Jarrainonga, Moggo-
j erring, and Munjacobydaring. It’s tiresome to
write Cootamundrajonga when “ Siding 14 ” would
suffice.

The tale of a Chinee cook :
The Chinese cook gave a timid knock, and entered with
a bland, apologetic smile.
“Me likee get off Sunday week, missee—wantee go
Chineeman funalal.”
She looked at him in amazement.
“Nonsense, John! They’d never keep anyone till
Sunday week to be buried.”
“Oli, lat’s all li’,” still smiling affably. “Him not
dead let.”
“John, I’m ashamed of you! Fancy wanting to settle
to go to your friend’s funeral when he isn’t dead.”
“hat’s' all li’,” imperturbably. “Him elite sure to
die —me g'O to his funalal Sunday week ? ”
“ But no one can be sure he’ll die, John. Even doctors
make mistakes sometimes.”
“No chancee make mistake. Him bellied Sunday
week all li’. Him going to be hung Fliday—bellied
Sunday 1 ”

United States Employers’ Liability Assur-
ance Corporation gives the four chief causes of
man’s financial downfall as revealed by the experi-
ence of its operations—(l) Women, (2) specula-
tion, (3) high living, (4) expensive hobbies ; and
notes that the first is responsible for by far the
greatest number of cases. Same old Adam—same
old excuse.
Weigall, headmaster of the Sydney Gram-
mar School is not very complimentary about the
young Australian intellect :
It may be fairly said that such success as the school has
gained, either on the intellectual or the athletic side,
during the past year, has been the result of organisation
and persistent effort, for the material at our disposal has
been singularly deficient in native brilliancy. It has been
noticeable for some time past that the intellectual quality
of those who have been admitted to the school has been
deteriorating.
* * * *
The Vic. birthrate was helped along the
other day by a lady who took advantage of the
facilities of a suburban private hospital. She
paid up all right, and when able to move about a
bit asked that a nurse might be allowed to drive
with her to a certain hotel. Leaving the nurse
with baby in the cab, she entered the private bar
door —and the nurse might be waiting yet if she
hadn’t had sense enough to go back home. Any-
one wanting a cheap month-old baby can be
accommodated with a bargain.
* m * *
Hobart had a shade temperature of 103
one day last week, through a corner of a heat
wave getting across to the little island ; and the
plump and juicy Tasmanians larded the lean earth
as they crawled around. About Xmas time the
Hobart man saw Mt. Wellington snow-covered ;
within less than a month he was suffering Red Sea
weather.
* * * *
Rabbits, K.C.M.Geese, sparrows, Scotch
thistles, briars, and snails do not exhaust the list of
pests imported to Australia from the dear old
Mother country. There is also the British break-
fast, which starts with an oatmeal mash —a stodgy,
mushy mass of boiled paste and bran —and con-
cludes with bacon and eggs. No oil subject of the
Empire considers himself properly British and
respectable for the day until he has swallowed
bacon and eggs. If he went to his office without
them, he’d consider himself naked. In a climate
which is mostly fog when it isn’t snow, bacon and
eggs are admirable, perhaps necessary. They
supply caloric plentifully, and enable the con-
sumer to let off a little steam at the dog which
annoys him, without running the risk of his life
machine being promptly choked by the fog or
frozen dead by the snow. But in a land where
the glass climbs up to lOOdeg. in the summer,
bacon and eggs as a breakfast food are almost
murderous. But they’re British, and that suffices.
Australia has hardly begun to think for itself in
the matter of food.
* * • »
Australia is full of penny-wise, pound-
foolish “economies.” It is found impossible to
provide a State asylum for the destitute illegiti-
mate at birth ; but when the abandoned infant’s
body has been found in a paddock, the State is
forced to give gaol accommodation to the mother.
Keeping a mother in gaol is far more expensive
than keeping an infant in a foundling asylum, and
it doesn’t bring with it the advantage of rearing
up another citizen.
JUST THE THING.
At a Churoh Congress during Melbourne Cup
week, one rev. gent said that deaconesses should
be provided with an attractive uniform. Cer-
tainly a few deaconesses of the right sort, who
know how to look attractive, should lead the
wicked to church, if anything will.
“ Binnacle,” as to the long hours of officers
on Australian coasters :
Have a personal friend on board a cargo boat belonging
to a popular inter-State line. He has been going to New-
castle for many years as chief officer. On duty en route ;
on duty when entering ; on duty when loading ; on duty
on clearing the port, and on duty at sea again. He has
by law to be examined at Newcastle for his pilot’s ticket,
which his co. insists on his taking out, and during all
these many years he has not had the hour to spare to sit
for his exam.
A CHRISTMAS GIFT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
* PENDANT of opal or pearl,
/m A bangle, a locket, a fan,
/ t The things that are dear to the heart
of a girl
That occur to the mind of a man, —
These are gifts for the friends that are nearest,
But crowned with the rose and the rue,
And woven with memory’s dearest,
I have only some verses for you l
You loved what I wrote as a lover,
You loved it because it was mine,
And many a fault was passed over
For the heart-beat that hid in the line.
’Twas for you I attempted the greatest—-
For you turned the best and the worst,
And for YOU I hare fashioned the latest
Whose love bade me fashion the first l
WILL H. OGILVIE.
A REVERSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
God, you, and I,
Above us the sky,
The world at our feet.
God and you flown,
And I, left alone,
Am at the world’s feet.

THE VARNISHED BOARD
