A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 17, 1905.
My Dear Arini.—
Home from the Mountains ! The train-loads of
grimy and dishevelled mountaineers are still
making their way back from Katoomba, where
your back hair caught fire if you went out for a
walk, and even the chilliest iceberg of a girl was
always liable to blaze up and burn as briskly as at
an auto da fe. Several people who heard that Mr.
Mark Foy had been giving away champagne to
the Medlow Bath visitors on particularly festive
days, cheerfully ran the risk of being cremated by
trying to drive over from Katoomba to get some.
Never since Lord Hopetoun gave away whole
cases of fizz to the unemployed has such a genuine
chance occurred. But the bush fires stopped the
thirsty ones, and they had to struggle back
with tongues hanging out and frillies already
smouldering.
Among the hastiest of the fugitives from
Katoomba was papa Cooper, with Mrs. Cooper
and their six charming Cooperlets, from Victoria.
Mrs. Cooper is a sister of Mrs. Malcolm
McEacharn, who is at present enjoying blizzards
and things, in Scotland. Papa Cooper was a
politician in this State about 50 years ago, and
went to Victoria for his well-dowered missus.
The six little Cooperkins, all made after the same
pattern, are of different sizes and capacities. The
younger ones are neat little barrels of varying
rotundity. The larger are handsome and imposing
hogsheads, so to speak. A man who rears six
daughters in these bad times, and takes them all
for a holiday at the same time, and then drops
into a bush fire, and only escapes with his beard
singed and his troupe smoking, deserves a special
vote of sympathy.
Miss Eva Bryan, who is confessedly, I think,
one of the classically beautiful women of Sydney,
was quietly married the other day. The lucky man
is Dr. Otto Wunderlich, a personable-looking
bridegroom in the early thirties, who doesn’t
seem to have much that’s foreign about him but
his name and the way he raises his hat. Foreigners
always score in that item. Miss Bryan, who
had some means of her own, was by way of being
musical, and her frocking was generally perfect.
For some years Sydney expected her to marry a
belted earl, or some such ; but she’s done better,
probably.
More swimming baths for women are badly
wanted. Present arrangement is that when, on a
day, a frizzled female feels that she requires a dip,
and tramps a mile or two to the baths, it is the day
for “men only.” The women’s day was yesterday
or last week. Here and there a swimming bath is
reserved altogether for women, and on a hot
Saturday it is as crowded as a revival meeting and
just about as comfortable. Half the time you are
bathing in water with a distinctly feminine flavor
about it, the rest of the time you are entangled in
the other woman’s damp hair. At one of these
places, the other day, I saw an infuriated slie-
bather giving the male boss of the establishment
the rough edge of a particularly rasping tongue.
Oblivious of appearances, she stood on the plat-
form in a dripping, skin-tight swimming suit and
threw eloquence at the case-hardened proprietor,
whom she had deliberately dragged from his den
that he might be her target.
Her complaint was that while she was in the
water, six adult she-bathers and eight children had
invaded her dressing bunk and scattered her gar-
ments on the floor. It was an appalling lapsus
lingerie. Then they had hung up their own
dubious raiment on the rusty nails, and declined
to retire when requested. The boss could only
mutter apologetically that he was having some
more boxes built, and that they would be ready in
a year or two. Saw the aggrieved one later on
when she was dressed. She was still boiling. Her
new gloves and one or two other unspecified
articles had disappeared in the scuffle.
There is a quiet-looking little professor of the
occult who has a large feminine following out
Glebe way. At a recent seance on a sultry after-
noon at least a score of goggle-eyed girls were
present, and nary a male thing among them except
the professor. He began proceedings by going
into a fit and prophesying in a high, shrill
voice. According to this authority, the Bear
and Monkey war will extend until all the
nations of the world are involved, and will
last for seven years. It will be the greatest
war in history, and when it is over the population
of the world will be one-tenth man and nine-tenths
woman. There aren’t nearly enough husbands to
go round as it is, and when there are nine women
to every man, the unfortunate male creatures will
Erobably be torn to pieces in the scuffle for a hus-
and, and thus man will finally disappear from
the planet. The Glebe soothsayer quoted
Hebrew prophets in support of his alarming state-
ments. The domesticated Christian woman who is
on the look-out for a “ respectable gentleman with
view to above ” had better double the.size of her
advertisement, or she may miss the ’bus. A
rival mystic, who is at present practising in
Sydney, is a full-blooded African' negro. His
specialty is going into a trance and talking in
what is said to be modern Greek. The King-
street oyster people have not as yet bowled him
out as an impostor.
Up at Ulmarra the bubonic rat has poked
his baleful nose into love’s young dream with
ghastly consequences in two eases. In one, a lover
calling on his beloved caught the plague from
her and died ; in the other, a girl who per-
sisted in visiting her lover when he was a
victim to the disease, succumbed to the contagion.
Wonder whether the average married man
or married woman would rise to the height of
calling on the life-partner in a plague-ward.
Some would, of course. But the average
man, who is frenzied at the thought that
his fiancee may get her feet damp when they are
both caught in the usual picnic thunderstorm, is
oppressed by very different fears when his wife
doesn’t know enough to come in out of the rain.
The difference that marriage makes in the situation
is that it causes him to grumble aggravatingly
over the prospect of an increased dressmaker’s
bill.
The “town buyer ” is the latest development of
feminine commercial enterprise. She makes pur-
chases for the bush women who cannot comedown
to town themselves, and she will buy anything,
from a motor-car to a tube of tooth-paste, for her
clients. The flustered bush lady who requires a
new hat can avoid a lot of anxiety, and procure a
thrill of pleasurable excitement as well, by leaving
the job of getting it to an alert young woman
about town. Chiffons or rat poison, a new draw-
ing-room carpet, or a pair of indestructible
pants for Tommy—it’s all the same to the
“town buyer.” She charges nothing for the
job, and contents, herself with the commission
fjsom the shops.’ Among the latest I notice to
take up this calling is Miss R. Venn Brown, of
Wynyard Square, who qualified by going through
the routine of her dad’s office—an indenting and
importing warehouse. The occupation snould
solve the problem of existence for many a young
woman, once the advantage of having your shop-
ping done for you is appreciated.
A forthcoming wedding of interest will be that
of the “ Little Boy from Manly,” whom contem-
porary Australian caricature has made in a mea-
sure notable. When the Soudan contingent was
dispatched from N.S. Wales there appeared
among the daily paper subscription lists, to buy
jam and so on for the troops, the item, “A Little
Boy from Manly, £5.” “Hop” seized on the line,
and thereafter “the Little Boy from Manly” has
typified N.S. Wales in his cartoons. The original
of the little boy was Ernest Laurence, son of Mr.
Charles Laurence, the well-known solicitor. That
was long years ago, and “the little boy from
Manly ” has now grown up to manly estate, and is
to be married this month to Miss Belle Brown,
grand-daughter of the late Alexander Dean, of
Manly.
Concerning that recent Enoch Arden-like
tragedy in England, in which a lady, a
former husband who had been in Austra-
lia, and an overdose of laudanum figure—
some years ago a woman in a neighboring
State was surprised by a letter from the hus-
band she had long considered happily dead
and securely buried in Canada. She had, acting
on that belief, married a shopkeeper, and they had
three young children, but Sandy wrote that she’d
have to return to him ; if not —well, he’d be the
terror that stalked at moontide and the some-
thing that flieth by night. Besides he had a
nice, wee bit farm in Canada. Wife was
a strong, attractive middle-aged woman. But
it is not difficult for a middle aged woman
to make herself look frightsome. Besides, she
knew how thrifty was the unwelcome re-
turner. At Brisbane, a joyous letter from Jesse
awaited Sandy, and at Circular Quay she cast her-
self upon his breast, dropping the sticks upon
which she leaned. She was toothless, her hair
was plastered back, and three ragged children
whimpered at her side. “ They’ll be so
useful on the farm,” she mumbled, “ when they
are grown. I wouldn’t dream of going without
them.” The horrified claimant gave her 55.,
and a sixpence apiece to the children, and pro-
mised fervently to call at the address she gave
him. Months afterwards she knew he had re-
turned to Canada, because he sent her a pound
from there, but without letter or address.
Miss Mildred Coffee, eldest daughter of Mr.
Frank Coffee, of Iroquois, Chatswood, whose
engagement to Dr. Kelly, a North Sydney
physician, is announced, is a very American girl—
clearly colorless as to complexion, black as to the
pile of hair, brushed up in a billow same as the
Gibson girl’s. She is a good violinist. The pater-
nal Coffee is an American merchant in Sydney.
As for the bridegroom elect, report says he is one
of “ the coming men ” in his profession.
Mr. Octavius Beale, whose correspondence with
Mr. “Orchid” Chamberlain has caused more or
less interest, sets, on the domestic side, a good
example. He has 10 (unusually handsome) chil-
dren. Two of the girls are by way of being
literary and artistic.
Little Nina Caron, who appears as a midship-
man’'in “Pinafore,” is conductor Leon Caron’s
only child. Madame Caron is a Tasmanian, and
was a singer of some reputation.
Sixth daughter of Archdeacon Hose, of Corowa,
is marrying, some time this month, a son of Mr.
G. F. Sugden, of Woodsome Lees, Tocumwal (on
the Murray). Sugden, senior, besides being- the
wise owner of silly sheep, is a wool-scourer in a
biggish way. To the passing stranger he is a per-
fect mine of reminiscence. His homestead, a
quaint “ bush ” house, has often been the “story”
place of an Australian tale, and his recitals about
bushrangers and blacks have been used partly or
wholly, in many a romance. His eldest daughter
is married to the manager of Boomanoomana
. (Hay’s), a “show station ” of the Riverina.
Ida Osborne (Mrs. Norcott) was in Sydney last
week. Her prominent contralto share in first
productions of Gilbert and Sullivan’s operas in
Australia is gratefully remembered.
Mrs'.'Charles Parkinson and Miss Amy Smith,
of Mojsman, are trying to move the women of that
suburb to practical sympathy with the bush-fire
sufferers. There will be a meeting to-day (Thurs-
day), when some means of raising money-will be
devised. Hon. Secs, are the two ladies mentioned.
A writer in an American paper discussing
stage beauties at the famous Horse Show in
New York: “Mrs. Patrick Campbell and Hilda
Spong have the finest necks and shoulders on the
stage.”
Sydney Mayoress Mrs. Allen Taylor will hold
her first public reception at the Town Hall on
Thursday, the 26th inst., from 3 to 5. A really
good “ show” is to be expected, for arrangements
are being made on such a lavish scale as will give
all previous mayoresses a pain in the egotism.
The nocturnal wedding is still fashionable.
Two young things—Mr. Ashley Campbell and
Miss Mabel Houston—were married a few nights
ago at Waverley in the presence of about 150
people, all in ball costume. The women looked
quaint in low-cut frocks and picture hats. They
had to wear their hats because St. Paul, or some-
body, says that women must have their heads covered
in church, and the minister backed up that rather
musty authority. After the knot-tying a reception
and sit-down supper at Woodlawn, Waverley, home
of the bride’s parents, continued the illusion that
a State ball was in progress, and the two princi-
pals departed in a carriage and pair somewhere
about midnight for parts unknown, leaving the
young hloods still making speeches. Heaps of
pretty girls on view, “plump and pleasing per-
sons,” as Sir Joseph Porter says. Among the
layer on top of the basket a conscientious judge
would put Miss Muriel Carter and Miss Hume, of
Burrowa—where the bush-fires come from. The
institution of the nocturnal wedding is very handy
for the guests. They don’t have to get new
clothes. Every potential male wedding guest
is supposed to have a dress suit, until he pawns it,
and every woman can wear her theatre frock and
a few dabs of pearl powder.
Governor Gerald Strickland, of the Flyspeck,
and Lady Edelino have started on an adventurous
voyage to the North-West Coast, where Lady
Edeline will see the biggest potatoes she ever saw
in her life if she gets as far as Burnie. Mrs.
Willie Jamieson, spouse of the Broken-Hillionaire,
has a summer house there, and goes over to
get cool whenever the heat-waves on our side of
the straits become unendurable. Her cottage,
fronting the sea breezes off Emu Bay, is one of the
most comfortable places in the Commonwealth
what time Mr. Hunt, of the Observatory, is reading
up in order to wrestle with the mercury in this
perspiring city. Lady Edeline —it sounds like the
trade name of a new hair wash, doesn't it! —will
hardly leave the North-West Coast without going
to Macquarie Harbor where Rufus Dawes’ ghost
still gibbers o’ nights. It is to be hoped that
she will have better luck on her Tasmanian cruise
than the Brasseys in the same waters. Old man
Brassey dropped his hat-box overboard, and in
grabbing to recover it ran the Sunbeam ashore
and nearly drowned “Sibby.”
It is printed in several London society papers
that when the late Queen lay dying, one devoted
princess was occupying the time by annexing all
the portable property available. It came to the
present King’s knowledge, and he very rudely
compelled her to-disgorge.
At a recent Christmas treat to ragged young-
sters at Dawes’ Point every kid was presented
with a text and a Union Jack. Fancy the joy of
the poor little dears. Now they’ll have a flag to
wave next time a Duke or anything visits Aus-
tralia. And before the texts every night they
can pray for their betters.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The two vice-regal houses have their blinds
drawn for the collection of the midsummer moth
and the indoor spider. The Northcotes tried
Colac, sniffed a smoky atmosphere for a stoical
period, then dashed on to Warrnambool for a
whiff of the salt breeze. The Talbots are at
Macedon, in the midst of bush blazes. The Grey
Dame never had seen the earth on fire, and was
eager for the sight. She satisfied her wishes,
putting up with a temperature of 120° in the
shade.
“Just as this blistered State was slowly arriving
at the conclusion that a long, droopy, unconcerned
Governor was an extravagant and ridiculous excess
and his body-guard a howling waste, the announce-
ment booms out in the daily press that his Ex. has
appointed an extra A.U.C. What for ? There is
a private sec. to drop a line on business affairs
once or twice in the year ; a whole aide to fan the
flies off the pink dome of his chief in the summer
and turn on the heating apparatus in the winter.
Then why the extra Jack-in-the-box ?
“ The very latest is that the wheels of a big poli-
tician’s matrimonial project have come to a com-
plete standstill, owing to the lady’s parents having
refused their sanction to the marriage until the gay
and frisky one can furnish satisfactory evidence
that he has arrived at years of discretion.
“Woman—ever worried by the thought that
she doesn’t wear so well as the other sex—finds
the double chin a woe. All flesh is gross when it
becomes over-abundant, and there is no more cer-
tain sign of age than fat, which proclaims forty —
or more. A beauty-doctor has devised a chin-
apparatus which the patient with a double chin
dons at night in company with her angel-case, and
wakes in the morning with the consciousness of a
dwindling neck. The strap drawn taut under the
jaws keeps the lips locked, and through the
night hours Woman must remain silent, and
Caudle—if there is a Caudle in the case—is pre-
served from curt’ain lectures. A leading Aus-
tralian actress is said to be wearing a ‘ chineen,’
and many of the buxom sisterhood are following
her lead.
“The summer girl this year pins her faith and
her back hair to the baby-frill hat—the large,
shady sort which flops with the wind and flirts
with her male escort, so to speak. It conceals her
freckle-crop, her tan and other sun blemishes.
But the mistakes and the awful disillusions
brought about by the flopping sun-hat cause many
a tragedy for the seaside Johnny. A chic
figure is observed, and a crisp gait to correspond.
A baby hat is the crowning charm. He swungs
along on the track of perfection and finds—
an old woman. In my experience, the older
the ‘girl’ the giddier her sun-bonnet, until,
as worn at 60, it is of a most pronounced nursery
type.
“Talking of scribes! An enterprising she-
reporter besought Tummy Bent and all his Minis-
try, together and in turn, to plant her in Pentridge
for a term on the charge of being an ink nuisance
or a moralist, or something equally bad. Falstaff
Bent blew his cheeks out, distended his
nostrils, thrust his specks on his bump of
obstinacy, and generally behaved like a startled and
flabbergasted fat man, but at last gave permission
for the persistent inquirer to pay a lengthy visit
to the Stockade. She did, and writes of gaol life
from the visitor’s, and not the inmate’s, point of
view. She also begged for a trip in Black
Maria, but Commissioner O’Callaghan snorted
violently, and refused- point blank.
“Recent photographs of Carrie Moore bear tes-
timony to the change wrought in the pert mum-
mer by her London experience. She appears
much older, much sedater, much smarter in style,
and much more extravagant in dress. A three-
quarter coat, of the handsomest Russian sable,
such as the Princess Olga Ivumantevisky always
wears in the diplomatic novel, points to tfie
fact that Carrie, who spent little on her clothes
heretofore, has awakened to the importance
in the Big Smoke of being opulently dressed.
That coat represents a small fortune. Two other
successful Melbourne footlighters— Alice Crawford
and Violet Elliott—have all along spent some
thought as well as lucre on their clothes, and been
conscientious in attending to the good points of
their figures.
“Miss Henry, of the Australasian staff, for a
long while a chronicler of the Creche and Home
for Desolate Mothers in the press here and occa-
sionally abroad, has to start off on a health jaunt.
She has been granted half-a-year’s holiday. Sister
scribes are raising a testimonial to recognise the
services of the lady who has grown grey on the
Inky Way.
“ Mrs. Forbes-Semple has been leading charmer
of the Hobart cake-walk for the past month. At
the matrons’ hop she appeared as the one rare
beauty in autumn brown a shimmering soft array
of chiffons, and tulle of the same hue in her
chablis-toned tresses. Miss Rita Kiddle has also
flounced about with some effect at smart Hobart
sprees.
“The Edward Trencliards start off to Europe
by the India. They touch at all the ports and big
centres possible within a limited period of six
months. The wealthy Mrs. Tom Smith, with her
one remaining ewe-lamb, the petite Edith, packs
away her glass and chinaware and stores her
household valuables for an indefinite period, hav-
ing decided to go to Europe, and, in passing, look
in at her two married daughters by way of a
New Year greeting. For the wealthy widow with
but one small responsibility, gadding is the thing.
“The Talbots loaded the M‘Eacharns with
pasteboards to exalted London friends when the
latter were leaving, and at present the ex-Mayor
of shining respectability is nobbing with some rare
old guns. Malcolm himself, among other things,
has become addicted to motoring, and whirls in
the best machine that his pocket can command.
‘ England’s good enough for me,’ he tells the Vic.
globe-trotter.
“Recent visitors to Tasmania met a honey-
mooning couple who called up memories of
Hoggenheimer of Parle Lane and his bride, the
Girl from Kay’s. Hubby, a fat, financial,
elderly foreigner, went out of his way to assure
new acquaintances that his pretty young partner
was ‘ very fond ’of him. In proof of this asser-
tion, he made a practice of chucking his property
under the chin in public, with the comment ‘Not
a bad-looking liddle girl, eh ? ’ The victim smiled
through thick and thin ; but there are smiles and
smiles.
“ Touring Britishers are a trial! One of them
testified, t’other day, that -she enjoyed the local
production of ‘Sweet Nell’ because she had not
seen it ‘at Home.’ But, of course, she couldn’t
enjoy ‘ Old Heidelberg ’ after having beheld ‘ a
gairl at Home ’do it. She didn’t even remember
the ‘ gairl’s’ name, but she was ‘ at Home,’ so, of
course, must be superior to Nellie Stewart.
“The plaintive sob that is wrung from the
Loongana turbine machinery has to be accounted
for to each fresh batch of tourists. The patient
stewardess doesn’t even smile now when she is
asked at nights if ‘ that poor creature is still suf-
fering,’ and she has to respect the inquiry of
Riverina people who want to know the amount of
live stock aboard.
“Miss Alexia Bassian is warmly enthusiastic
over Marchesi. ‘ Her age! Why, no one who knows
Marchesi thinks of her years. She is Marchesi.
That answers all things—her wit, her spirit, her
staying powers, her incapacity for fatigue. The
old Marquis—now he is old. We all call him the
Marquis, and he is a marvel in a way. He gets
to like certain pupils and dislike others, and he
teases his favorites and worries Madame into a
shrill exclamation of petulance. Then it is over,
and she laughs.’ Miss Bassian corresponds regu-
larly with the venerable voice-trimmer, and con-
fesses to liking her mightily.

HEARTY APPETITES.
There is nothing can give you an appetite like Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. The very first box will
make you hungry. Remember, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are not like common patent medicines. They
don’t act on the bowels. They don’t bother with mere symptoms. They do only one thing, but
they do it well—they actually male new blood. In that way they strike at the very root and
cause of all stomach troubles.
« p or two years I never enjoyed a decent meal,” said Mrs. Frank Musgrave, 10 Little Belmore-street,
Surry Hills, Sydney. “ I was almost at death’s door with Indigestion. My appetite was completely gone.
I hated to think of meal time. I would no sooner swallow my food than I would start to vomit. A piercing
pain seemed to catch me at the end of the breast-bone. Every day I grew thinner and weaker. I could not
sleep, and got up tired. I hadn’t the strength to do my house-work. My face had no color. I grew worse
and worse, till a friend made me get some Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. The first box or two made me feel
hungry. When I got through three boxes, I was able to eat what I liked. I had no more headaches and no
more bilious attacks. I got a splendid color, and gained weight every day. After seven boxes, my
health could not have been better. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills worked wonders for me.”

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The Seddon-Taylor (Maoriland) alleged
slander action is to be tried over again, the
plaintiff having applied for a new trial and a
change of venue.
Colonel Murray, the Commonwealth judi-
cial officer in New Guinea, has a brother, Gilbert,
a professor in Glasgow (Scotland). He is a notable
Greek scholar and something of a playwright.
George Dean, the central figure in one of
the most exciting criminal cases of a century, now
lives quietly in a Sydney suburb. He has resumed
his old occupation of ferry-boat master, and finds
employment as a casual hand with one of the ferry
companies. The long term of gaol left its mark
on Dean, but he is still a fairly young-looking man.
A Governor’s private sec. in one of the
Australian cities, the other day, told a reporter to
“go to blanky ,” which fact suggest the need
of revising one current notion of natural his-
tory. Governor’s private secretaries in Australia
have always been looked upon as pink-eyed rab-
bits, without the shadow of a cuss in them.
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley was sworn
in on December 7, 1886, and has thus entered on
liis nineteenth year of office. In staying capacity
lie ranks next to Alfred Stephen in N.S.W.
Forbes held office nearly 14, Martin nearly 13,
and Dowling seven years.
N.S.W. Justice G. B. Simpson, who has
just gone on a holiday trip, began life in a com-
mercial capacity in the office of George Alfred
Lloyd, a general commission agent and auctioneer.
In the office at the same time were Fred Lassetter,
Josiah Mullins, and Sam Dickinson.
An octogenarian hotelkeeper, of West
Melbourne, came to a highly appropriate, almost
poetic, end the other day. He locked up the bar
at 1 o’clock on a Sunday morning, and laid him
down upon a couch in the bar parlor to sleep, per-
chanoe to dream, of his Saturday night’s takings.
Then the Angel of Death stole gently into the bar
parlor, and the form of the aged sluinberer was as
an empty bottle.
HOPE WAS LOST.
CYRIL (gloomily): “Yes, Maud, my girl , I know
it is a vile habit. I wish I could give it up
But it’s too late. I am a slave to tobacco.”
Thorold Waters, known in Australia as a
journalist, who is seeking the bubble reputation
as a singer in England, has been appointed prin-
cipal tenor to the Benedictine choir, Ealing,
London.
L. A. Bernays, who wears some small tag
to his name, has been Clerk of the Queensland
Assembly ever since it was born, 44 years ago.
During that time there have been nine Speakers
and 22 Ministries.
It was proposed to engrave the names of
all Cohentingenters who went to South Africa on
the soldiers’ memorial to be erected at Orange, but
an objector pointed out that two or three had been
looked up for horse-stealing since their return.
H. H. Champion states that “Robbery
Under Arms ” when first published in a N.S.W.
weekly excited little remark. Not a fact; the
appearance of Rolf Boldrewood’s book in the
Svdney Mail, in ’79 or thereabouts, literally set
the bush afire. It was talked about in every pub.
and at every camp-fire.
Back in S.A., after a 15 years absence,
Hermann Brauer, professor of languages in the
University of Wisconsin (U.S.A.). Brauer was a
schoolboy at Mt. Torrens, in the hills behind
Adelaide, and is reminding the natives how he
used to crack stones on the high-road, after school
hours, to earn the necessaries of life.
The hardy bride of the early days. Joshua
Hatch, an old S.A. colonist who died at Nuriootpa
recently, was married at Kapunda in ’52, his wife
having just been driven from Adelaide in a bullock
dray—a GO-miles trip. After the ceremoiiy the
couple started their honeymoon by walking to
Nuriootpa, 15 miles or so, and never left the place
again.
W. N. Willis, N.S.W. ex-M.L.A., is said
to have an agent selecting land for him in Mada-
gascar.
Died in Adelaide, on January 9th, Charles
Williams, veteran printer, aged 84. Deceased
made a special study of music typesetting, and
was engaged to set upJohn Bishop’s arrangement
of the “Messiah,” “The Creation,” “Judas
Maccabeus,” and other oratorios. He was a mem-
ber of the Anti-Corn-Law League, and took part
in the demonstrations of Cobden, Bright, and
Fox. Williams came to S. Australia in ’5B, and
worked as a printer in various offices. He had
lately completed a small work on Dickens.
A suburban ex-M.L.A., who went down at
last N.S.W. election, has resumed his local hustle
for a living, and has built a modest lean-to shed,
and daubed up his name, and an intimation
that he will do anything that anyone is too tired
to do for himself. Next door to his humpy is the
local town hall, an imposing edifice on which the
ex-M.L. A.’s name is emblazoned in gold lettering.
It somehow suggests a dethroned king running a
stall outside the palace that used to be his,
and trying to sell 24 bananas for 3d. to his suc-
cessor.
Just 21 years ago it occurred to a South
Australian brewer, Charles Mallam, that
he was an old man at last, and ought to
have a bit of sympathy for the other old
fellows in the Home for Incurables ; so he
sent them round a lialf-lmrrel of beer,
and said to himself that he’d keep it up
every week as long as he lasted. He is
still lasting—though he is close up to 90
now—and he is still keeping it up.
Recently deceased Jas. Rice was the first
discoverer of gold in the Walhalla (Gippsland)
district.
Up north in W.A. dwells a descendant of
one of the Six Families who practically lives
amongst the blacks. To see him shifting camp is
Jiathetic ; a huge, unshorn man sitting in a buggy
anything on wheels is called a buggy in the West)
with a bevy of black women and a crowd of chil-
dren. His very highly-placed brother once nearly
got him made a J.P., but there was an outcry, and
the thing didn’t go through.
Malcolm Reid, the Adelaide timber mer-
chant, who once made an unsuccessful attempt to
become Mayor, returned from the Transvaal the
other day. Reid bought land there, and the spec,
proved good. He says Transvaal farming was
shattered through the war, and advises Australia,
which helped in the shattering, to send produce
and fill its pockets before the Boer whose farm
was burnt is able to make up lost ground.
In the same week the Melbourne papers
announced that Father Dalton, the Sydney Jesuit,
was dead, that the Rev. H. N. Wollaston was in a
critical state, and that Trinity (Anglican) Church,
East Melbourne, had been burned down. For
nearly 33 years Wollaston was rector of Holy
Trinity, from which he retired 15 years ago; and
30 years ago, Dalton and Wollaston figured in the
then famous case of “the Bates children.” A
woman died in the Melbourne lying-in hospital.
Before she died she sent for Father D., and asked
him to look after her three illegitimate children.
The next day Father D. went to take possession,
but was repulsed by a bulldog. The Jesuit got
the aid of a policeman, the dog was overpowered,
and the children borne off. Rev. H. N. Wollaston,
the day after, published a letter in the Argus,
reporting how the children had been kidnapped
by a Jesuit and a Roman policeman, and the
Protestant father robbed of his own flesh and
blood to be brought up in Papistry. Court pro-
ceedings followed. The Jesuit came out a winner.
About an almost snuffed-out prophet:—
Te Whiti, the prophet, priest, and king of the Taranaki
Maoris, still holds the fort at Parihaka, the big native
town under the shadow of snow-capped Egmont. Te
Whiti is a tame fanatic—a mild-looking old fellow, with a
straggly grey beard and a set of false teeth. He is as
diligent a student and expounder of the Bible as any
pakeha sky-pilot; his favorite texts are taken from Reve-
lations, wherein he sees special references to the Maori
people. Parihaka is one of the “ sights ”of Taranaki pro-
vince—a well-built town, with a drainage-system and a
good water-supply, carried out by European work-
men. None but Maoris are allowed to live in
the settlement, but pakeha visitors are welcomed
and are hospitably entertained in Te Whiti’s large
house—the only Maori house in which writer ever
saw a bathroom. Hospitality is one of the primal
virtues of the Maori—and Te Whiti prides himself
on being a much greater man in his way than
the King of England, for, says he, “/have 2000 panni-
kins ” (for the Maoris and white men who gather there on
feast days). “How many has King Edward got?”
Although many European innovations are accepted by
the Te Whiti-ites, they will tolerate no pakeha schools.
The only education ailowed in the town-bounds is in the
poi-dance, which, with its accompanying chants, is almost
• sacred ceremony in Parihaka. The old “poropiti ” may
be seen day after day sitting on his verandah watching
the training of the young girls in the movements of the
poi. The music of the chant is very wild and high—quite
unlike that of the Rotorua poi-women. The “libretto”
chiefly consists of Te Whiti’s oracular utterances done into
poetry ; the refrain of every song is a declaration that the
Europeans will soon be driven into the sea, and the
Maori come to his own again; “and overall is Peace—
Peace”—typified by the white plumes of the albatross
which the dancers wear in their hair. The Parihaka
people are intensely anti-Government. The old war and
the old land troubles are not forgotten.
THE RETORT COURTEOUS.
CANVASSER BORE : “ Excuse my persistency in
calling, sir!”
THE BOSS : “ Excuse my persistency in not giving

Mr. Justice A’Beckett has reported on the
Sinclair allegations against Taverner, once Vic.
Minister for Agriculture, now Victorian Agent-
General. Victoria, which has lately had a surfeit
of found-out politician, breathes a sigh of relief
because A’Beckett doesn’t report, on the facts,
very adversely to Taverner. The Judge allows
himself to draw no conclusions or deductions he
merely sifts out the facts, and, whilst declaring
against Mr. Sinclair on several points, still cer-
tifies to enough to show that Mr. Taverner was an
appropriate colleague for Mr. Bent.
Well-known S.A. squatter John Melrose,
who lost his eyesight a year or so back, now
manages his affairs quite as actively as he
ever did, and writes his letters better than
most squatters with two whole eyes to guide
them. Some body recently invented for him
a little ar*rangement to facilitate the signing
of cheques. There is something peculiar
about a man inventing an arrangement for
a blind man to decrease his banking ac-
count with.
Stuart Alexander Donaldson, grand-
father of Brisbane’s new Anglican Bishop,
was only about two months and a half Pre-
mier of N.S.W., and about two years alto-
gether in Australian politics. He was of
the mercantile mind, and beyond fighting
Charlie Cowper, cared little about national
affairs. It was because Governor Denison
could not induce Deas under-
take the first Government that Donaldson,
the makeshift, was called in.
Resident at Neutral Bay, Sydney,
is a medical practitioner, admitted in 1871,
who is also a barrister-at-law admitted in
1884. Dr. H. B. Mugliston, the medico-
barrister in question, was a Member of the
Tasmanian Legislature for some years. Dr.
Todd, ex-Deputy City Coroner, is the only
other in N.S.W. who holds the dual quali-
fication.
In Launceston is a Hindu laundry-
man whose signboard invites customers to
“support British industry” as against the Chinese
Dead, John Plimmer, “ Father of Welling-
ton” (M.L.), a pioneer of some note. He had
been 92£ years on this earth, and 63 years in
windy Wellington. During that time he was only
known to have absented himself once—for a week
—from the Cook Straits port. He originated the
Wellington and Manawatu railway (between
Wellington and Longburn), a wonderfully difficult
bit of engineering, upon which the Government
has long looked covetously. When a young man
he was engaged on the job of building the first
State school in England, at a time when that
country was shivering at the name of Napoleon.
Actor Harry Plimmer, a grandson, was in
Wellington at the time of death, playing in “ The
Eternal City.”

largely on religious questions, re, S?J. e< J
to 1876. Samuel Way, a devout Methodist has
filled in the last 28 years, and is even now only
68 years old.
In politics and other slow-moving,
comparatively harmless occupations the
physically big man with a bulls voice
seems to be a necessary institution.
His physical impregnability and Irigtit-
ful bellow serve to clear his life s
track very early and enable him to
rough-ride his way to leadership.
But for the job of quickly and
vigorously snatching a nation
out of a great difficulty, he is
not in it with that rare crea-
tion, the 10-stone human bull-
ant. Caesar, Napoleon,
Nelson, Richelieu, Dewey,
Roberts and Kuropatxin
represent the type.
Senator Staniforth
Smith, who has now taken
Oceania as his province,
left a record of practical
municipal Socialism behind
him in W.A. It was Smith
who broke up the fruit ring
in Kalgoorlie a few years
back. One firm had a vir-
tual monopoly of th e banana
business, and its prices
were taller than the poppet
heads at Boulder Block.
Smith was Mayor of Kal-
goorlie at the time, and
founded a municipal market,
which brought prices down
with a run. This fruit market
has paid interest from the start,
and has also provided for a sub-
stantial sinking fund. Staniforth Smith likewise
started the W.A. Children’s Presh Air League,
which sends the goldfields youngster to the sea for
a spell in summer.
There are more vicissitudes in politics
than in any other calling on earth. Who remem-
bers Joe Winter nowadays ? Winter was a v ic-
torian Laborite, and held Albert Park when he
died in 1895. Many nominations were made for
Winter’s seat, and some of the candidates who
were small-bore individuals then are large-bore
persons nowadays, and vice-versa. J. B. Tucker
was nominated in the Labor interest, and won.
Tom Bent got 49 votes, and lost his deposit. Dr.
Hall Owen, Mayor of S. Melbourne, put up a
good score. Sam Mauger and H. H. Champion
polled fairly well, and a party named Daglish lost
his £5O along with Tommy Bent. Now Bent is
Premier of Victoria, and Daglish fills a similar
billet in W.A. Tucker is an outsider, and Sam
Mauger holds a safe seat in the Federal Parlia-
ment.

OLD SOL : “ Dern my taters if he isn’t apologising
for me!”
No mistake about the way in which
“Larry” Rentoul, that fiery little Ulsterman,
dominates the Melbourne Presbyterians. All
Victoria’s theological students pass through his
hands, and the forceful, brainy little professor
grains a hold on their minds that they never shake
off. Then, when they enter the Assembly, they
yield like boys to all the arts of oratory that the
old man possesses. Rentoul confesses that his
youthful dream was to enter public life in England.
He would have done well in politics.

Aboriginalities.
In a Westralian village, recently, the local
J.P. sentenced a gin to six months' hard labor, in
Geraldton gaol, for “indecent exposure”—the
irony of it ! The ordinary Westralian gin wears
a few rags as covering, or a piece of calico made
into a cross between a nightdress and a sack. This
poor wretch actually did four months’ solitary
confinement, and from morning till night her
mournful wails could be heard in the town. Think
of sentencing the aboriginal who has on all the
clothes she possesses to six months for insufficient
wardrobe !

“T.F.M.” writes on the Burke and Wills
expedition :
The episode of Johnny Fawkner, resurrected by “ Old
Chum ” (8., 29/12/’O4), contains much of the naked verity.
Burke’s appointment was simply a smellful political job.
His qualifications for the leadership of such an enterprise
were absolutely nil. The only colonial experience he
possessed was seven years’ service as Inspector of Vic.
Police, into which billet he had been pitchforked on his
arrival from Ireland, without the formality of passing
through the ranks. He was no bushman,' for he had
never camped out in his life, and until he set out upon
the trip which was brought to such a mournful and in-
glorious finale at Cooper’s Creek, had seldom been off a
main road. He knew just as much of the sciences as the
average policeman knows to-day ; he could neither map
out his course, nor write an intelligible account of his
journey ; and he showed such a lack of skill in handling
men that his party was hopelessly demoralised long before
it crossed the Murray. Burke was never suited to be an
explorer, and the committee which placed the lives of so
many men in the keeping of such a leader should have
been indicted for manslaughter.
It was tolerably well known in Melbourne long before
the expedition started that, although there were plenty
of capable and experienced men available, an elaborate
system of underground engineering was at work on
Burke’s behalf, the engineer-in-chief being John
O Shanassy, who was at that time the most prominent
political entity in Victoria. With its lavishly equipped
train, the expedition should have proved, under proper
guidance, a kind of Cook’s Excursion across the .Conti-
nent ; but by Burke’s ignorance and obstinacy he brought
about not only his own death, but also involved seven of
his subordinates in a like fate, while the scientific and
practical results were of the most meagre character.
No ! Burke was not an explorer ; neither was he a hero.
That flying trip from Cooper’s Creek to the Gulf was
more like an act of insanity than the carefully-weighed
conception of a man of dauntless courage, tempered with
judgment. The ease with which a capable man like
Alfred Howitt accomplished, with small and scantily-
equipped parties, all he was asked to do, showed what
might have been done by the lavishly-furnished Burke
and Wills expedition but for the woeful shortcomings of
its leader.

A Bananaland man, who had read some-
thing about intense heat being generated by the
sun’s rays reflected by mirrors, tried the experi-
ment, and found that the prickly pear pest so
treated shrivelled up to a pulp in a few minutes.
An experiment is to be tried on a large scale.
The white man is a duffer when it comes to
identifying a Chow. The Chow is equally unre-
liable when he has to pick a white from a few
others. A man spent ninepence in fruit t’other
day at Wong Yik’s, and tendered a florin. Wong,
who had just heard of the fall of Port Arthur,
gave three shillings change—in his excitement.
The man got on to a tram with the surplus. Then
Wong discovered his mistake. With clenched
hands, he cast himself, yelling, into space. “You
dam lobber, you bling ba’ my money. ’’ The tram
whizzed up George-street. Wong sprinted, hold-
ing his breath in both hands. Some Chows can
run. He almost touched the car as it glided
softly away. Wong hung on like the business end
of a typhoon till the tram pulled up. Then he
swung.aboard, panting, and started looking for
the thief, and soon discovered that he was the
white fat man, holding a paper bag in the far
corner. He addressed the quiet hunk of flesh
insolently, demanding his bananas or his cash.
Fat, with rage and indignation, rebuked Wong.
Wong grew hysterical, and apologised for his mis-
take ; then, in the voice of an over-worked vege-
table, asked a thin, elderly man to return his
bananas or his money. When the police arrived,
Wong, with flashing eyes, was pointing to a small
boy in a straw hat as the impostor who had taken
his ducats. The real miscreant he altogether
neglected.
* * * *
Near Gunnedah (N.S.W.), some more than
usually poetic settlers have given their paddocks
a park-like appearance by leaving a few pine trees
standing, which they have trimmed. Almost in-
variably the N.S.W. cocky clears an acre and
plants his bark palace in the centre of the clearing,
so that'passers by can view his domestic arrange-
ments, which are not princely, as a rule. By
judicious thinning, he could have natural avenues
and patches of shade. No wonder the cocky is a
gloomy person, when the view from his verandah
consists of a dreary grey vista, be-ribbed with
skeletons of trees and obscured by a flannel-girt
clothes-line and a stack of jam tins.

“D.” : The best white ant exterminator
is the little black ant. Trail him on to their
camp with a few grains of sugar, and for days
afterwards a long train of blacks will be seen
carting home the bodies of whites killed in battle.
The small black ant suffers severely from Japanese
big head. Once watched two of them trying to shift
a dead beetle that weighed about a quarter of a
pound. After each pull they would stand back
and consult, then rush in again. After four
hours’ hard work they gave the job up and walked
away together, but even then every few inches
they would stop and wipe the perspiration off
their brows, and look disconsolately back. I left
them plugging each other to find out whose fault
it was the beetle wouldn’t budge.

“ Elmore ” : lie lyre-
birds’ mimicry. Was up at
the head of the Buffalo
River with a mate, strip-
ping bark. We had brought
half-a-dozen traps with us
for rabbits, but found them
very scarce. One evening,
after being three days with-
out meat, we heard terrific
squealing out where the
traps were set. “ There’s
a couple caught at last,”
yelled my mate. “Get a
fire on. We’ll have stew
for tea.” I bustled round
peeling spuds and onions.
Mate came back without
the rabbits. “ G’ould have
sworn they w r ere rabbits
squealing,” he grumbled,
“ but none of the traps is
touched.” As he spoke the
squealing started again. We
made for the traps, and as
we got near them heard dis-
tinctly the rattle of trap
chains and more squealing.
“No mistake this time,” I
said; yet still not a trap
was disturbed. While we
were trying to solve the
mystery a laugh rang out as
natural as any jackass could
do it. We both “ tumbled ”
at once ; and though we couldn’t see the lyre-bird
we listened to him as he went through his perform-
ance, even to the ringing tap of the setter, driving
home the peg. No one, to my knowledge, had
ever trapj>ed there before. [The lyre-bird pro-
bably learnt its imitation from a gramaphone.—
Ed. B.]

“ J.E.W.” : Know a half-caste in W.A.
who can give the average white man points in
most things. The progeny of a white squatter
and a Westralian gin, he is a fine, tall man, with
the forehead of a philosopher and the build of a
champion fighter. He has been drover, pearler,
farm-hand—everything nearly except prospector
and pen-j ostler. And he can talk like Deakin.
Was once present when this curiosity fairly broke
up a company by his knowledge of Spencer and
Darwin. He is well up in Mill, Huxley, and
Hume, and has even read some of the classics—in
translation, of course. The same phenomenon
once killed a nor’-west nigger with a punch on the
jaw. He then rode 300 miles to the nearest police
station and gave himself up, and was afterwards
acquitted, being able to prove that the nig. had
aimed first blow with a spear. These are not
aboriginalies ; these are facts.

“A.P.R.” : Saw three turkey-gobblers re-
cently strutting round a game cock, at whom they
chortled with discreet vehemence. The latter
affected supreme contempt and pecked at a straw,
though he watched them keenly. Suddenly No. 1
gobbler threw himself at the cock, but missed, and
stood on his head. Then No. 2 stood on the
rooster’s back, and got severely spurred, but
meanwhile No. 3 had seized the cock by the comb.
The latter spurred him off, and wheeling round,
calmly pecked a piece out of top-knot of No. 1,
who was planning a cowardly rear assault. For
an hour the absurd contest raged. Finally at
dusk 1 saw three bedraggled and bleeding gobblers
trot off in Indian file to their wood-heap, pursued
by a lame rooster, who flew into his tree, shoved
two hens off his roost, and flapped his wings. He
didn’t crow ; the occasion was unworthy.

“Thurla”: “ R.H.A.” writes of “the
loud, booming noise
made at night by the
small quail” (B.
20/10/’O4). Strange that
these birds do not make
similar noises during
the daytime. Bush
people credit the por-
cupine, bronzewing
pigeon, quail, and also
the black snake with
being the author of
these queer nocturnal
sounds. And recently
at sundown, hearing
the peculiar “Woot,
woot, whoop,” I took
my gun along and made
towards the noise. I
started up a big hare
from about the place
where the noise had
been. Having laid him
low, and seeing no other
animal or bird, concluded that
the hare had been calling to his
mate. Since then have seen a hare
in the act. It reared itself on its hind
legs, and with head thrown well back, called loudly,
“ Woot, woot, whoop,” and getting an answer
from another quarter, it cantered away towards
the sound.

“ Nym.” about stings :
The bull-ant’s is an easy first for suddenness, pain, and
force, but ’tissoon over ; the river-hornet’s comes second ;
the scorpion’s third ; the horn bee’s a poor fourth. The
centipede conies sixth, unless he strikes you fair, which is
seldom ; then he runs up third. The scorpion’s bite is
like a hot wire coursing through the system.

“8.5.” : That splendid flytrap, the house
spider, is popularly supposed to be poisonous, but
the insect is libelled. I have been bitten, and
experienced no ill effects. But know a man, re-
Eutedly -plucky, who tramped 10 miles to a doctor
ecause a spider had bitten him. “It was one of
them black devils with a red streak on it,” he
explained ; “ s’pose I’ll lose me bloomin’ arm.” He
only lost his guinea.
Quite a number of Hawlcesbury Rive?
(N.S.W.) residents make a decent living by
gathering the leaves, or rather fronds, of the
beautiful native fan palm, which grows in hun-
dreds of thousands in that district. The fronds
are dried, then compressed and packed into large
flat cases, and exported, in great quantities
annually, to Germany, where they are converted
into the familiar palm fan.

“ Elmore ” : Staying with some friends in
the country, the boy (aged about 11), asked how
he got hares, said : “Run ’em down myself.” I
went with him up to the crop paddock, just ripen-
ing. There were about 40 acres of wheat and five
acres of pumpkins, and below a creek. Going
into the pumpkins, the boy started a hare, which
made straight for the wheat; but, before it had
gone 100yds., the boy had it and brought it to me.
I noticed how distended it was, and, opening,
found the paunch nearly bursting with wheat.
The boy caught 14 that morning. Puss cannot
run after a feed of wheat and a drink of water.

“ Outcast ” : Heard of a party of bushmen
who had everything but eggs for the construction
of an able-bodied bush plum-duff, and as a substi-
tute for this necessary constituent they used what
they afterwards found to be carpet snakes’ eggs.
They mistook them for the soft-shelled eggs of
some wild bird. The duff went down O.K.
“WHEN YOUR BACK’S AGAINST THE WALL!"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Though it’s fine to lounge and liquor
And make love to girls in bars,
While your coin flies quick and quicker
And you smoke the best cigars ;
Though your swell friends say “ good fellow ”
As you shout for one and all,
Yet there comes a savage pleasure
When your back’s against the wall !
When you’re out of luck, and thinking
How a trifling loan you’ll float,
And your flash friends go in drinking
Never asking ’bout your throat;
Oh, you think yourself a martyr,
And you feel a glorious gall
Towards the world and all its baseness,
And your back’s against the wall !
Then there comes a poor Bohemian
With the price of two long beers,
And his kind request to join him
Almost melts you into tears ;
And the humble beer you’re sipping
In the company of “ brain ”
Is a nectar far outstripping
All the glories of champagne !
When you’re fondly loved and trusted
By some girl with starlit eyes,
It’s a mellow, easy blessing
Which you never fully prize ;
But if the maiden jilts you
And she’s at another’s call,
Oh, you nurse a sweet hurt feeling,
And your back’s against the wall!
So it is in all your troubles
When misfortune’s clouds o’erhang,
And bad luck back on you doubles
Like a blessed boomerang !
Oh, it’s then you clench your molars
And Life’s blows you fiercely stall,
Yes ! you feel a savage pleasure
When your back’s against the wall!
W.J.H.
EVOLUTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Four souls from the nest of Heaven were cast,
Half-fledged and with pinions weak,
Who nothing had known of the buffeting blast,
The talon, the tooth, or the beak.
Now one has developed the bill of a hawk,
And battens on things unseen ;
Whilst one like a solemn old jackdaw can talk,
And says what he doesn’t mean.
And one pretty birdie with plumage grand
Now frets in a gilded cage,
And sings in a language few understand
A song of ineffable rage.
And one who with stolid, abstracted air,
Has watched what has come to pass,
Slowly wags his grey head at the raven Care,
And laughs—like this

“Silas Snell ” re the perils of miners :
Bates and Cameron, and four others, had the contract
for sinking the shaft of the Joker Quartz Mining Co. a
further 150 ft., and they were getting along excellently,
when Wilson, one of the bracemen, started his tear ; and
Wilson’s tear was terrific. A man could not be found to
take his place in Musk Gully, so Reynolds and O’Brien
worked long shifts, and cursed Wilson nightly. The shaft
of the Joker Q.M. Co. was situated in a wooded gully
between timbered hills. There were no neighboring
mines, and the nearest residence was two miles distant.
Reynolds and O’Brien had been doing twelve-hour shifts
for eight days, when O’Brien knocked-up, and began to
comfort his soul with beer. There was no braceman for
the afternoon shift on Tuesday, but the whip boy was
careful, and the horse educated, and Bates and Cameron
resolved to go below and square up. They had been at
work about an hour, when suddenly the* big wooden
bucket, laden with centres and chocks, crashed into the
shaft and burst. Bates and Cameron escaped destruction
by a miracle. Bates’s leg was broken, Cameron’s scalp was
lifted, but in the silence that followed he heard the shrill
voice of the whip-boy crying : “ Look out, below ! Wilson’s
come, and he’s in the horrors ! ” There followed sounds of
a scuffle—a scream, and then a thud in the shaft. This
time it was the whip-boy. Then, with two of the logs of
a “set,” Bates and Cameron fashioned a hasty barricade.
Then came the second bucket. It was followed by a
shower of logs, rocks, picks, and mullock. There was a
brief interlude before Cameron heard Wilson cursing
again, and the beating of hoofs above. This ended in a
wild yell, and a moment later a heavy body came bump-
ing down the shaft, tearing out 20ft. of centres. It was
the whip-horse. So far Cameron and Bates were living,
but the whip-boy had. ceased to breathe. The men
waited, cowering, for the next manifestation. Wilson
was laughing gaily down the shaft, and uttering cries
implying that in his exuberant imagination he was bom-
barding his enemies in Hell. “Look up, below ! ” screamed
the braceman. There were thuds of something rebound-
ing on the centres, and another body plunged into the
slush beside the horse. This time it was Wilson. Then
there was peace in Musk Gully till Reynolds came on to
do his shift at the Joker.

“M.Kn.”: A favorite dish amongst the
blacks out Cooper’s Creek way is prepared as
follows: —When a good shower of rain has fallen
after a drought, swarms of “ wood-lice” come out
of the cracked ground. As soon as the grass dries
sufficiently the gins light a ring of fire around the
insects, and slowly burn towards the centre,
gradually converging the insects into a heap.
They then scrape them into a cooliman (a canoe-
shaped dish made of bark, about three feet long,
one foot wide and six inches deep) until it is
about two-thirds full. They have round stones,
about three inches in diameter, ready-heated in a
fire, which they throw amongst the crawling lice,
rolling them about until the insects are suffi-
ciently cooked, when they turn a bright red
instead of a dusky grey. They are then ground
up between two stones with nardoo seed. The
gins occasionally, during the process, squirt water
out of their mouths over the mass. It is made
into cakes, cooked on the coals, and apparently
eaten with the greatest gusto. Whites who have
had the courage to eat it state that it is very
palatable, with a slightly cayenne-peppery taste.

“H.S.R.”: The sundowner, who is
generally a good roadster, is not always one
from choice. In the remote past he was
probably a worker, and only degenerated into
the sundowner through years of almost hopeless
search for. a job. He has solved the problem of
how to live on nothing a day, and earn it.
Ostensibly looking for work, he has got the
hardest of all work waltzing matilda about the
bush, and although a cut above slum life in cities,
it is one of the most miserable of existences.
This is the person to whom the miserable ground
parrot alludes as “going about looking for work,
and praying to God not to find it.” " He is the
modern wandering Jew.

“ Timbarra ” (B. 5/l’/05) starts the New Year by pulling
my hair re Gippsland bullock-punchers. Well, I wrote of
what I know. A few years ago, a thresher and portable
engine passed along the road here, drawn by eight and
six bullocks respectively. The lads driving don’t speak
half-a-dozen words to the team all day, and are in my
class at Sunday-school. They don’t swear. “ Timbarra ”
may be an authority on jackass bashing and gohanna
chasing, but if he tries to pull my hair again, I’ll turn a
searchlight on his general ignorance.

“ J.C.” : Where were the police while
“Kooka” and other Bulletin correspondents
were dynamiting fish ? I did that sort of thing
once on the Hauraki Gulf, and harrowed the
waters of Auckland Harbor, but being resident
now close to a police station inland, I am compe-
tent to reprove unsportsmanlike Waltons of the
“Kooka” brand. Our explosive was a miner’s
gelignite cartridge. The concussion caused by its
bursting disabled, but did not kill, the mullet or
herring. Have recovered 43, per dingy, from one
shoal of mullet. Records of schnapper, kahuwai,
hapuku, and other fish which do not shoal are not
so great. They recover quickly from the shock
and subsequently claim the old age pension. But
the real murderous effect of the explosion is the in-
jury to the spawn, which is an addled egg thence-
forth.

“ Wyung ” : The earliest bush bird
is the wagtail. Also, the latest bush
bird is the wagtail. He is up before
the magpie, and he is around and war-
bling after the mopoke has shut his
face and gone to bunk. I live right
at the Mitchell River, and have been
out on it every hour that there is
on a clock scores of times, but I
never got there before the wag-
fies, not even starting the day
efore. Latest I have heard the
mopoke has been 1 a.m. I’ll defy
anyone to phoneticise the mopokes
remarks. I tried for half an hour,
and in that time he got in all the
variations from “moraporak” to
“ moopook,” and sometimes the
“m” was a “w.”

“ Timbarra ” : Add to the
list of Australian mimics the thrush.
Every evening just at dusk, one
perches in a hedge in front of iny
house and holds a little bird con-
cert on its own, giving the trill and
flow of the canary, the cry of the
mud lark, the unbirdlike clattering
and snapping of the starling ; and
next a flood of melody—the thrush’s
own notes. Then I hear the pecu-
liar cry of a plover issuing from the
middle of the hedge, quickly fol-
lowed by that of a minah. By way
of variety, sometimes, the thrush lets
go the weird noise of a tank-engine,
which, though lacking in power, is
startlingly real.
“ Itan ” : Be the instinct of birds. For
many years flocks of sparrows nested and roosted
inside the folds of ivy which clustered around the
dead branches of an old pear-tree at Singleton
(N.S.W.). A few weeks ago the occupant of an
adjacent house noticed a great chattering and
chirping amongst the sparrows, and just before
dusk they left in a body and did not return. Two
days later one of the limbs of the tree in which the
birds had been accustomed to seek refuge snapped
off at the base from dry rot, and brought down
deserted sparrow-town with a crash.

“Homo”: The daily press chronicles
among its bush-fire items that a resident of
Invermay (Vic.) lost four acres of grass, and
another one, two acres. This conveys but a faint
idea of the appalling loss. It would be no exag-
geration to say that the sustenance of half-a dozen
grasshoppers vanished in this awful conflagration.
I trust the herbage in The Bulletin’s back-yard
escaped the ravages of the fire fiend.

Labor : Well, The Bulletin hasn’t gone into the merits of
the Newcastle trouble because it reckons that the Arbi-
tration Court has as much right to be respected as any
other court of justice, and that therefore matters pending
before it shouldn’t be subjects of newspaper discussion.
But, allowing that the order of the Court for miners to do
wheelers’ work is wrong, which it may be, that doesn’t
justify a strike ; violence is never justifiable when
peaceable legal redress is available. And supposing
the wheelers’ contention to be correct, that the custom
of the Newcastle coal district does not warrant
a reduction of the wheelers’ wages as a corollary to the
reduction of the sell.ng price of coal, still the wheelers
were not justified in striking. They might have, as the
Lithgow smelters did, accepted the reduction under pro-
test, gone on working and appealed to the court for re-
dress. In the Lithgow case the court held that the reduc-
tion enforced was not warranted, restored the old rate of
pay, and ordered the employers to pay over all the money-
short-paid since the reduction. To sum up : The work-
man should not strike when he has the chance
of getting redress from a court of justice, and
should be even content to put up with a little injus-
tice to maintain the principle of industrial arbitra-
tion. The principle is worth interest of that sort....

A correspondent of S.A. Register, de-
scribing a Far North corroboree, says that when all
was over the three white men present were invited
to remain till midnight, when the lubras would
appear and dance in the “altogether.” He says
they all went home immediately. But is that a
native custom, or has the Australian native bor-
rowed the methods of Port Said and Shanghai ?
MISS FANNIE GRAY—the first Victorienne to
qualify at the Dental College of Victoria.
“L.C.” : I could give evidence before a
commission to investigate the worship of the cow.
Know an intelligent man who had a lovely little
vineyard, in which he took great pride. But the
dairying microbe bit him, and he rooted up 50
acres of vines to make a cow pasture. He draws a
good cheque now from the neighboring creamery,
but he is a changed man. The wine-press is still,
and boon companions no longer haunt his cellar on
Sunday afternoons ; also his once light-hearted
sons and daughters have lost the gaiety that
became so well the jolly vigneron’s family, for
they have to rise at unholy hours to milk and
chambermaid 50 or more cows, and when they
fall into bed late at night it is to dream of
“Sukey” and “Strawberry,” instead of lordly
vats, the wine-press of old romance, and the juice
that maketh glad the heart of man.
Griffith and Allen, Patent Attorneys of
Sydney, have opened a branch of their business in
Melbourne—(Broken Hill Chambers, Queen-st.),
under the management of Mr. H. P. Keogh, of
Melbourne.*
“ Sailor-man ” : We were off a small South
American port, and the climate was like a damp
corner in Hades. The meat in the harness-cask
groaned aloud. The skipper swore it was as sweet
as a nut. We demanded to see the Consul. When
he came alongside he was met at the gangway by
the old man, who took him down into the cuddy.
Presently we heard a sweet gurgling sound.
“Nobbled !” said English Bill; “the game’s up,
boys.” The Consul came on deck and demanded
our grievance. English Bill was spokesman.
“ Show me the meat,” the Consul commanded.
He was led aft to the harness-cask. With inju-
dicious zeal, he whipped off the lid, and staggered
back, half-stunned. But he came on again, and,
with his fingers protecting his nose, bent again
over the cask. “ Nothing wrong with the beef ! ”
he sniffed. “Beautiful beef ! Usual case of
chronic grumbling. Quite sympathise with you,
captain.” All hands were then fined two-and-six
apiece for making a frivolous complaint, and the
beef hummed gaily on.

STARLINGS AND OTHER NUISANCES.


It has transpired that a N.S. W. milk-
dealer has made£Boo “clear” profitinone
year on the water alone with which he
diluted the,staple. Clear profit, indeed !
Convinced that there is more money in
water than in High Art, “ Hop ” has
knocked off a little invention which he
thinks of floating into a company
soon. There will be water enough in it,
be says, to float the Russian Navy
when the Japs get through with it.
EXPLANATIONS.
■X Ifon tank, modelled mote or less after
a cow.
B—Suction pipe, let into unlimited under-
ground water reservoir.
CC" Practical tail, acting as pump-handle.
D—Down pipe, connecting with water-shed
(in this case a cow-shed) as.an auxiliary
supply.
b, c, d, e—Service pipes.
E—Catchment Area, for the “clear”
profits.
F—Opening, to admit water and things.

the refreshments.
DIDN’T KNOW IT WAS LOADED.
A peculiar explosion occurred in the yard at
the rear of Messrs. Aylett Brothers’ store, near
Wagga.
The firm does a large business as wine sellers,
and the two brothers were, during the cool ol
Friday evening last; preparing to “sulphur" a
cask preparatory to its return. Throughout
the heat of the day.the cask had been lying in
the yard buiig' downwards, and had to be turned
to admit of the sulphuring process being carried
out. This consists in inserting a lighted sul-
phur torch into the cask through the bung-
holt.
No sooner was this done than a terrific explo-
sion occurred, which was heard a mile away.
The explosion blew out both the heads of the
cask, a portion of one striking Alfred Aylett ’
on the knee, breaking the cap, and inflicting
othtr serious injuries. Other fragments smash- •
ed a picket fence, and one piece was carried a <
distance of ZoO yard®.
ORDER BY THE CZAR.
“The Czar, in an army order announcing the
fall of Port Arthur, exhorts ‘my brave soldiers
and sailors not to allow the sorrow caused by the
fall to discourage you. The enemy is strong and
brave, and it is extremely difficult to sustain a
struggle 10,000 versts (6000 miles) from home, but
J am confident the hour of victory is striking.’ ”
but the worst of it is, it’s striking the
wrong party,

A rumor has been spread through Australasia that
the above action for infringement of Patent rights
has been withdrawn. This is not correct, the
defendants having procured a Commission to take
evidence in Europe which will delay the case six
months.
The Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd.,
desire to warn the Public against dealing with In-
fringers of their Patents. As soon as the above case
is settled the Company will be under the necessity of
proceeding* against USERS AS WELL AS IMPORTERS
for infringement and damages.

WRITES
“ Gentlemen, —About three years ago I
was confined to my bed for a short time with
a very acute attack of Liver complaint.
After other medicines had failed to take any
effect, I was recommended by my doctor to
try Kutnow’s Powder, and I am glad to say
that after taking two bottles I was com-
pletely cured, and my health has been
better ever since. I have since recommended
your excellent preparation to quite a number
of sufferers, who testify of similar results.
“(Rev.) CHAS E. WINCOTT.”
(Incumbent of St. Phillip’s Church,
(Adlington, Lancs.)
Dr. THORPE w M te S:
“ Grant Building, Los Angeles.

it myself occasionally ; it never fails to bring
about the most gratifying results in the most
pleasant manner.”
Sir J. B. MONCKTON
(Late Town Clerk of London) wrote :
“ Guildhall, London, E.C.
“ Gentlemen, —I have much satisfaction in
stating that for the last two years I have
regularly used Kutnow’s Powder, with the
result that troublesome and frequent attacks
of lumbago, with other gouty symptoms that
had troubled me for years, seem to have
entirely disappeared. ’ ’
THE FOOD PASSAGES
and ORGANS.
The body is a working machine, and like all other
machines wears away in use. Our bones and muscles, so firm
and strong, need constant repair to keep them so. For this
purpose food is necessary, and a series of instruments or
organs by which it may be changed into flesh and bone. We
are, therefore, furnished with teeth to chew, palates to taste,
stomachs to digest, livers to aid digestion, and bowels to carry
off the refuse matter. The Liver is the very gateway of
health, the sewage farm, where the poisons are neutralised by
the action of the bile ; it exerts an all-powerful influence on
the mind, temperament, and disposition. If your Liver is
active you are happy and light-hearted, if sluggish you are
crabby and miserable. Kutnow’s Powder will keep the
