POLITICAL
POINTS
Blind leaders may mislead the union work-
ing man into voting against Industrial Arbitra-
tion, but not the working man’s wife. She knows
best of all what strikes mean.
About the saddest proof of the fall of
George Turner in public estimation is that when
some other Turner made the asinine proposal that
the suburb of Prahran should change its name to
Albert Edward, “ in honor of the King,” a lot of
people thought that George Turner was the culprit.
That Australian youngster who, in a recent
exam, for Imperial Navy cadetships, defined, for
a British examiner, an Antipodean as “ a strange
sort of wild animal that burrows,” wasn’t so far
out. Of course he meant “borrows.”
Crick will, of course, resign the Chair of
Committees when the N.S.W. Assembly meets
again, with a view to the election of O’Sullivan to
the vacancy, but a strong combination is being
got together for the return of Cann, the former
worthy occupant of the chair.
Joseph Carruthers is economising in N.S.
Wales on true Bent principles. An old age
pensioner, who has been receiving the little income
for twelve months, was refused the last payment
as he had not a certificate of birth. There is
absolutely no doubt as to his age, and he was
accepted without question at the original inquiry.
The black Tory crowd up north tries again
to block Morgan’s Franchise Bill because, though
it brings the franchise about up to the level of the
Federal franchise, it doesn’t carve up the State
into equal districts. A crowd like that wouldn’t
take medicine for collywobbles unless the dose was
also guaranteed to cure carbuncle. It is the Poli-
tical Pale Pill Party.
CHILD'S PLAY.
“ One day of the Premiers’ Conference will be devoted
to mountaineering, when they will visit the summit of
Mount Wellington.” —News item.
BENR : “Don’t know what you think, Reid, but
to my mind scaling blooming mountains is rather
undignified for chaps in our position!" (And
for once the State and Federal Premiers agreed.)
Major Carroll has been lecturing before
the Prahran branch of the A.N.A. on Australian
defence, putting forward sound views. The first
line of defence should be, he maintained, naval;
then garrison artillery and submarine mines ; and
lastly a field force, for which every able-bodied
citizen should be trained. The most conspicuous
lack at present was that of any sure naval defence.
Another example of the inefficiency and
wasteful extravagance of Socialism. Privately-
owned farms and stations in Vic. suffered by bush
fires to the extent of over £70,000, while in the
state forests, which were attacked in a number of
places, every blaze was blocked by the breaks
which the-Department's officers had made at the
start of the summer, at a total cost for the whole
state of £l5O.
Vic. Education Minister Sachse is now
sitting in judgment on the case of a democratic
small boy, who is the subject of half-a-dozen long
foolscap reports from school inspectors and
teachers. The youngster was told to compose
a number of sentences containing certain words,
and one of these words was “ resilient,” the mean-
ing of which was given as “bounceable.” The
youngster smiled, then let out several inches of
his tongue, and scrawled on his slate, “ The
Premier (Mr. Bent) is very resilient.” Knowing
that the boy’s father was a dismissed railway
striker, the teacher “suspended” him from the
school for the fortnight preceding the holidays ; he
had a joyous time swimming, while his fellows
were working hard and a dozen highly-paid Govt,
officers were trying to discover a way of punishing
him for lese majeste.
Excusing Australia for not making aa much
in mining dividends as S’Africa, Sydney D.T. says
that Chow Africa has the advantage of cheap
labor ; “we should not make so much out of the
Rand as S’Africa is able to do.” No; we should
not make so much for the foreign, absentee Jew-
owner; but we should make more for our own
country. Can’t the D.T. get out of the habit of
reckoning up what a thing is worth to the foreigner
and valuing it accordingly ?
Tobacco Dixson’s place, as Tory represen-
tative of the City in S. Aus. Assembly, may go to
Dr. Angas Johnson, a youngster whom the Angas
family estates have heavily gilded, and who, not
having need to worry about practice, spends his
time and money mostly in scientific research. S.
Australia badly wants an enthusiastic sanitarian
in some high place ; but the Angas influence holds
Angas Johnson back. It’s years since the State
had a doctor in its politics—Dr. Cockburn was the
last in the Assembly, and Dr. Campbell in the
Council. Both did good work. But that was
years ago.
Daily Mail war correspondent M‘Kenzie
reckons that the balance of the war in the Far
North is swinging against Japan. The Russian
army is improving in morale and effectiveness,
and the .laps have made an enemy in their rear—•
the Koreans :
The Korean people, as a whole, from the members of
the Court downwards, believe that the Japanese intend to
take everything- from them, and to make them hewers of
wood and drawers of water. Nine months ago, when the
Russian cavalry swept down into Northern Korea, the
natives lied to them to send them back, and aided the
Japanese with information. They are now prepared to
hail the Russians.
An alarmed London deputation of proprie-
tary medicine folk has asked M.L. Agent-General
Reeves to urge on his Government the cruelty of
making the various quack nostrums, known (incor-
rectly) as patent medicines, disclose their formulas
when offered for sale. Unfortunately, the depu-
tation was needlessly alarmed. The M.L. Govern-
ment is understood" to have abandoned its first
wise intention of following a German law and
making all proprietary medicines disclose their
ingredients on each bottle. A great service would
be done to the people by such a law'. The pro-
prietary medicine is almost always a fraud ; as a
rule it is worth only from 10 to 25 per cent, of
what is charged for it, and the purchaser is lucky
if he escapes with money loss only and is not also
damaged in health by consuming noxious and
unsuitable drugs. If the ingredients of most of
the pills and swills so freely advertised were
announced on their bottles few would be fooled
into buying, and those few would thoroughly
deserve to be fooled.
The trustees of Melbourne Public
Art Gallery have lately borrowed for exhi-
bition some old prints of the highest in-
terest. Mostly they show Yarratown in
various early stages of its development.
The one of most human interest is of the
first Legislative Council meeting at the old
St. Patrick’s Hall, in 1851. In a little
square room, like a present-day suburban
court, are crowded many early celebrities—
Latrobe, Sta\yell, and Bedmond Barry
(afterwards Justices), Haines, McArthur,
Snodgrass and O’Shanassy (a hefty-looking
pair), John Thomas Smith, Francis Murphy
and J. P. Fawkner. This of Fawkner is
probably the best likeness extant, showing
a somewhat weak, petulant face, with
skimp, dark hair, but fine eyes. Among
the spectators is E. Henty, who, if the
likeness is correct, was a good double of
W. M. Thackeray.
The Labor-Liberal party in the
S.A. Assembly, in the hope that it will be
returned to power at the coming elections,
will attempt to take its Attorney-General
into Parliament with it. Paris Nesbit, K.C.,
has been selected by the Labor party as one
of its candidates for the Assembly.
Tommy Bent’s idea of a “Day of Rest,”
as he explains it, is evidently a lethargic period of
time devoted to boozing, gormandizing and sleep-
ing—the English Sunday. But a day of genuine
rest must be a day of change. The “ break-away ”
from the routine of the week is the great thing
needful, and that involves Sunday excursions,
Sunday museums and galleries. Then those who
serve the public health on Sunday can have their
rest on some other day.
The S’Australian State school exhibitions
and bursaries (the former for boys and the latter
for girls) usually go to the sons and daughters of
comparatively well-to-do people. A school in-
spector explains why
No child who is not promoted more rapidly than the
regulations prescribe, or who does not learn subjects out-
side the ordinary school curriculum, has the slightest
chance of winning an exhibition or a bursary. Special
coaching is needed in every case, and the poorer children
have very little hope of succeeding.
The State thus is in the position of offering a boon
to the children of the poor with one hand and
taking it away with the other.
Victoria’s Bent Government made a char-
acteristic arrangement for Christmas pension-
drawing. It fixed up the civil servant’s big
pension so that it could be drawn on the 23rd of
December, in time for the purchase of Christmas
wassail and supplies ; but it hung up the small
old-age dole till after the holidays. It required a
fairly hoggish mind to split the State’s pensioners
into two classes, leaving the more numerous and
more deserving to draw in its belt while the other
crowd waved a collective corkscrew and enjoyed
itself in general. And when anything hoggish is
called for, Premier Bent is the man to step into
the breach.
Bent has just appointed the members of
the Land Purchase Board, on whom the chief
work of the Closer Settlement Act devolves. The
expected has happened. The Board gives not the
slightest promise of successful administration. It
consists of Thomas P. Cumming, J. M. Gillespie,
and D. J. Duggan. Cumming is a reactionary
Tory, and a landowner connected with the Rupert
Clarke coterie. J. M. Gillespie is a relic of the
Boom. He at present fills the post of manager to
the Freehold Assets Realisation Company, which
is a Boom product. D. J. Duggan is a good-
natured weakling, who sat in the Assembly for
many years as member for Dunolly.
Whenever the Victorian Lands Depart-
ment makes an effort to get in some of the
£600,000 of land-rent arrears owed by the Reformer
cookies, there is trouble. Recently a circular was
sent out to those farmers whose rents were more
than 12 months in arrears, stating that if they did
not pay up they would be called before a land
board to explain the reason. Many of those who
are cheating the State of its money are well-to-do
men. If the matter got before a land board this
fact would be brought out. The circulars, there-
fore, had no sooner been distributed than the
newspapers were flooded with sad, tearful letters
from “ Struggling Selector” and “Poor Farmer.
Probably now the Bent Government will with-
draw and apologise.
FURIOUS SWIMMING.
COPPER (starting suddenly out of the vast deep): “ Your name an ’ address, if ye please!”
Many years ago, Bananaland Parliament,
in the interests of shipwrecked people, planted
cocoanut trees on the islands of the Great Barrier
Beef at a cost of £5OOO. The trees are now bear-
ing, and the Morgan-Kidston Govt, proposes to
turn them to account by leasing them. This sug-
gests the duty of every State to attend to forestry.
Trees planted in the arid wastes of the West would
help to make the Never-Never country habitable
and profitable. In Maoriland, forestation is being
carried on by prison labor.
The Federal Navigation Commission has
elicited the fact that the enthusiastic way in which
the Boys of the Bulldog Breed turn their backs on
the briny is partly attributable to the vile food
usually set before the hungry sailor. A wastrel,
who has generally made a hideous failure of
several callings before he makes a more hideous one
still of cooking, most often gets the billet of cook-
ing on a coasting vessel. (These remarks do not
apply to passenger ships.) It is argued that if
sea cooks had to possess certificates, the same as
masters and mates, life on the ocean wave would
lose one terror. But in addition to certificates
they’d need to have decent galleys. The average
small, stuffy galley is impossible to keep clean, and
has cooking apparatus of a most antiquated type.
ZIG-ZAG.
MAGISTRATE: “By all accounts, this man was as
drunk as he could be. Why didn’t you effect an

POLICEMAN : An’ so I would have, yer honor; but
he ran away zig-zag, an’ faith wiry toime Oi
wint zig he wint zag — an’ voisy-versa.”
There is trouble in S.A. orer the contem-
plated resignation of Premier Jenkins to take the
Agent Generalship in London. Treasurer Butler
thinks he ought to step into Jenkins’ shoes as a
matter of course, but Darling, who fathered the
present coalition, shakes his head. “ Where do I
come in?” he asks in effect; “ I stood aside for
Jenkins last session because it suited me ; but why
should I sacrifice myself for Bucler ? ” It is the
Darling party that has kept the Government in
office for the last two years. The Ministerial
reconstruction must take place before the elec-
tions, which should be interesting. The National
Ass threatens to oppose all candidates who support
an alteration of the Legislative Council franchise,
and the Ministry, as constituted at present, is
pledged to liberalise the suffrage.
“Gr.H.”: The “ what-has-it-done-for-De-
mocracy” cry of Queensland Worker anent the
Morgan Government is typical of many
Labor prints. A Government may do its utter-
most in order to stop the country's drift towards
insolvency, and its Treasurer may tear his heart
out in the awful task of ledger-straightening, but
the short-sighted Labor press will meet him with
the same old yap as regards his “ doings-for-De-
mocracy ” unless he can find time in between to
pass a lot of “ breaking-manacles ” and other legis-
lation of the advertising order. It was the same
when Watson and Isaacs were trying to root up
the Federal tariff in order to provide more work
for the Australian population. Sydney Worker,
I think, was the only Australian Labor paper that
did not curse the Watson-Isaacs alliance.
Ararat (Vic.) Council lately took a referen-
dum of its ratepayers as to whether it should
borrow £250 to cover £250 conditionally offered by
Bent for fencing and otherwise improving its town
hall. There were over 600 entitled to vote, and
they had three choices : (1) Borrow—for which 115
voted ; (2) let it rip—for which 107 voted ; and
(3) raise the money by a special rate —and only 11
were honest enough to make the last choice.
The American Harvester Trust is steadily
licking up the Yankee reaper and binder industry.
The biggest firms are now combined under the
name of the International Harvester Company,
which makes ploughs, harrows, seed-drills, mowers,
hay-rakes, etc., as well as reapers and binders;
and, in a short time, unless tariff revision inter-
feres, the agricultural implement business of the
Commonwealth will be entirely in its hands.
Probably the' English Hornsby Co. and one
or two smaller firms will be forced to accept
the terms of the combine; and then prices will
whizz upward. The Yankee firm makes a
specialty of the reaper and binder. Its
value, landed on the wharf in Australia, is
probably not more than £2O ; but Australian users
are charged more than double that figure. There
is not a single portion of these machines that could
not be made in Australia. Indeed, the knotter
device was invented by an Australian in the first
place; and was collared along with a lot of other
Australian improvements by the Yank. Now the
Australian farmer will have to pay through the
nose for his own improvements.
The chronicle of S. Australia’s descent
from Kingston to Darling :
Kingston began in ’93 with Playford, Cockburn, Gordon,
Holder and Gillen as colleagues, the strongest lot South
Australia ever had. Then Playford went to England, and
Jenkins came in, because he had been whip. Kingston’s
promising young man, Gillen, died, and O’Loughlin
arrived. The democratic pace was too warm for
‘ l Johnny ” Gordon, and O’Loghlin came in. The decadent
Oockburn chose to go to England as Playford’s successor,
and Butler was admitted—a Minister for Agriculture was
wanted, and Butler was a farmer. In ’99 Kingston looked
round and found himself left with just one of his colleagues
of six years before ; and he went out. Then, after a
few days, Holder further watered the team by taking in
Foster ; but, with Batchelor, another new man, he went
out himself to go to the Reps., and then Jenkins had for
colleagues Gordon (resurrected, the pace having slack-
ened), Butler, Foster, O’Loughlin and Brooker. The two
most democratic, O’Loughlin and Brooker, were dropped
when portfolios were reduced to four. It was still an
allegedly Liberal team—one part Liberal and 10 parts water.
Then Gordon escaped to the bench, and the frankly Con-
servative Von Doussa was added. The diluted Foster
was strained out next, and the frankly Conservative
Vardon substituted. Von Doussa was changed for the
still narrower Homburg. And now, finally, Jenkins him-
self crawls out and goes to Enaland as Agent-General, to
make room for Darling, the Conservative leader.

Mr. L. C. RAWLS, Searight, Ala., writes:
“ M.v little boy, five years old, took WHOOPING-
COUGH. I thought he could not live. The doctor said
his lungs were diseased, and gave him medicine, but it
did no good. JAYNE’S EXPECTORANT CURED HIM.”*
When the heat fiend is around, handing out a
fiery atmosphere for common or garden breathing
purposes, a man needs to use serious, not to say solemn,,
discrimination in the matter of liquid relief. Such dis-
crimination always results in D.C.L. A wise citizen
knows well the brand which will suit his complaint in
any time of stress upon his mental or physical powers.
When the grateful, not-too-often query “ What’s yours?”
rings in his perspiring ear he feels the D.C.L. thrill pass-
ing through him, and he winks at the Hebe behind the
bar. The wink of the wise is as good as a word to an
experienced barmaid. She knows it for what it means—
D.C.L. every time.*

| S.A. is playing the goat to some purpose.
I John Bailes, who has 600 miles of country beyond
j Oodnadatta, tells Adelaide Begistek that in
years he has bred 140 common goats up into 650
I “ third cross ” angoras (besides eating 200 a year on
! the station !). The wool already fetches 7d. a
pound, and will be worth more as the cross-breed-
ing works up. Bailes, by the way, denies the
usual theory that a goat will live where a sheep
can t. “ When we had had no rain for two years,
1 could get a fat sheep where I could not find a
fat goat. The advantage is that the goat eats the
bushes, while the sheep eats the grass ; and so the
two industries dovetail in.

Division of the High Court of Justice made
in an action in the matter of the Estate of
SAMUEL STANIFORTH Bower v. Staniforth
1902 S. No. 4455 the Creditors of Samuel Stani-
forth late of Marulan in the State of New South
Wales and Commonwealth of Australia of no occu-
pation who died on the 29th day of December 1891
are on or before the 11th day of April 1905 to send
by post prepaid to Mr. George Holme Bower of
Number 4 Breams Buildings (Chancery Lane)
E.C. London England member of the firm of
Bower Cotton & Bower of the same place the
Solicitors for the Administrator of the deceased
their Christian and Surnames addresses and des-
criptions with full particulars of their claim a
statement of their accounts and the nature of the
Securities (if any) held by them or in default
thereof they will be peremptorily excluded'from
the benefit of the said order. Every Creditor
holding any security is to produce the same before
Mr. Justice Warrington at his Chambers Room
No. 292 at the Royal Courts of Justice Strand
London England on Tuesday the 18th day of
April 1905 at 12 o’clock noon being the time
appointed for adjudicating on the claims.
Any person who has any knowledge of any Will
of the said Samuel Staniforth is required to send
particulars thereof to the said George Holme
Bower.
Dated this 29th day of November 1904.
THOS. A. ROMER
Master of the Supreme Court.
Note. —The said Samuel Staniforth formerly
resided in Yorkshire England and left England
for New South Wales in the year 1852 and con-
tinued to reside there (except for a short period)
until his death in 1891.

G. E. CRAKE & SONS } '
“Vigilans,” from the back end of
Nowhere: —
Globe-trotters who have described the life of Aus-
tralian swagmen as a picnic should visit the nor’-west of
Westralia. They are allured up to places like Broome by
nigger owners in Fremantle, only to find when they get
there that all the work is done by Japs, Chows, Manna-
men, and Malays. Very many try to negotiate the water-
less track back to civilisation per boot, with the hope of
picking up a job en route. They might as well look for
snow in Sheol. Every station has from 40 to 100 inden-
tured blacks, who sign on for 12 months. This signing on
is a joke. The niggers under the agreement are supposed
to get clothes, rations, and medicine. Most of them
receive rations—lolb. flour, lib. meat, jib. tea, lib. sugar,
and a stick of nigger tobacco per week for three months
only at shearing ; then they go on a “ holiday ” for nine
months, receiving nothing. Nigg-ers do all the shearing,
rouseabouting, pressing, etc., while their gins pick
up and roll. The only whites are the boss, overseer,
and blacksmith. Fencing, team-driving, well-sinking, and
boundary-riding is all done by blacks, Chows and Malays.
The stranded white man is to be pitied indeed. The
squatter keeps a store and charges fabulous prices—ss.
for a towel ; Is. fid. for a tin of jam ; 2s. a tin of milk ; Is.
a loaf of bread. The distances between stations and
water are enormous—sixty and seventy miles from one
homestead to another, and forty between water is quite
common. The country is hungry-looking, without a tree
for miles, except on the hanks of the so-called rivers,
which are always dry. The heat is intense, and huge
clouds of red dust, called “cock-eyed bobs,” are continu-
ally rising up and coloring everything red. The flies are
frightful, and you soon become half blind. You travel
for days without seeing a living thing', even a bird. Along
the teleg'raph-lines there are shed-tanks at intervals
of 25 or 30 miles. These are for the linesmen and
are usually locked ; but they have very little
water. Scores of men perish in this awful wilder-
ness. Going from the Yanarie to the Ashburton
I was shown four spots where men had died of thirst.
The squatters are adopting a penny wise and pound
foolish policy by employing niggers on their runs. As an
illustration : A nig is sent round a paddock and reports
fences, water, &c., all right. He never goes near the place,
but has a quiet doss under a tree. The overseer goes
round a week after and finds 100 or so dead sheep, or the
fence down and sheep g'one. In an extensive tour along
the Gascoyne, Mainlya and Ashburton Rivers I only came
across one station without blacks. Messrs. Burt Bros., of
Peedamulla (Ashburton), have not a single black on their
run, and are now beginning to find out, after lfi years’ ex-
perience, that, it pays better to have white men. They
told me that they had battled in this awful country year
after year with heavy losses in til they became grey-headed
from anxiety, and ic is only during the past three years
since they did away with the blacks, that things began to
look up and the overdraft look down.
The colored print, • ‘ It’s a shame to take
the money,” just now being freely sold in Aus-
tralia, has been prosecuted in London as an “im-
proper picture.” One vendor was fined £5.

“Caro”: How many people have noticed
the. cat’s susceptibility to color ? Give puss a
choice of cushions, and it will take the warmest-
tinted one. Crimson is greatest favorite. Should
a table boast a crimson cloth, the house cat will be
fond of a perch upon it, dreamily inspecting the
color between her fore paws. Purple is another
favorite.

Killeen’s Woodyard.
The sergeant at Melb. City Court puts the
holiday drunks through at the pace of a champion
cyclist. Thus:—“John Jones pleads guilty,
your Worship. Here before on th’ tenth last
month. There’s a shilling cab hire.” “Five
shillings, and a shilling, or 12 hours. Call
Thomas Smith”—and so a hundred or so are
rushed through in an hour.
“Exile”: Know an old cockie who is a
whale for patent medicines. He is fully convinced
that there is something going “bumpety bump”
in his head, for which he cannot account, accom-
panied by a “ rushin’ and roarin,” which he
elaborately explains as the result of “three holes
in the left drum and two in the right.” He has
consulted a number of quacks by letter at great
expense (one of them sent a soothing ointment
which took the skin off), but ultimately bec'ime so
enamored of the merits of “ pills” that he
took the contents of 24 bottles. The result of this
course of medicine was that he was brought into
the town hospital. This cost him £6 6s. private
hospital fees and £l5 medical attendance, etc., but
he still swears by the pills. He is over 70, and
tough.
Mr. N. N. CAZEN, Sparrow Bush, N.Y.,
writes: “ Jayne’s Expectorant is the best Cough
medicine I know of. IT HAS SAVED BOTH MY OWN
AND MY CHILDREN’S LIVES.”
“T.D.”: Even in conventional England,
the Church hasn’t a particularly rosy time nowa-
days. At St. John’s Wood, London, I came
across a notice —“This freehold chapel, with seat-
ing for 500, also vestries, class-room, and baptis-
tery, to be let or sold.” The edifice hadn’t been
replaced by another, the congregation had
simply slumped until there was practically none
of it left. Also, I was taken into a church the
other day, which first belonged to the Wesleyans,
who abandoned it; then to the prosperous Salva-
tionists, who have taken more commodious pre-
mises; then served for a time as an auctioneer’s
premises; and is now inhabited by the Roman
Catholics.

Result of a recent experiment by Victorian
Government Viticultural expert Burney suggests
chilling as a method of improving Australian
wines. Samples of Cabernet and White Hermi-
tage of the 1904 vintage were placed in the
Government cold stores in June, and taken out
about the middle of December. Brought to normal
temperature they compared most favorably with
pasteurised wines. The chilled white wine had
improved in flavor, bouquet and finish. The
Cabernet had lost some of its color, much of its
roughness, and all of the saline flavor peculiar to
new Victorian wines. Thought that the improved
flavor is attributable to the precipitation by the
cold of the cream of tartar —always excessive in
Australian wines—and with it the albuminous
matter and tannates.
An added terror to the lot of the public
servant is the annual mental earthquake he goes
through when the Estimates are on. Every public
servant who, after years of worry, has climbed to
the dizzy height of £350 or £4OO a year, is liable
to slide down the greasy pole of affluence to £3lO
or £350 at the shortest notice. The financial year
starts in July, so that any reduction made by
Parliament in December counts back about six
months, and the dead horse has to be worked off.
In bad years a rapid greying of the hair sets in
among civil servants as the Estimates approach,
and when the day of anguish is safely past there
is a reaction—an hysterical feeling of relief like
that of the person who dreamt that he went into
a shop in a nude state to buy a copy of the
Watchman, and woke up to find that he didn’t;
and the Under-Sqcyqtary is almost driven.to shed
his dignity and throw a paper-ball at the mes-
senger.

SPORTING NOTIONS
Bob M‘Leod has stated that the Australian
Eleven was “ picked in the pleasantest manner,” although
Darling, as one of the selectors, formally declined to vote
for himself. Satisfactory to know that Darling showed
no unpleasantness when his two colleagues insisted on
picking him. He doubtless dissembled his astonishment
and bowed gravely to the inevitable. What a subject for
a great historical picture—“ The Picking of the Team!”
» * * *
South Australia is inclined to be disappointed
with Darling for putting himself in the Australian Eleven
instead of Claxton. Right up to the moment of his de-
parture with the South Australian team for Melbourne
and Sydney, Darling assured his Adelaide intimates that
he had no desire to go to England, and would not accept
a place in the team if he were picked.
Syd. Gregory goes Home for his sixth trip with
the Australian XI., but apart from him, the accusation
of old-stagers levelled against the team does not work out to
its disadvantage, compared with previous teams. Darling
and Hill have gone three times before, but Blackham went
eight times, A. Bannerman and Boyle six ; Bonnor, Giffen,
Murdoch, Spofforth and H. Trumble five times ; Trott,
Jarvis, Mac Donnell, S. P. Jones and Palmer four times
each. Johnny Briggs, on the other hand, came to
Australia six times, and he was the nearest to approach
Blackham’s record, which Noble, Hill and others, though
“ not out,” have a long way to go to reach.
* * * *
“ 0.K.” : Two or three members of the selected
Australian Eleven were knocking round Collins-street,
Melbourne, the other afternoon, with seemingly nothing
to do but display themselves. Their pose of greatness
was only a little less absurd than the air of reflected glory
assumed by their admiring companions. A stage hero
strutting aiong the street in sublime consciousness of the
notice he attracts is reckoned a fair mark for sarcasm,
whereas these “star” cricket performers rarely or never
get a reminder that their heads need poulticing. This is
intended for a reminder.
* * #
The “understudy ” wicket-keeper doesn’t retain
his position in the Australian XI. Johns went in ’99,
Carter in 1902, and Newland is in the present team—alias
second “ stumpers ” to Kelly. The selection committee
of the team this year should give Kelly more spells and
the “ understudy ” more work.
* * * *
The Australian XI. members are aged as fol-
lows : Cotter 21, Gehrs 24, Armstrong and Duff 26, Hill
Trumper, and Newland 27, Hopkins 28, Noble 31, Gregory
and Darling 34, Howell, McLeod and Laver 35, Kelly 37.
* * * *
By demoralising Waverley and scooping in
8 wickets for 86 runs on Saturday, Central Cumberland
cricketer L. W. Pye gave the denial to those who allege
that he is no trundler. His bowling was of a very high
order, and but for many catches being dropped and faulty
fielding, his average would have been much better.
* * * *
Doubtful if Cotter’s fast ones will be of very
much assistance to. the Australian team in England. At
the first they will no doubt scare the batsmen, and a few
of the unfortunates may be killed, but the way that Gehrs
slogged even the swiftest of them when he got the stroke,
proves that they look a lot worse than they really are. it
would be unfortunate if one of them should strike Warner ;
nothing would dissuade the English press that we hadn’t
sent Cotter home specially to wreak revenge on him for
libelling our country.
* * -Jr *
“Old man ’’ J. C. Reedman, of S. Aus., reaped
17 wickets in inter-State matches, the highest tally
during the presentseason.
The plaint of “G. 8.” : Samuel Johnson once de-
clared that the ruin was assured of a nation wholly given
over to trading,, which brought about no internal increase
and entailed a mere gambling with values, without any
real addition to the sum-total of national wealth. Were
his ghost to re-visit the earth to-day and take passage
from his beloved London to Australia, he would be com-
pelled to re-model his aphorism and to ask how long could
a people exist almost wholly devoted to sport—even with
the aid of the foreign money-lender ? For we are appar-
ently rapidly approaching' an epoch wherein all our able-
bodied, both men and women, will be professional foot-
ballers, cricketers, cyclists, boat-sailers, scullers, anglers,
swimmers, polo players, pedestrians, pugilists, two-up
spielers, tennis players, rifle shots and jockeys; while
most of the older and physically incapable will earn their
daily bread and beer by barracking, betting, bookmaking,
horse-training, sweep-promoting, tote-running, and
gambling. The remainder, if any, will live by shop-keep-
ing, liquor-selling, entertaining and preaching. A few
will run ponies all the week while talking temperance on
Sundays. Of course, it is possible that, in the days to
come, all the championships will be held in Australia,
which will then have become the playground of the
world, and will raise revenue by charging the other
nations for admission—so much to land, so much a week
to remain, and so much for admission to the races and
sports then going on. In those days, the Australian XI.
will form the Federal Cabinet; M. A. Noble will be Prime
Minister ; Larry Foley will refuse the High Commissioner-
ship for the more lucrative post of Referee over the Inter-
national Boxing Matches ; and Jockey Barden, Swimmer
Cavill and Footballer Wickham will constitute the High
Court —all the disputes then coming before that august
body being of a sporting character. That is what we are
coming to.
* * * *
The German residents at Lobethal, in the hills
behind Adelaide, decided their annual “ Kingship ” shoot
this year in great style. Open to all comers, it has been
won for five years by J. T. Lake, the international shot,
who got himself disliked by objecting to march in pro-
cession with a kingly wreath round his brow. This time
he was away winning a much bigger event in W.A., so
Paech won, with Henzenroder 2nd and Bom 3rd, and the
winner was crowned with oakleaves by Miss Altman, and
secretary Kleinschmidt saw to it that all Lobethal swam
in lager bier.
* * *
*
The Bulletin has received “International
Cricket, England v. Australia—Review of Fifty-one Great
Test Matches,” compiled by R. H. Campbell and edited
by “ Old Cricketer,” and is still amazed at the amount of
method and careful statistics the cricket crank gets into
his madness. There is no detail of the matches described
which seems to have escaped the notice of this pains-
taking book, and everything is tabulated and indexed, so
that to the man who writes cricket notes the work is a
most valuable one for reference.
* * * *
A. A. Cameron, Scotland’s champion all-round
athlete, and G. H. Johnstone, ex-champion, arrived in
Dunedin (M.L.) in time to compete at the New Year
sports. Wrestling with Cameron, R. J. Scott, the M.L.
champion, at one time looked like a winner ; but looks
told another lie. Cameron, who beat George Lurich, but
was beaten by Hackenschmidt about twelve months ago,
says he wants another go with the Russian. He weighs
I7st. odd. Johnstone is taller than Cameron, weighs 16st.,
is steely-grey-haired with a little hit off the top. In his
two ’rastling bouts with J. Riordan (from M.L. West
Coast), the Scot went down ; and Riordan succumbed to
Harry Pearce about a couple of months ago.
* * * *
“Delhi” Neilson, the tough little Bendigo
miner who tried in vain to make a match with Carkeek,
was allowed to meet Hackenschmidt on his own terms.
The big man clothed his nakedness in a canvas jacket,
same as Neilson, and got his first public experience of
Cornish wrestling at Melbourne Opera House last week.
Neilson, a victorious expert in his own style, had
looked a likely winner “on paper,” but this impression
was not confirmed when he faced Hackenschmidt on the
stage: The difference in their weights was exactly 5
stone, arid the big ’un proved to be a mighty smart novice
at the game. No wrestling on the mat in the Cornish
style. The trick riiust be done in one act, both shoulders
and one hip, or both hips and one shoulder striking the
mat at the same time before the fall counts. Neilson was
downed several times, but he fell safely face downwards
as a rule. Then he succeeded in getting a “ crook ”on
Hackenschmidt’s left leg. It made no difference to the
huge Russian, who carried his man along with him,
“ crook ” and all. Finally he worked the one point that
the little chap had been afraid of—the point which was
bound to end the unequal struggle. Getting a suitable
grip of Neilson’s jacket, he drew him to his chest off his
feet, and fell upon him fair and square. Time, 6min. 40sec.
This combination of strength and weight was too much
for the nimble tactician.
* * *
Standard of Queensland swimming is advancing
remarkably, spite of poor facilities for practice. Young
Springfield’s mile the other day topped anything done by
Australian champions prior to the advent of Cavill, Reran
and co., and Dan Gailey’s “ hundred ” (in 1.2 2-5) was
also good. Quality of Queensland swimming is only
second to that of N.S.W.
« * * *
Cecil Healey’s win of the 100yds. championship
of N.S.W. was accomplished in the face of a strong wind
at Elkington Park Baths. Owing to a badly-scratched
knee Wickham didn’t swim, and Healey was never worried
in the whole race. He got in front at the first, and kept
on increasing his lead till he was two yards in front of
Murray, the second man. The time (lmin. 1 l-ssee.) was
decidedly slow, but is accounted for by the strong N.E.
wind.
* » * *
During past week some of Sydney’s fore-front
sprint swimmers have been doing big things. Hobson
(Randwick and Ooogee Club) threw GO yards behind in
34 3-ssec. at Redfcrn carnival, Monday night, which sup-
planted Australian Fred Lane’s previous world’s record —-
35 l-ssec.—and that, strangely enough, is exactly what
schoolboy champion “ Len ” Murray did at North Sydney
Club’s three-event meeting a few days before over 6 yards
more (66), but, as he swam with costume short of that re-
quired by rules, and watch-holders were not specially
appointed by the Amateur Swimming Association, his
performance (though a great one) doesn’t count as a
record. Difference racing with ordinary swimming trunks
and the full costume makes to a swimmer is demonstrated
by the fact that Hobson, wearing trunks only, swam 81
yards in 48 2-ssec., and with the complete outfit couldn’t
do better than 49sec. over a yard less.
* * » *
Jockey W. Henderson, fatally injured by a fall
from Silvermine in a lepping affair at Perth, the other
day, was verging on 50 ; he had ridden jumpers for years
(between 20 and 30), particularly in Queensland, without
once having the slightest offence charged against him.
* * . * *
Adelaide Racing Club, fearful of “ ringing in,”
has decided that markings and brands of all known nags
shall be taken before they go out to race. N.S.W.
National Coursing Association has had that check for dogs
during past two or three years.
Entries for Doncaster Handicap and Sydney
Cup (A. J.C. Autumn meeting) are short this year. The
Doncaster has 106 as against 115 last year, and Sydney
Cup 70, as against 80, but the quality of the horses en-
gaged is better than before. Sydney Turf Club’s anni-
versary meeting (two days) has attracted 143 entries—
three better than last year.
* • * *
James Scobie’s stable supplies eight of the 22
entries for the Champion Stakes, and the most imposing
names are among Scobie’s lot. But if a 3-year-old is to
win this year’s Champion—and the youngsters have the
best of the w.f.a. scale in March—it will probably be old
Jimmy Wilson’s Blinker, who ran so stoutly in the
Melbourne Cup.
* * * *
Westralian sports seem to be impressed by the
fact that two or three locally-bred 2-year-olds are showing
their heels to the best of the imported ones this season.
It remains to be seen whether these precocious juveniles
will continue to shine by comparison with the foreigners.
The average climate of Westralia is perhaps good for the
development of racehorses.
When Merry Del Yal was made a cardinal by
Pius X., it was cabled out to the Australian papers —
“Merry Delval Cardinal.” One Launceston daily sent
the wire on to the sporting editor. The three names, of
course, meant to him the placed horses in an English
race. He turned up his English sporting book, and thus
the cable appeared next day
The Turf in England.
The Old Tom Stakes were run for to-day: Merry 1,
Delval 2, Cardinal 3.
„ * * *
M.L. Axemen’s Association’s annual meeting at
Eltham last month-end saw a dozen well-endorsed con-
testants, representative of the whole continent, compete
in Championship Chop—2ft. standing block. World’s
premier underhand and standing chopper, Victorian Gus
Blanc—who has held the title two years and owns the
record (3min. 58sec.) for a 2ft. block—won with 30 seconds
to spare after a tussle, throughout which each competitor
had every ounce up. Previous year’s winner, Maorilander
Hutton, struck a particularly knotty job and used up
three axes in dealing with it. Pretty (Hamilton) beat
Casey (Eltham) for second by just a blow or two, and the
pair were lOsec. in front of Tasmanian Goold. Winner’s
time was smin. 34 2-ssec. Third man, C. W. Casey, ap-
propriated the championships in .1901 (smin. 15sec.) and
1902 (6min. 13 2-ssec.).
* * * *
Sydney Flying Squadron has hitherto been in
the lucky position of having its prize-fund regularly
guaranteed by that good sport, Mark Foy. This year
Foy reckoned it was time the dog learned to swim, and
cast the club adrift on its own resources with a cheque
for £5O and his blessing.
* * * *
The five yachts that started in the Prince Alfred
Club’s ocean race, on Saturday, found the north-east
breeze blowing in the harbor with the fury of a young
typhoon. Outside, it was comparatively calm, and the
CYCLIST GEORGE FARLEY, Australia's
Champion Sprinter.
Photo, by Herbst, Sydney.
boats, that had been reefed down inside, set topsails when
through the Heads. True to her name, Dempster’s Petrel
took the lead after passing St. George’s Head, and climbed
the short steep seas outside splendidly. Handled by A.
C. Saxton, Magic flew along in pursuit, but it was a hope-
less chase, and the Petrel rounded the mark an easy
winner.
* » * *
The gusty nor’-easter, the choppy seas, and the
keenness of the struggle between Vision and Donnelly
made the Sydney Sailing Club’s race for the 18-foot
championship exciting. Donnelly went to the front at
Bradley’s Head, where the Arline promptly went right
over and gave her crew a thorough ducking, and for a time
it looked as though the Vera would also come to grief at
the same spot. Immediately Donnelly got the lead Vision
came in pursuit. At Chowder Head the Qui Vive took
a big cargo of water on board. Vision was then only a
minute behind Donnelly. With small ballooners hoisted
they came shooting up the harbor, the Vision steadily
overhauling the leader till Fort Denison was reached, and
the Vision was only 17 seconds behind her quarry. Don-
nelly rounded like a flash, but the Vision lost time shift-
ing the ballooner, and at the next timing at Shark Island
Donnelly had increased his lead to 25 seconds. Then the
Vision made a supreme effort. Shooting out a big bal-
looner and hoisting a topsail, she fairly flew along, over-
took and then passed Donnelly, drew right away, and
won by 14 seconds. It was a great race.
Of the seven boats that entered for the 14ft.
championship of St. George’s Club (Botany Bay) two did
not start owing to death in the family of the owners, and
a third had to retire from the race on account of the
heavy weather. The N.E. breeze and choppy water made
Saturday an exciting day for pleasure craft. Of the four
that got through the task, Irex got in front early and
stayed there till the finish, with last year’s champion
boat, the Clio, in close attendance, only a minute behind.
* ♦ * *
Thorwald Ellegaard, three times champion
of the world, at present champion of Europe for
the second time, is a tall, fair man with a
serious expression and a keen sense of humor.
At present, conversation with him can only be carried on
with the aid of Mrs. Ellegaard, who speaks English well.
Ellegaard first took to biking at the age of 18, purely for
purposes of pleasure, and for three years raced as an
amateur, and was content with the pewter tea-pot and
the dubious shield. His first professional appearance was
at Copenhagen, when, on the off-chance of doing some-
thing fairly remunerative, he entered for a big meeting
at which the championship of the country was at stake.
When the big race was over, the order was —Ellegaard,
first; Bourelliau (then champion of France), second;
Jacquelin, third ; Arent, fourth. Copenhagen was wild
with delight that night that the national premiership had
been gained by a Dane. It was in Berlin in 1901 that he
first won the championship of the world, and Kaiser Bill
immediately had him brought to the palace under a
strong guard, where he lectured him and presented him
with a silver cannon. The second time he won the great
race it was run in Rome, and King Victor Emmanuel pre-
sented him with a gold watch and chain, the former bear-
ing his initials in brilliants. The third occasion was back
in his own city (Copenhagen), and after the race the
King of Denmark walked up and —shook hands with
him. Ellegaard chuckles softly and requests Mrs. Elle-
gaard to explain that the King is liberality itself, but
spends so much of his meagre allowance on really good
objects like education that he has nothing left for pro-
fessional people who are already well-paid During the
three years he was champion of the world Ellegaard won
178 first prizes, and received good appearance money as
well. He says France is the best country in the world
for the professional cyclist—there are bigger prizes and
more of them than elsewhere. He has raced in Denmark,
Holland, Belgium, Germany, France, Italy, Russia,
England and Australia. Likes the Australian climate
best of all; but, naturally, prefers France for business
purposes' and comfort of living. They order a lot of
things better in France. In Russia the racing season is
the winter, and it is carried on indoors. Ellegaard
raced at both St. Petersburg and Moscow. He thinks
that if Australian cyclists are to make a splash abroad
they will have to give up the long handicap races and go
in for the short sprint. In Europe they will have noth-
ing but the short sprint with the lightning finish. On
the Continent all the cycling is carried on on Sundays.
In Paris Ellegaard gets up to £75 for Sunday appearances
alone, and all the prizes he can win—some of them big
ones. When his Australian season is over he will return
to Europe, and has at present no intention of going to
the U.S.A. He can make more money in the old world.
* * •* •*
“Truth”: Saw crack cyclist Lawson ride his
mile at Sydney Cricket Ground,. Wednesday last week,
and took it with a very reliable watch, which showed over
four seconds slower than the official 1.47 4-5. Others near
me made the time 1.53. Next day met a well-known
Sydney jeweller who has had years of experience in timing
races, and ascertained he had two watches on the sprint
and each made it 1.52|. Evidently nothing like 1.47 4-5
was done.
* * * *
The announcement that the Australian Federal
Cycling Council will hold its next annual meeting in
Sydney has not brought any wild joy to followers of the
sport. So far, the council has done nothing to justify its
existence, and there were many enthusiastic followers of
cycling who were unaware of the existence of the council
till they saw it being used by boodlers to spray the white-
wash over the pie-biters.
Somebody in Melbourne made a desperate
attempt to make the Sydney January cycling carnival a
farce. Many of the best riders were advised that it would
be well to stay away from the Sydney meeting, as the
Sydney Council would disqualify any rider who beat the
fellow who carried the council’s coin. Some actually got
scared and asked for special guarantees that they
wouldn’t be disqualified except under certain conditions.
Sydney Council certainly has its faults, but it is not
quite so cronk or mad as all that. In the end no
guarantees were given, and everybody raced and was
satisfied as far as the control of the meeting was con-
cerned. With all its faults, the Sydney management is
considerably cleaner than that of Melbourne.
* * * *
The final heat of the international race between
George Farley and Thorwald Ellegaard, at Sydney
Cricket Ground on Saturday, proves the Victorian to be as
had as a sportsman as he is great as a rider. Of his ability
to ride a stiff uphill race the first heat was proof absolute.
The great Dane had actually headed him, and left him
nearly a length behind, when, in spite of the speed at
which both were travelling, Farley again jumped level,
and finished half a wheel in front. This startling finish
made for intense excitement in the second heat. Out
went all but the track lights, and, as the pacer dropped
out, Ellegaard streaked away as though hooked to an
invisible motor. Farley went after him, but the Dane
was ten yards away, and although the Australian whirled
along at what seemed a tremendous speed, the Dane
reeled away in front, lengthening the gap between
them every second, till he crossed the line fully
25 yards ahead. It seemed pretty plain that when
Ellegaard put his ears back the Victorian had no hope.
The third and half-mile heat started auspiciously, Farley
trailing the pacer, and apparently waiting till that indi-
vidual should drop out to make his jump. Ellegaard an-
ticipated him, and with another most amazing leap he
passed Farley and the pacer. Farley shot out after him,
then suddenly slowed down and held up his hand. The
way that Ellegaard streaked round that remaining half
lap was wonderful to see. When he dismounted he
learned that Farley had protested that he had been inter-
fered with by Ellegaard crossing in front of him to take
the inside place. Ellegaard was very indignant, as well
he might be, for, as far as could be seen, the protest was
frivolous. Ellegaard crossed to the inside running too far
in front of the Victorian to interfere with him in any way.
The League ordered the heat to be re-run.

Maoriland cyclist Albert G. Campion, having
made up his mind to go in search of foreign capital,
descended on Westralia and won the Westral Wheel Race,
the value of which was a cool £2OO. This was a good
beginning. Probable result will be that Canterbury will
empty itself on to the Australian mainland in search of
lucre at cycle race meetings.

Victorian Injured Cyclist Fund has become
hard up—a fact attributed to the throwing open of the
track at the Exhibition Building, and a consequent heavy
crop of spills. Visiting wheeling stars are co-operating in a
special benefit meeting to be held in April. The Vic.
cyclists can’t have much humor, because they have
decided to run the benefit special off on the Exhibition
track 1
* # # *
Sailor Sharkey, the ’Murkan bruiser, who has
just retired from the ring a rich man, was a sort of
Admirable Crichton in his way. He discovered that he
had stamina, grit, and great hitting power. So he took
to bruising deliberately as a business. He lived carefully,
was a keen business man, paid as much attention to the
collection of the dollars as to the other part of the busi-
ness ; and, with perhaps the exception of his scrap with
Fitzsimmons, always fought square and fought well.
When he knew he was beaten he still fought on gamely
to give the spectators their money’s worth. It was good
business, he said. And it was. Now that he has amassed
sufficient wealth to keep him in what he considers -com-
fort for the rest of his days, he retires from a business
which, he says, had never any particular attraction for him
except as a money-making show.
Published that young Griffo is again on deck in
Chicago (U.S.A.), where he is said to have turned “blue
ribbon,” and to be making a comfortable living teaching.
* * * *
They killed professional bruising in one hit in
Maoriland. The new Act gives the police power to pro-
hibit anyone charging for admission to see a bruising
match, and when the Griffin family went down to Dunedin
to knock spots off the local talent, and thereby raise
wealth to send Jim Griffin to Sydney, the Act was sud-
denly enforced. The promoters took up a collection —in
Dunedin, where even the Chinaman has to call himself
MacPhairson to live. A ghastly array of empty soup-
plates showed them the error of their way, and there’ll
be no more professional bruising in Dunedin.
* * * •
For the man who loves to watch two other
fellows knock pieces off each other, Sydney is just now a
rather dull locality. At Sydney National Sporting Club
last Tuesday night week two very sick men faced each
other and reduced the business to a howling farce. Harold
Smith was suffering from stomachic trouble, and B.
Whitelaw had influenza, and every time Whitelaw
stopped a tap he looked as though someone had
knocked the top off his head. Anyhow, it was a wretched
exhibition, and was brought to an abrupt conclusion by
the gentleman with the ’flu butting the other invalid on
the nose with the top of his head. If he really had influ-
enza—and he fought as though he had all the seven deadly
complaints—no wonder he was anxious to get away from
the ring. Imagine being hit on the nose while suffering
from influenza !
• * * *
The Green-Murphy contest at the Gaiety Club
on Saturday night began with deadly tameness, but in
the fourth round both feather and bantam livened up.
Green’s nose began to assume a bashed appearance, and
Murphy’s whole face showed signs of wear. Later on
Murphy assumed a dazed look, and then Green began to
wear a far, far away expression. Both seemed to stop a
great many blows with the face. After the 19th they
were both very sick, and each waited for the other to
attack, and when the last gong sounded the referee de-
clared it a draw.
* * * *
Announced that Tom Colquitt, the amazing
English lepper with Wirths’ Circus, recently established
an Australian best. Thomas is a wonderful “ lepper ” ;
at Phillip Park (Syd.) he used to leap 20 yards on to the
top of a pile of eggs, and, without smashing any of them,
leap off again on to the surface of a tub of water, and
without making a ripple leap off that on to the face of a
supernumerary. If the supernumerary wasn’t there he
hired a mosquito. His Australian record was established
in a match against Morris Davis, in which he conceded
4|ft. Davis had no hope, and at the last Colquitt
sprang 40ft. 7in.

The biggest importing scare N.S.W. has
had was in 1841-2, when the Orwell sailed into
port with a big cargo of green tea from China. A
rumor got out that the tea was poisoned. After
the Chinese war of 1838 the Chinese resolved on
many curious forms of revenge, and among them, it
was alleged, was the poisoning of all:“barbarians.”
That allegation was the basis: of the green tea
scare. The chests, when opened in Sydney, gave
forth a terrible stench. The tea was, however,
sent inland in great quantities.- The Queensland
(at that time part of N.S.W.)'stations were speci-
ally invaded. Station hands struck work in all
directions; many fled when they heard of the tea
arriving. Really the tea, though smelling horril ly
through bad packing, was not. injurious to health,
and as the stationholders got it almost for nothing
they determined to make their hands use it. A
portion was destroyed, but in the end it went
freely into consumption; and as no deaths weie
caused by using it, the scare gradually died down.
“Kaylee” : Wentworth, out in the back
of N.S.W., where they still make odd efforts to
settle people, is trying to think it’s alive after it
has been dead for years. Besides the prison cells,
it has an elaborate gaol which never has a
prisoner, the four policemen never having an
opportunity to lock anybody up. There is a large
hospital with never any patients, and a fire brigade
with a high look-out and no horses to pull the
reel. Residents practise every evening at pulling
the reel about, in the forlorn hope that some day
there will be a fire.
“ S.G.M.” : One time two friends of mine
worked a eucalyptus still up in the Dandenong
Ranges (Vic.). Hearing of a man in the vicinity
in the last stage of consumption, they offered to
cure him, and one night they carried him off to
the camp. Taking the top off the large boiler in
which the eucalyptus leaves were boiled, they sus-
pended the consumptive over the fumes, and sat
smoking, watching him. Somehow they fell
asleep, and forgot their patient. Towards inid-
night one awakened, and realised, with a thrill of
horror, that the patient was still simmering over
the boiler. They cut the consumptive down, and,
thinking they had done for him. the distillers of
cough-mixtures took to their tent, and in a trice
had gathered'their belongings and scooted. It
was not till some years after they heard that their
patient had realiy been cured, and was doing well.
Once more the advertising columns of the
“ great dailies ” are sprinkled with advts. :
“Governess wanted, teach all English subjects,
drawing, painting, advanced music, violin, needle-
work, &c. Salary, £2(5 a year. ’ Some of these
unfortunate sweated creatures in Victoria have
plucked upenough courage to talk about their griev-
ances in the press and to have the audacity to
suggest the formation of a union to keep up prices.
Governesses, women clerks (and men clerks for the
matter of that), have nothing to hope for from the
Tory employers who flatter them with the adjec-
tive “genteel” and then sweat them dismally,
presuming that their “gentility” will prevent
them from protesting. Let them go straight
for unionism and radicalism in politics and they
will find work conditions becoming better.

A man was shifting a stack of drain-pipes
at 58 Queen’s Bridge-st., South Melbourne, the
other day, when a grey rabbit ran out of them and
into the street, then doubled back again into the
railway yard, and hid in a heap of old machinery at
the back, and themen have not interfered with him
since. He had probably heard of the poor farmers
of Collins-st. (whom the Victorian Land Tax Act
saves from exaction) and had come to sample their
crop"-.
In prosecuting a S. Australian waterworks
superintendent for embezzlemt nt, the Jenkins
Govt, showed itself up as a swi ater of the worst
description. The amount alleged to have been
embezzled was small; and all that was proved was
that the overworked official, who had to personally
get in rates amounting to £3OOO annually, had
overlooked entering one or two items. One of the
sums was traced —it had been forwarded to the;
Kept, in Adelaide though not entered in the heel
of the book. The officer, working the whole con-
cern single-handed, had had no leave for 10 years ;
his working day averaged 12 hours; and he hau
to walk all over a huge district hunting up tho
rates. The Bench unanimously found him not
guilty, but unfortunately suppressed its opinic n of
the prosecutior.

SATISFACTION GUARANTEED,
J.A.K.”: Know a Melbourne lady with
a quiver full who has had experience of the diffi-
culty of getting a suitable house, owing to owners’
objections to encumbrances. Now she sends her
youngsters to see the Springthorpe monument
while she interviews the .owner or the agent.
“Family?” “ Yes, poor dears,” she says,'“six
of them ; and they’re all in the cemetery.”
“R.M.” : Whatever you do, teach ’em to
read ; thus you give them a chance to get away
from themselves. Worked on a farm one time.
Sunday : I’m reading. Day not long enough.
But some of the cookies wouldn’t read, or couldn’t
read, and they, all day : “ Ain't Sunday a long
day?” “ Ain’t Sunday a cow of a day ? ” “A man
d be better at work.”
Although a recent Maoriland Act provides
that a private application may be made for a pro-
hibition order against a person, nothing was done
in the more urgent matter of the establishment of
Children’s Courts. Government probably thought
that it would mean great additional expense, and
shied at the proposal. Yet the Auckland beak
has ushered in the reform without the slightest
fuss by trying all cases against youngsters in his
private room. This could easily be copied through-
out the State without any expense whatever.
“ Bunny” : Bought butter in five different
shops in Broken Hill, only to find that a labelled
pound weighs 13 or 14 ounces. When I com-
plained of short weight, I was informed that the
curious climatic conditions of B. H. caused the
shrinkage. Shrinkage is nearly as common a
disease locally as typhoid. In fact I have begun
to shrink myself. One would not mind if one’s
bills shrink proportionately, but they don’t.
Before things get any more tangled, the
G.P.O. ought to make a rule to refuse a P.O. to any
place which has the same, or - nearly the same,
name as any post-office town already in existence
in the Commonwealth. The rule adopted in
U.S.A. (although only as regards each State) has
saved a lot of trouble and no end of time to P.O.
sorters. It might have been lately used to stop
the stupid alteration of the name of Lad Robin-
son’s Beach to Brighton-le-Sands, seeing that
already Victoria, South Australia, and Tasmania
own a Brighton apiece.
Thus “ Spare-Me-Days ” :—•
The examination farce is about the hollowest yet stage-
managed by N.S.W. Public Service Board. Writer, after
a lifetime’s experience, is convinced that the worst officers
are those who pass highest in examinations. They mostly
use the service as a means to live until, by attending Uni-
versity night classes, they can qualify for a profession.
The smartest officers now in the service—Lockyer, Collec-
tor of Customs ; Curry, President of Land Board ; Saun-
ders, Under-Secretary for Finance and Trade ; Brodie,
Chief Inspector of Accounts ; Charlie Gregory, the ac-
knowledged ablest accountant in the Federal service—all
entered as cadets at from 13 to 15 years of age. They
have not yet competed for the silly honor instituted by
the P.S.B. of dux of the service. If they did some junior
would probably beat them hollow.
“ H.E.R.” : In Perth, there is an unlucky
shop. It was half burned down once. Then one
night a big water-pipe burst, and poured a few
hundred gallons all over the silks—a milliner had
the lease then. This broke the milliner. Six
people in one year tried to start a business there,
and failed. Among them were a tailor and hatter
(he went mad), a bootmaker, a land agent, and a
fruiterer. There was also a fortune-teller, and a
head-reader, and a renowned herbalist. Lately, a
man filled the window up with magazines, and
called it a “ bookstall.” To-day the window is
empty again, and a fine, big, fat Jew is talking to
the landlord and smiling. Perhaps he’ll open a
pawnshop, and call it “Young Australia Loan
Office.”
A Methodist “circuit,” in one of the best-
favored southern parts of S. Australia, had a 50th
anniversary, the other day ; and the connexional
historian furnished the “circuit’s” chronicles,
which contained this ghastly fragment of fact: —
At Corn Hill, Springbank, and Carracalinga, the old
churches are now used as barns, &c. No use can lie made
of the ruin at Talisker, as only one house in the region is
occupied ; and, apart from the lighthouse-keeper’s, there
is only one; family at the Cape, where formerly 17 families
lived. The depopulation in many parts of tlie district is
saddening.
Adelaide papers—the Register especially—are
always talking about “our inheritance in the
Hills” ; and this district is on the southern and
well-watered fringe of those Hills. S. Australia
entered upon its inheritance 50 years ago; and
one family living now where formerly 17 lived is
the proud record of its achievement.
On a recent voyage of the Orizaba one of
the colored firemen handed in his checks at Fre-
mantle, and his comrades obtained permission to
bury him ashore, which they did with great cere-
mony. The corpse had been a true believer of some
sect or other, and according to his sectarian bill-
of-fare, should have been burnt, but circumstances
were not favorable. It is the custom, when a
Hindu is to be burnt, for four of his friends to
carry him to the burning grounds. One of the
rites of the religion is that, should the funeral
procession pass a pub, all hands must liquor up
for the good of the departed spirit. If a pub is
passed without this being done terrible things will
happen to the bearers. On one occasion which the
writer remembers the day was hot and the “stiff”
was an extra heavy-weight, consequently when a
pub was reached the four bearers indulged in long
sleevers of bhang. There were quite a number of
houses along the route, and the bearers blunged
at each one. When half the distance had been
covered the four were run in, and having left the
deceased lying on the .roadway while they re-
freshed, the police had to take it to the pyre and
finish it off. i

A S’African paper publishes a 10-line para-
graph under the heading “A Peculiar Mistake,”
telling how a Kaffir, charged with attempted
criminal assault on one of the same race, was sen-
tenced to receive 25 lashes, and, on being stripped
at the triangles, was discovered to be a woman.
Following the Australian custom nothing hap-
pened to the dusky damsel who laid the eharge.

TO A HAT THIEF.
A BRAND-NEW FELT HAT was “ exchanged ” at
a local hotel last Thursday, and has not been
returned. If the present owner should escape hang-
ing, it is the late owner’s devout wish that the light-
ning from above may strike through the hat into his
miserable skull, and convince him that he is a low-
down thief and a disgrace to an honest hat.
“S.L.H.”: He introduced himself as a
phrenologist, and asked me in a confidential and
persuasive whisper to let him read my head and
tell me by what profession I should endeavor to
keep myself in cigarettes and the latest thing in
ties. As stern necessity had already decided this
detail for me, I refused. Then he warnted to read
my wife’s character; but I reckoned that after
five years I knew it pretty well already, arid that
an outlay of half-a-crown wasn’t justified. Next
he suggested running his hand over the servant
girl’s cranium—just to satisfy me that, the spoons
and an imitation silver mug were safe in her
charge. After that he told me a pitiful tale of his
sick wife and 75 children (I think he said 75, but
it might have been 74), and finished up by borrow-
ing 3d. The last I saw of him he was leaning
over the next-door fence, beseeching the lady of
the house to allow him to relate the future des-
tinies of her dog, or fowls, or husband, or some-
thing.

North-Western Associated, Kalgoorlie, is turn-
ing out better than most people expected. At 400 ft, the
lode has been driven on over 200 ft. and is payable, lhe
pay chute apparently becomes longer at depth ; in the
oxidised zone it *vas very short. Concern is largely run
by London guinea-pigs, who have been trying to boom
scrip of late. Tributors have, during recent years, done
well, but shareholders have had no return ; and it is
doubtful if they ever will, under present control.
* * * *
Kalgurli (W.A.) makes a very good showing for
its financial j r ear ended July 26. Co. realised a profit of
£82,500, and distributed £60,000 in divs. From next
month co. will increase its output from 5400 to 8500 tons,
and its profit should run into something like £lBO,OOO a
year or 150 per cent, on the capital issued. No fuither
expenditure on plant is anticipated, so all surplus revenue
can be devoted to* divs. Ore reserves are estimated at
365,000 tons, worth'OOs. This would mean 43 months’ sup-
ply, and over £5 a share profit. But owing to the variable-
ness of the lodes, a proper estimate of gold contents is
practically impossible ; the best that can be said is that
manager Moss generally allows a reasonable margin foi
contingencies. Co. is sure to pay ss. a share quarterly.
Total costs on mine are now about 255., including ‘develop-
ment, and, with increased plant running, should not
exceed that amount, even including London exes, and
depreciation. „
* * * *
Westralia Mt: Morgans is very dicky, and may
wholly close down any time. If it does it will be largely
the result of mismanagement. Mine, like Sons of Gwalia
in the early days, was over-run by cuffs and collars ; now
all the best ore in the available stopes has been depleted,
and the stuff remaining is not worth more than 7dwt.
Mine may come again later on when economy, is the rule.
It is absurd that a mine with easily treated ore should run
into 41s. to 4‘2s. expenses, when Kalgurli, even with
roasters, has reduced costs to 255. and under.

Murchison Associated, Day Dawn (W.A.), has
had another farcical year, in 'spite of che gaudy predic-
tions of its sponsors. In the balance-sheet equipment
flo-ures at £20,000, and development at £36.000, although
it is very doubtful; if one ton of pay ore is in sight to
represent this alleged asset. Co. is said to have £10,500
in cash yet directors are issuing 100,000 new ss. shares at
par- if they can fin'd any to take them. Half this issue is
guaranteed by four of the biggest shareholders. Concern
has been going in one form or another for ten years, and
has been several times reconstructed.
Hainault, Kalgoorlie, showed a profit of £lOOO
during Dec., equivalent to ss. per ton, which is a very
small margin. Main shaft is deepening very slowly, pro-
bably to limit expenses and show a profit. Average grade
of ore is barely 7dwt, hut developments are fully two
years ahead of'mill Six levels are being worked, and ore
'is being drawn from each. Co. looks pretty hopeless, and
not likelv to pay .more than Is. a share per year on its
issued capital of ,£121,000. Lionel Robinson and Co.’s
year’s option at par on the remaining 9000 shares expired
on December 31, and, as shares never got within coo-ee of
par, it is safe to say the option was allowed to lapse.
Some big slugs continue to come from the head
of the Kanowna firewood tramline. Chris. O’Brien in one
day picked up two weighing 112 oz. and 1540 z. O’Brien
and his mates had decided to give their claim best after
six weeks’ fruitless work, and struck these big slugs on
the very day they intended to quit. The locality is
promising, but, like Kurnalpi and most of the rich alluvial
fields of W.A., the source of the gold seems impossible to
locate.

Ivanhoe, Kalgoorlie, is erecting an 050h.p.
en"-ine to drive the 100-head mill, as the old engine is over-
loaded The new engine should be the best on the fields,
and should help the output slightly. This will complete
the programme of surface equipment. On the basis of
17,000 tons a month, mine’s ore reserves are three years
ahead of mill, and are being kept well ahead. Profits in
sight equal fully £4 a share, and the mine, so far as one.
can judge, has a long life before it. It has the most
regular lodes on the field.
Princess Royal North, Dundas (W.A.), long
regarded as a wild cat, is, according to the management,
goin°* ’to prove it isn’t. The main shaft has been
sunlT3Bsft., and two levels opened up at 250 ft. and 380 ft.
At 250 ft. about 350 ft. of driving has been done before
locating pay ore. Recently a pay chute, or dab, has been
located, and is being followed by a winze, the width
rangin' l - from 10in.lt-> 4ft., the average value being .given
as 17djvt. At 3SOft. abbut 120 ft. of driving has been done
in poor ore, but Manager Creer predicts that he will cut
the continuation of the pay chute from the 250 ft. level by
driving south. There is nothing muph to get excited
about, so far, hut the Adelaide crowd, who control the
show are certain to make the most of the development in
the market.. .Co.’s Desirable lease hasn’t yet justified its
acquisition. When co. ordered its 10-head battery some
two years ago, show was declared a fraud, as there was
nothing to crush, and so it has proved.

Briseis (Tas.) is at last at work on the drift in
Briseis Hill, and just on the Brothers No. 1 boundary is
o-ettino- 61b. of black tin to the cub. yard. This is a long
way short of the 1 per cent. (331b5.) dirt predicted in the
flotation stages of the show, and it remains to be seen if
the Brothers No. 1 will help the situation. At present
the workings left by the old miners at the last-named
show are being uncovered. What everyone wants to find
out is just how far said workings extend. #
“ Gr H.” : A Pederal party lately inspected the
Heskett-Moore iron-making process, which claims to do
awav with pig-iron, Bessemerising, puddling, and all the
other ancient British processes. The ore is fed in at one
end of the furnace, and first-class malleable iron or steel,
according to the inventor, comes out at the other. Heskett
is an ironworker from Middleborough, U.K., and has been
laboring over this invention in M.L. for many years. He
claims to have produced excellent steel in one operation
from M.L. ironsand ; and, certainly, the tools shown were
of A 1 quality. I had a man striking with a sledge on a
Heskett-Moore cold chisel, and the implement rose a
curling strip of iron as thick as a rooster’s comb.
The furnace will only > treat finely-crushed ore,
and a dynamo extracts the gangue and impuri-
ties for a start. Then the ore is heated up
in a preliminary furnace, preparatory to reaching the
reducing chamber. The r.c. is a huge revolving steel
cylinder, with shelves running horizontally along its inner
side, and a lining of fire-brick over all. The semi-molten
iron’dust falls from one shelf to another inside this re-
volving machine, and injected fuel raises the temperature
very high. Below there is another cylinder, in which the
ore is finally turned into malleable iron or steel. Electric
indicators record the internal heat, and by raising or
lowering the temperature the operator can produce steel
or iron as he pleases. If this invention will work on a
lar°-e scale, then Australia will have a big thing in hand,
for” Yankees and Scotchmen have vainly spent fabulous
sums trying to find means of doing away with the crude
and costly pig-iron process. A company is running the
affair at present, and wants cash to enable it to establish
works on a larger scale. It is up to the Commonwealth,
which badly wants an iron and steel making industry of
its ojvn, to pay the closest attention to the Heskett-
Moore proposition.
