The Westralian Timber combine has started to
compete in N.S.W. by introducing karri and jarrah, to
fight local hardwoods. But, instead of welcoming trade,
as they generally do, the Telegraph and other papers are
scoffing at the hardihood of the Westralian people in
bringing this timber, dressed, to sell in Sydney. Compe-
tition to defeat the local manufacture of any finished pro-
duct they would welcome ; but to interfere with the
crude business of chopping down a tree makes the D.T.
very angry
The Bear-Monkey war has, up to date, advan-
taged the A 1 people more than any British firm,
the premiums paid to Lloyd’s underwriters having
run already into millions sterling. During November
more than 30 vessels were covered to Vladivostock
alone. The average tonnage was about 4000, and the
premiums paid, £lO,OOO. For that month no claims had
been made.
In scarcely two mines does the thermometer
Show the same increase when it is lowered down towards
Where Hades is supposed to be. At one Bendigo mine,
where fairly elaborate tests were made, the average
increase down to 3250 ft. was 1 degree Pahr. for every
135 ft. The increase was, curiously, more rapid near the
surface (1 degree in every 100 ft. for the first 500 ft.) than
at greater depths (1 degree for every 110 ft. near the
bottom). But at another Bendigo mine another scientist
reported an increase of 1 degree for every 77Jft. down to
3645 ft. In the Comstock lode (U.S.A.) 1 degree in every
30ft. was reported—the most awful figures on record.
At the Calumet and Hecla copper mines 1 degree in
227 ft. was reported. On the average figures, it seems
that the miner who works at 3500 ft. may expect to sweat
in an impure atmosphere 45 degrees hotter than the mean
at the surface (usually about 60 degrees). And yet men
crowd one another to do this work. Plenty of the
shareholders in whose mines this takes place are quite sure
that white men can’t work in the tropical industries of
the northern fields. White men have often been chal-
lenged by the northeners who love the Kanaka to go into
the cane-field and work beside the Kanaka, and see what
will happen. Why not turn the table, and ask the
northeners to see how their Kanaka would curl up if he
worked alongside the white miner at 3800 ft. or so V
“Mrs. Be van, wife of the woolly-headed Con-
gregational parson who loves the Chinaman, has
been ill, and her medical advisers ordered a long
sea-voyage. So the parson of the posas, nothing
loth, books immediate passages for himself and his
sick mate by an Orient boat. Mrs. Bevan will
probably have to wear a hat travelling, and that
she won’t like. The dear old lady drapes her
head and shoulders ordinarily in real laces, and
looks for all the world like an ‘ arrangement ’ by
Whistler, a symphony in black and silver. Her
only vanity is to look picturesque when doing
good, and as she does much good the vanity can
pass.
“Little bandbox Captain Collins is much
pleased and flustered over the cabled news of his
boy’s entrance pass (the exam, is a stiff one) to
' the ltoyal Military Academy in the Cold Country.
Several lads from these parts are this year in
training for that Woolwich pass, most of them
sons of officials who are committed to a gore and
glory career.
“ A stalwart and rather handsome young sister
of Florence Young is back of her at present in the
chorus. This is Gladys—the last of the Youngs
to be disposed of. By the way, the most stately
unit in the ‘Silk’ society team just now is Miss
Georgia Musgrove, and the most voice-gifted in
the chorus a little scrap of a Jewess (pretty at
that), Eileen Lyons.
“ The coming concert star (of Williamson’s im-
portation) found her platform name—Burkina —in
a curious way. A Marchesi student, she was out
at a Paris musicale with some chums, and sang at
the hostess’ bidding. A big critic of one of the
newspapers immediately asked her name. A
spiteful English miss said ‘ Porkina, identifying
the young Yank with the prosperous pork trade
of her country. By a misprint it appeared next
day as ‘ Parkina.’ The Yank heard how it all hap-
pened, and stuck to the name.
“By the next out-going German liner Miss
Rubenstein sets sail for London. The pretty
Viennese authority on feminine rose-bloom and
lily-radiance goes to Europe to garner extra know-
ledge for the vain sex from the most forward skin
specialists of the world.
“ A stray polo match or two helps the north
wind to scatter the dust at Kooyong and Moonee
Valley this January. So far none of the matches
has drawn a crowd. Half the sporting world is
mopping its moist brow at Macedon or Morning-
ton. Almost another half is in extremity as to
what part of the landscape is at the moment in
flames. Odd poloists have yet other cares :
Everard Browne, the Colac champion, is away
globe-trotting ; and Lachlan McKinnon is honey-
mooning.
“Melbourne took its unprovoked and most
abrupt heat wave last week in absurd fashion.
When the mercury shot up to 115° Melbourne got
real mad.' It bought a pine-apple and some
steamed peaches off the carts, and rushed home
in a rage. And it stayed home for four Sol-run-
riot days. Half the community thought'the other
half dead. No one ’phoned, no one wrote, no one
blackguarded Bent, no one lied or stole. .Only
the baby-murderer had energy left, some infant
bodies being found in the river with stone pen-
dants round their necks.
“ The tragic loss of cyclist champion Ellegaard’s
only child—a sunny-haired boy of two—was a
shock to many Melb. people who had noted the
couple’s worship of the youngster. The cyclist
and his pretty Swiss wife were extraordinarily
fond of the boy. I have seen them at the Grand,
as they played baby games with little Victor.
The champion was never too tired to amuse him.
Madame Ellegaard, while here, wore some gorgeous
Paris frocks, but nothing seemingly gave her more
delight than to see the child reef off the costliest
laces in a burst of good spirits.
“A certain very talented dame of Australian
fame was asked recently why she didn’t get a
divorce from her husband, from whom she lives
mostly apart. She closed one eye cautiously, and
whispered—‘l am keeping him for company in
my old age.’
“One of the bush-fire tragedies: A property
was completely destroyed, and the family left
without a piece of furniture or a change of hand-
kerchiefs. Included in the loss was a very beauti-
ful trousseau. The daughter of the house was to
have been married with due fuss this week.
“There’s one thing about the dames of wool-
wealth. Their conqern about the cattle and stock
is as real as their anxiety about the barns and
station residence. They are mostly good sports,
and their interest is with every kind of useful
quadruped. For the last fortnight lots of squatters
(back from Mountains and sea-side) hang about
town within call of the wires, and their wives are
not far off. No amusement counts when horses
and stock are in danger.
“ Mr. Williamson Wallace, the leisurely Govt,
director of the Grain and Wool Department, is
packing up to board a February liner, for England.
Private affairs require his attendance in the Big
Fog. There is a Mrs. Williamson Wallace, a
dark, strenuous dame, mainly noted for a beautiful
raven thatch just threatened with silver.
“ The death of Montagu Pym (a name Dickens
might have coined) leaves Exchange and Mel-
bourne Club circles less a notable figure. The
deceased beau had the heavy, ponderous swagger
style of an ex-Guardsman, and was one of the
politest and most punctilious business men in the
■city. He leaves a widow and a large farpily of
little Pyms.
“ Outside this State there is only one Nat in the
memory of man —Nat Levi. Consequently when
I wrote last week of Miss Thornley being the
daughter of the late Hon. Nat, the autocrat
who sets up the type took it upon himself to add
Levi —thinking it naturally followed. It didn’t.
Thornley was the missing word.
Yours Affectionately, Akenehi.
AT POVERTY POINT.
A Holy City waxworks show announced the addition of
William Bontield to its collection the day after his execu-
tion. Yet it is said that Adelaide is slow.
Nellie Stewart announces that now she never reads the
papers, and that she wonders whether she will be acting 1
when she is 60. Well, when any public personage starts
to not read the papers it is a sign that —er —maturity is not
far away.
“ Hamlet ” : Am glad old Howard Vernon, after many
hardships, is getting a decent show on a decent stage.
Saw him in the provinces a couple of years ago in Rip \ an
Winkle under his own banner. ’Twas a pathetic sight.
The house was almost empty, and the only decent
supporter he had was his son, Victor Prince. The female
parts were taken by inexperienced girls, who were borne
heavily down by them, but, who, nevertheless, battled
manfully—or rather womanfully—against the inevitable.
Vernon worked like a stone-cracker on piece work, and
when, he, as Rip, went into his long sleep, his voice could
he heard leading the chorus
Peter Dawson, the young South Australian basso, is
said to be doing well in ,London. During the Christmas
season he sang in four performances of the “ Messiah,”
twice in London and twice in Glasgow.
The manager of the “ Ben Greet Co. of English
Players” writes from ’Frisco that it contemplates a
descent upon Australia with the morality play “ Every-
man,” a miracle play, “The Star of Bethlehem, ’ and
Shaksperian comedies “ played in the Elizabethan man-
ner.”
“ 0.K.” : It's a bad look-out for Australia when one can
read on the front sheet of a metropolitan evening journal
(Melbourne Hkr.VLd) a telegram from Warrnambool
announcing that the A.N.A. competitions are still in pro-
gress, and that Mr. Somebody scored 70 points in the
comic song class for his rendering ef “ It Wasn’t Long
before I Shifted,” whilst Mr. Somebodyelse took second
prize for “ I Wouldn't Like to go Halves in That.” What,
a discouragement to humdrum industry is the “fame”
bestowed on A.N.A. competitors by newspaper para-
graphs 1 There’s a time coming when this country’s
public business will be wholly conducted by amateur
comic singers and reciters.
1 << q « fy, 0 a reco g n ition of the fine work of the chorus
under Leon Caron at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, should be
added a word of warning : Get your brass to phrase and
leave off that abominable tootling of a succession of notes
as if they had no possible relation to one another. This
want of phrasing is peculiar to brass bands and the brazen
section of most orchestras. The wood-wind players are
generally well educated on the point, which should form
one of the three R’s in music, but the ignorance of the
cornet and trombone tootlers is sublime. It is really time
conductors bestowed a little attention to these gentlemen
to drill into them what they should have learnt from their
first masters.
Foldesy, the’cellist, who leads J. 0. Williamson’s Con-
cert Co. "in Australia next month, is the son of a member
of the Buda Pesth Opera House orchestra. He displayed
much interest in music from the first, and his father
attempted to teach him the violin. But Foldesy
developed that tired feeling in an aggravated form. He
refused decidedly to stand up to practice, so his father
- bought him a chair and a ’cello, and thenceforward all
was peace. The hoy made wonderful progress under the
best Continental masters, and at seven made his first
appearance in public. From then until now his career
has been notable. Australia will hear him at the zenith
of his power.
“ Ku Klux ” : “ Jika’s ” par. re Prince’s Court is a trifle
inaccurate. I am behind the scenes at present, and
know.’ Takings for the first week were £B6O, not £5OO
a day, and so far that week is the record. Shares are
held'by (amongst others) Tom .Bent qind John Forrest.
The syndicate proposes to spend £6OOO on a new theatre
on the site of Fitzgerald’s circus when the lease of that
building expires, which will be in about ten months. At
the end of fifteen years the property all falls into the
hands of Melbourne City Council. But (it will be a huge
frost long before then.
“ F.E.P.W.” : In visiting a large musical competition re-
cently with a ladies’ choir I noticed that almost without
exception the sopranos were rapid and constant talkers.
Contraltos, on the contrary, are inclined to be soulful.
I will get my intended wife to sing. If she is not a con-
tralto I’m off her.
“ Hamlet the Mummer ” : Things have been pretty bad
for us mummers this winter, and Lord knows when they’re
going to brighten up. Fisher has just come home
(“home” is a small boarding-house in Fitzroy, Melb.),
and has thrown himself on his bed, and is talking in-a
savagely pessimistic strain. “ Have I got anything?
Nice question to ask ! I’ve finished with asking managers
for jobs—finished with ’em, by the holy Shake I have 1
They’ll come to me in future, not me to them ; I’ll see ’em
blanked first, my oath I will ! I went to Bullson to-day,
and said, ‘ Good day,-Bullson. I hear yer forming a com-
pany. Are yer full up?’ ‘Yes,’ he yelled, ‘full up to
the blessed neck,’and slammed the door in my face. Thfen
I went to Jingle’s, and found him upstairs in his office, -in
a raging temper, and when I asked, ‘Can yer give us a
start, sir?’ he snarled, ‘ Yes, to the top o’ the stairs. ;If
yer not gone in five seconds I’ll kick yer down!’ I
strolled down to the Royal, and watched the crowd
struggling for engagements in the panto. ; but I’d
had enough of pantos., so came away. Last year’s
job—remember it—elephant’s hind-legs. Damn hot work
that was for 15s. It’s a confounded indignity for an
actor to have to come down to pantomime—hanged if
I’ll do it again. Didn’t strike a soul in Bourke-street wjio
asked me if I had a mouth—people are either darned
mean, or very poor now ! When an actor’s in work and
got money people drown him in drink ; but when he’s down
on his luck no one inquires if he’s got a mouth that is
thirsty, and if he tells ’em voluntarily, they rush off to
keep an appointment, or feign deafness. As I couldn’t
get anyone to shout, I went into a pub. myself, and as I
entered I heard one barmaid say to another, ‘ Here’s
Fisher, put’em under the counter,’ and I saw the girl
grab a plate of pasties, and other eatable things and put
’em out of my reach, leaving me a tough bit o’ bullock" to
hack at. I don’t "know how people expect an actor-to
keep honest, I’m darned if I do,” muttered Fisher to him-
self, as he turned his face to the wall. “ Thank God, I’m
still honest,” he concluded, sinking into a noonday doze,
and dreaming he was on tour again, dodging landlords,
and drinking good beer.
Irving proposes to start his stage farewellings 'in
America next September. America has of recent years
adopted the old man for its own representative actor, and
he will spend a full seven months in touring from San
Francisco to New York. Returning to England he will
begin a final London season in April, 1906, and make his
last appearance on his theatrical jubilee night. Irving
played his first speaking part in 1856. He had not bden
long on the stage when Charles Dickens Spotted him as a
peculiarly powerful actor and predicted his future fame,
but the big hit in “ The Bells ” did n’t come until he was
a man of 35.
The biggest pest that touring concert companies have
to put up with in backblocks towns is the local musical
genius, his relatives and' his friends. Bill, the genius,
pays the lowest admission price, stands just inside the
do ir’Yay, and- makes loud observations about the in-
capacity of the performers, especially the one who occu-
pies that place on the programme that he thinks he
should have himself. Also, liis father comes along in a
maudlin condition of bad beer, and hangs outside the/shOw,
and explains to the doorkeepers and the assembled hood-
lums that the fellow singing inside isn’t a patch on his son
Bill. If remonstrated with, he offers to put up half-a-
sovereign to match Bill against the visitor in a vocal
catch-as-catch-can, and finally states that even Bill isn’t
essential for the purpose of downing the other, as he
could do it himself, even if he is drunk, Bill’s genius being
inherited from him. Forthwith he proceeds to give a for-
tissimo illustration of his singing capacity, interlarded
with hiccupy appeals to the onlookers to say whether
that doesn’t knock spots off the bloke on the platform.
Then he gets firmly and impolitely shoved away from
the front of the concert hall, but he finds his way round
to the back, and his voice can presently be heard blending
more or lesS with that of the principal soprano. If the
manager of the show really wants to get rid of the local
Bill’s old man, the only w;lv to do it is to take him
in hand at an early stage of the proceedings, and make
him so drunk that he can’t lie down without holding on.
“T.D.”: The Jews, who own most things in London
now, have a lien over the fine arts. It isn’t nearly so
necessary to have merit as it is to get a Jewish syndicate
to boom yau. Most people who haven’t heard violinist
Kubelik, for instance, are under the impression that he is
the nearest approach to a Paganini now living, this
impression having been created and assiduously fosteied
by the artful Hebraic crowd who have the handling of
that more or less soulful wielder of the bow. And Kubelik
draws big houses where men like Ysaye and Sarasate, who
are both magnificent artists still, are always dubious as to
their chances.
Ah, my Beloved, fill the Cup. You may
just as well fill it with the purest spirit available.
Wolfe’s Schnapps is the liveliest stimulant and
best tonic known for mind or body. If your back
aches it may be your kidneys— Wolfe’s Schnapps
cures kidney diseases.*
The Bulletin has received from the publishers,
Messrs. W. H. Sproull & Co., of Newcastle, Knaggs’
Almanac and Inter-State Ports Guide. For such as go
down to the sea in ships and come up from it again in safety,
the Almanac is a store of information which cannot he
overlooked. It contains sailing directions for Torres
Straits, charts of Newcastle Harbor, N.S.W. coastline,
showing lighthouses, etc. ; and is stuffed full of general
information of utility to shipping people. *
The Melb. Christian Students’ Union,
whatever that may be, is now holding a summer
school at Healesville, and, last week, six of the
most earnest workers in the blessed cause failed
to turn up to tea. After tactfully ascertaining
that no girl visitors were missing from the local
hotels, the conductors of the school gave the alarm
and Healesville was plunged into a state of excite-
ment sending out search parties. The people-who
ran the school did all they could to effect a rescue.
Nevertheless, they couldn’t help thinking what a
fine advertisement it would bo for Christian
Unionism generally if, in a couple of months or
so, six bodies were found in attitudes of prayer.
However, the efforts proved successful.

ONE of the Uses to which
THE GRAMOPHONE may be put.
Nowhere is the Gramophone heard to greater advantage than when played across the
water on a still summer’s evening. All the tone and quality of the great Artistes’ voices come
gently swelling over the echoing water. There is no need to breathe the vitiated air of tne
crowded theatre or concert hall. On the river bank where there
is peace the Gramophone brings you the best music you could
hear from far-off cities.
There are people still, who, having heard some
old-fashioned and long out-of-date talking
machine, think that the Gramophone is
inartistic.
Play the Gramophone in the wrong key, or
play Records which are entirely out of sympathy
with the audience, and possibly even to-day
you will create a bad impression with the
Gramophone.
BUT—play the Gramophone properly as a
musician would, what is the result ? You delight
the musical critic and you entertain your friends.
Who shall criticise the Gramophone when
Edward Lloyd writes :
“I am content that future generations shall
judge my voice by the Gramophone.”
On receipt of a postcard we will send
Catalogues of Gramophones and Records
and the name and address of the nearest
dealer in our Gramophones. Gramophone
Records, and genuine Gramophone Needles,
sold only in metal boxes with our trade -
mark picture, “His Master’s Voice,” on
the lid.

(By a Sydney Reporter.)
As the following case achieved un-
usual notoriety it is proper that the
main facts should ho put before the
public in complete form. We are in-

21 Redfern-street, Redfern, for the nar-

this statement by a reporter
“I have been informed that about
thirteen years ago you caused general

ticulars V”
“ttilh pleasure,” acquiesced Mrs.
Lawes. “Yes; it's all thirteen years
since my jilness, for it was after my
first child was born that 1 sank into
such a pitifid state of weakne s that
for six weary weeks I could not put
my foot to the ground. The nurse who
attended me told mother she thought
1 was going into a decline, and you
phoiuld have seen me then. You would
have thought so, too. Talk about
feebleness ! it was awlul. I had a lit-

would you believe that 1 didn't have
enough strength to push him olf V
“At the end of six weeks were j 7 ou
any better ?”
“If 1 was it was very slightly, I can

Uric Acid. —The presence of this acid in the blood is the cause of people suffering from Rheu-
matism, Gout, Neuralgia, Bachaehe, Lumbago, Sciatica, Gravel, and Bladder Troubles. The accumu-
lation of uric acid in the blood is a certain indication that the kidneys are acting inefficiently.
Warner’s Safe Cure acts specifically upon the kidneys, and invariably restores those vital organs to
health and activity, when all suffering due to uric poisoning ceases. So potent is Warner’s Safe Curo
that it will even cure Bright’s Disease of the kidneys.
A simple test to make as to the condition of the kidneys is to put some of the urine, passed tha
first thing in the morning, into a bottle or covered glass, and let it stand until next morning. If it is
then cloudy, or if it contains a sediment like briekdust, or if particles float about in it, or it is of an!
unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time should be lost in commencing to taka'

A Sick Call.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THEY step inside the bedroom where I he in
disrepair,
Their looks are a delightful blend of cheer-
fulness and care ;
Slouch hats and silk, straw hats and caps, are
softly laid aside,
And then they tell the lies about the men who
never died.
‘God bless your heart,” says William 8., why
there is Peter Young,
He’s lived for forty years upon the fraction of a
lung.
I met him just the other day and said How do
you stand ? ’
‘l’m blowing the big trump,’ says he, ‘in the
Salvation band.”
“That’s nothing, sir,’’ says Frederick, with cir-
cumstantial air,
“There’s Simmons —all the doctors gave up Sim-
mons in despair;
He had no lungs at all, they said —I’ll give you
names and dates ;
He’s now the finest diver you can find in Torres
Straits.”
All beautiful, translucent lies, and beautifully
meant—
Your friends assume that, being ill, you must be
innocent.
They want to cheer your spirits up and fill your
mind with mirth —
The Bohemian bedside-manner is the queerest
thing on earth.
They sit in semi-circle very solemn by my bed,
And look at me respectfully, although I am not
dead ;
Then quietly they fill their pipes—a freedom
understood —
Bill’s pipe she is a meerschaum, but the rest smoke
cherrywood.
“What ails you, V.J.D.,” they say, “that you
should look so pale ?
The world is full of beauty still, and poetry and
ale !
Your pipe is filled, your glass is filled, we drink
long life to you—
And why, then, should you leave us, as you seem
to wish to do ? ”
In vain I shake my weary head and turn round to
the wall,
They will not let me die like that, or let me die at
all.
They clap a pipe into my mouth, a glass into my
hand—
The Bohemian bedside-manner takes some time to
understand.
I smoke the pipe, I drain the glass, I gasp and
choke for breath;
But five strong men surround me and they have
no fear of death.
I sleep for seven moments, then I wake up with a
start
And hear Sylvester holding forth on Poetry and
Art.
“We get no darned encouragement. Our friend
here on his bed
Is dying, simply dying ” “ Curse the blanky
world ! ” says Fred.
“ Now what’s the reason, gentlemen, why Govern-
ment won’t give
A paltry thousand pounds a year to him that he
may live ? ”
Then William, with the waving curl upon his fore-
head high,
Springs up and says “ That question needs a swift
and straight reply.
What are these blanky statesmen, at whom every-
body laughs ?
Obituary notices and played-out epitaphs ! ”
Says William : “Fred and Tim and Bert, you’ll
have a sweet surprise.
You see the danger-signal that is blazing in my
eyes?
Before my dear old dying friend I would commit
no crime,
But when I was at school I was the terror of my
time.”
They laughed and wrestled round the room, those
gleeful friends of mine,
And then they sang me Luther’s song of women
and of wine :
“ Cheer up ! cheer up ! you will not die,” my kind
Bohemians cried,
They meant to do their best for me ; —at 10 p.m
I died.
N.s.w.
Y.J.D.
A M’martre.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FOR the first half-mile north of the Seine
the surface of Paris rises almost imper-
ceptibly. Then you pass the Opera House
and the Madeleine, and find a hill that would
make a cyclist swear. Your first rest is at a big
broad boulevard that extends along the hillside
like a terrace. This is the Boulevard de Clichy,
the centre of M’martre, the suburb of Paris that
exists mainly for the purpose of upsetting all the
conventions and shocking visitors from respectable
England who go there on purpose to be shocked.
Here, in the centre of the Boulevard de Clichy
stands the world-renowned Moulin Rouge, with
only its externals to perpetuate the memory of the
Moulin Rouge that gladdened the Paris of the
Third Napoleon. The stranger who is looking
for shocks will pass the Moulin Rouge of to-day.
He will not turn to the west, where the end of the
boulevard is blocked by a big octagonal building—
Bostock’s Hippodrome, in which the nightly cir-
cus entertainment ends by the wild beasts being
turned into the arena just as they were in the
Coliseum at Rome ; or in it being filled with water
for a mimic naval engagement. The wise man
will turn to the east. Here lie La Cigale and
half-a-dozen minor music halls, where the dip.
criminating visitor may be shocked to precisely
the extent he desires. On both sides of the broad
boulevard shine the glittering lights of the eccen-
tric cabarets, carefully prepared to outrage all the
conventions. Above all, on the crest of the high
acropolis that overlooks Paris, the basilica of
Montmartre shines snow-white in the moonlight.
The music-halls and the dance-halls and the
cabarets are entered from the street level. The
basilica is reached only by a hard and a laborious
ascent of a very steep hill and a climb of about
500 steps. What an allegory seems to be con-
tained in this.
Opposite the Moulin Rouge, and facing the
distant basilica with shameless defiance, glares the
red demon’s face that marks the entrance to the
Cabaret of Hell and the blasphemous caricatures
of church windows in the Cabaret of Heaven next
door. Past these there is a street as steep as the
streets of Outtrim or Walhalla. In the middle of
a row of shops is a brilliant building with a power,
fully-drawn poster of a guillotine before it. People
are crowding through the doorway. The show is
the “ Mort de Jacques” ; and, as it costs only 25
centimes to see Jacques die, it is ever popular.
There is the inevitable little room with a pro.
scenium at one end. Everybody stands, but the
floor has a dip that provides a good view for all.
The curtain rises, and the guillotine stands black
and sinister against the sky, with a view of Paris
and the towers of Notre Dame on the back cloth.
There is weird music, and Monsieur de Paris and
his two assistants enter, clad in their belltoppers,
frock-coats and tricolor belts. They bring a
basket to the front of the guillotine. Monsieurde
Paris raises the knife and drops it a couple of
times to make sure that all is in order. They re-
tire, and from behind the scenes comes the
murmur of a crowd waiting for blood. 1 hey re-
appear with Jacques, and an attendant print
recites the prayer for the dead. Jacques is a
pallid youth, with his shirt turned down to expose
a scrawny neck. Also he is a born actor. As I e
catches sight of the guillotine, he shivers in such
a way that the audience says “ Oo ! ” and you ha\ e
a sensation of ice-cold water trickling down your
back. He stares at the instrument of death. His
eyes are rivetted to it, and the muscles of his face
work in horrible contortions.
Monsieur de Paris takes his stand by the side of
the guillotine, while his assistants manacle the
wrists and ankles of the trembling Jacques to a
long board. The signal is given. The board is
lowered, and Jacques’s head is seen projecting
beyond the line of the guillotine as he mutters his
final words after the priest. The wooden flap is
*hut down, and Monsieur de Paris pulls the string.
An awful cry, shooting from the mouth of the
criminal, is cut short by the descending blade, and
the head of Jacques topples into the basket with
a spurt of blood. The audience applauds wildly,
and, horrible though the show may be, the illusion s
carried out so perfectly that anybody would be
inclined to say that a mistake had been made and
the youth really beheaded. And when the curtain
falls and we file out, to find .1 acques at the doer
with the everlasting tambourine held out for some-
thing for himself, we are inclined to wish that he
had been.
Further up the hill is the Moulin Galette, where
all M’martre and its girl go to dance. This is the
headquarters of the Apaches of Paris, who cor-
respond with the pushes of our own land. And
the superficial resemblance of the Paris Apache to
the Australian larrikin is remarkable. It is far
more marked than the resemblance of the London
Hooligan. The Apache’s face is brown instead of
pasty-white. His physique is superior, and he
has a well-fed, outdoor sort of look that it is im-
possible to find among the Hooligans. He does
not take the dance so seriously as his Australian
brother. In M’martre, the dance is a means of
am use then t, not. a sacred and solemn function.
Yet it is more decorous than the larrikin hop.
There is no M.C. to give orders with a wealth of
questionable imagery. There is no drunkenness
—at least, not in the dance hall itself ; the stalwai t
officials patrolling the promenade take got d
care of that. There is seldom any violence,
but when it does appear, I am told, it is violence
of a type happily unknown in Australia. Fights
are conducted “all in,” and the appeal to the
k.iife is almost invariable. They dance the
waltz, the quadrille, the lancers, and all the
dances that we know, except the Alberts,
and it is only in “le cakewalk” that .the
ladies make any attempt to emulate the high-
kicking of the ballet. The Apache is a genial,
irresponsible-looking youth, generally with a small
black moustache and a fringe. There is nothing
weedy about him. He is sturdy and well knit
and he wears a soft felt hat or a cloth' ckp and good
clothes, with the trouser '‘cut into the
bell-bottoms that indicate the larrikin instinct all
the world over. Probably if we could mount the
time-machine and go back to a mediaeval combat
between the ’prentices and clubs, we would find
the tights of the former were cut saucy in some
ingenious way. The girls might be some of our
own, ready for Saturday night in Oxford-street,
Sydney, or Smith-street, Melbourne, save for the
fact that they are not so pretty as our Australian
factory hands. They have the same coquettish
air, the same wealth of hair hanging down their
backs, and the same appreciation of the interest
of the stranger. ,
We return to the Boulevard and the Cabaret de
Quatr’z Arts, perplexingly pronounced as if it had
something to do with cats. We pass several
cabarets. The Criminal Cabaret, where you are
admitted through a barred gate to sit in a chain-
ber adorned with relics of past crimes, while
waiters attired as convicts serve the drinks, and
M’martre poets recite original verses glorifying
the author of the latest crime; the Cabaret of the
Conservatorium, in which the best topical songs of
the day are said to be given nightly ; the Cabaret
of the Red Ass, the Cabaret of the Dead Rat,
and outside the charmed circle of M’martre itself,
the Cabaret of the Black Cat, founded originally
by one of Paris’s famous poets, and, for a time,
the centre of artistic and literary life.. But the
Black Cat has degenerated, and to-day it is one of
those places which guides tell you about m
mysterious whispers.
We close the night appropriately with the
Cabaret of Death—death as exemplified at the
Cabaret du Neant—the Cabaret of Annihilation.
Here was a shop-front decked out with black and
purple trappings, and behind it a darkened room
filled with coffins placed on trestles. There were
seats by the side of the coffins, and when we give
our orders the waiter, attired as an undertaker s
assistant, places them silently before us on the
coffin lid. The light becomes gradually brighter,
and we see on the walls large pictures of men and
women in the perfection of youth and health.
Suddenly the lights fall, and, in place of flesh and
muscle, the figures turn to grisiy skeletons.
The head-undertaker whisjjers to us that it is
time to enter the Chamber of death. We form a
melancholy procession down a dark corridor, and
reach a small black room with an upright coffin
standing under its one light. A girl enters in the
costume of a pantomime principal boy—a young
and pretty girl, who gives each individual male visi-
tor a smile all to himself, and takes her stand in
the coffin. A minute passes, and the coffin con-
tains only a skeleton. Another passes, and the
girl is again before us, with brighter smiles than
ever and a half skull extended under our nosea
for contributions.
The clock in a bioscope parlor, which the
Sydney detectives would have closed in five
seconds, points to five minutes to 2, and 2 o’clock
is the hour when all good M’martrois go home to
bed. Even the eccentric cafes of the Boulevard
de Clichy close then. Groups are strolling down
the slopes towards central Paris, and cab loads of
dainty Parisiennes in evening dress flash past us,
all the cabs with pneumatic tyres. The lights fade
from the boulevard, the red arms of the Moulin
Rouge have long been stationary ; the streets are
empty save for the gendarmes working their beats,
and M’martre sleeps the profound sleep born of the
clear conscience.
M‘G.

The Destroyer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cl he raced away down the sunset track,
Beyond the mines and the boom ;
The spray flashed red on her turtle back
To the wlifrr of her engine room.
Her funnels spouted their smoke-plumes black—
She looked the spirit of doom.
Along her sides the wavelets hissed.
As she opened out her speed
They fell astern to snarl and twist,
And writhe in her wake and bleed.
Hers was a force no seas resist,
And she gave them little heed.
Away in the west the red sun sank
To drown in the heaving flood.;
And fast—with never a noisy crank
Or piston rod a-tliud,
Her stern set low in the high wave-bank—
She swam on a sea of blood.
Into the night, when the sun had gone,
The fast Destroyer flew,
And never a side-light gleamed or shone,
As the pale stars grew and grew.
What errand grim did she speed upon ?
Only her captain knew.
Through the sweeping seas she clove a track
Into the blinding gloom—
Stumpy-tunnelled, sinister, black—
She was the Spirit of Doom.
And the keen spray hailed on her turtle-back,
To the throb of her engine-room.
* * * *
Back to our forts the Destroyer crept,
As the dawn rushed in aflame;
Her stacks were blistered, her decks sea-swept,
But she licked her lips as she came;
And she took her place, where her comrades slept.
Like a hound that had killed its game.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
“Captains of Enterprise.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LONDON is the town of the Damned-By-
Waiting—the birthplace of the Hope
Deferred. Never was there before such
a city, and such a people of vast pretension and
little act. You come to London with a scheme,
and in proportion to the soundness of it, so is
the difficulty of success. It is not only the
fact that here, as everywhere, people are
sheep, and will give millions for camel-
dung during a boom as cheerfully as they will
refuse to buy sovereigns for nineteen shillings
during a slump ; the great trouble is, that at every
turn somebody springs up and demands reward
for absolutely no service. Failing the indirect
bribe, these blackmailers inva: iably do their best
to spoil business —so that it is only the man who
bribes most who can get easily through with his
business.
The commercial morality of the newspapers,
especially of the financial press, is shocking to the
Australian, even though he be used to the methods,
of the Australian dailies at a general election.
One of the financial papers, finding the pros-
pectus of a friend’s scheme (when it had really
been underwritten, not by the Rothschilds, but
by Australasian traders), brought to the brokers
the proof of a badly-written, ill-informed article,
which they stated they would publish if the
brokers did not pay fifty guineas. They were told
to “go to and they went back to their
office. Next day the article appeared, but it did
no harm.
I have interviewed many magnates in palatial
offices, and have withered under their scorn when
they spoke of Australian mines ; and went away
believing that now my country was irretrievably*
damned. I did not then know the true inward-
ness of these frauds—that their office furniture
was all their capital, that they knew we were in
the middle of a very able-bodied and long-living
slump, and that, knowing they could do nothing,
they talked contemptuously of Australian mining
to the man with an Australian mine, and coldly
of Africa to the man with a West African con-
cession.
Sometimes they would not touch Australian
ventures on account of Whitaker Wright’s failure ;
sometimes they objected to the drought; oftener
they showed that profitable mining was impossible
in the Commonwealth by reason of all the gold in
the lodes having been seized by the Labor party.
The Labor party was always the trump-card of
these penniless adventurers. Any dreary Londoner
who had heard of Socialism and mixed its meaning
with Anarchy, is ready to prophesy the down-
full of Australia, owing to the Labor party, and
the London correspondent of the Argus hunts up
these gentlemen at a coffee-stall, and cables the
terrible statement at great expense.
In the “Fleapit,” on one morning in October, I
saw a city man—“city” in his clothes, anyway —
lunching frugally on a sausage and ale. His frock
coat and tall hat had at one time been beautiful;
now they testified to the poor devil’s
bility. Yet he kept to the habits of his days of
comfort; still talked largely ; still shot at inter-
vals four inches of cuff, of certain age and uncer-
tain cleanliness. On his face-value, he had not a
pound in the world ; later, to test it, I asked him
to drink, and, although I was a stranger, he
accepted with badly concealed eagerness.
Somebody mentioned Australia, and the shabby
city man shot his cuffs again, and said :
“ Ah ! ah ! Australia is in a very bad way.”
“ The drought, you mean,” said I.
“No—that is the least trouble.” He lowered
his voice impressively. “It’s the socialistic legis-
lation that’s ruining it.”
“Do you know Australia?”
“No, sir ; but I can read, and I know how this
Labor legislation is regarded in THE city.”
“ But,” said I, “Maoriland has had a bellyfull of
socialistic legislation, as you call it, and has vastly
improved under it; and England has had Tory
legislation always, and couldn't be worse than it
is—so how do you prove your case? ”
He didn’t attempt it—beyond shooting his sus-
picioned cuffs, again, but he said triumphantly :
“At any rate, sir, while Australia persists in
the present suicidal socialistic legislation, not a
penny of my money will ever go into Australian
securities.”
My poor country ! There’s tuppence lost to
you, anyhow.
And this magnificent pretension of being able
to turn off the seasons at the meter and put a plug
in the sun, is not merely individual—it is tradi-
tional. For over a hundred years the Englishman
has believed in English omnipotence, and it is
only a superstition—a mixture of the Norse Saga
and the Arabian Nights and the Roman fable and
the Greek myth.
Newspapers talk the same magniloquent bosh
as the cuff-shooter of the Fleapit. I read in the
alleged financial column of a London weekly that
a new loan from Natal would be a success, as in-
vestors admired the colony for its loyalty to
England—which is to say that a pawnbroker lends
money for God’s sake.
They are a marvellous people. The Trade
Unionist workmen, as far as I can see, are fol-
lowers of a long dead and exploded superstition
called “Cobdenism.” when it was alive; although
Cobden was the bitterest of Tories —a man who
said that “ Trades Unions are founded upon prin-
ciples of brutal tyranny and monopoly. I would
rather live under a Dey of Algiers than a Trades
Committee.”
England imports £127,000,000 worth of goods
from the United States, much of it iron and steel,
every year ; yet the English Trade Unionist is
alleged to be still a Freetrader. They have a
Government that built a Uganda railway over
which £3,000,000 of money can’t be accounted for
—a Government that allowed its War Office to buy
war horses from Hungarian knackers’ yards, and
yet nobody has been hanged for these things. In
the event of a national crisis, the pink Lord Rose-
bery prattles two columns and a half of cheap
phrasings, and next day England reads the speech
and breathes freely again, and says: “Thank
God ! England is England yet.” If the trouble is
very threatening, Kipling writes a poem, and the
enemy is expected to run for his life.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Little Bush Slave.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was a little girl of four who pleaded:
“ Mummy, earn’ I play ? ”
“No, yer carn’t! Rock the baby, while
Mummy gets Daddy’s dinner ready, or I’ll beat
yer.”
Little barefooted Bessie toddled to the heavy
cradle (a wooden box, nailed to weighty rockers,
made of green ironbark timber), and swayed it to
and fro until one of the rockers flattened out her
little toes.
“ Mummy, me toe —me toe ! ” she wailed.
“ What on earth ’ave yer done now ? Yer alwus
doin’ somethin’. Why carn’t yer be careful?”
growled Mummy, as she bandaged up the sobbing
child’s foot with a piece of old shirt.
“ Put salve on it, Mummy—put salve on it! ”
“ There ain’t no salve ” (ointment made of sting-
ing nettles and fat was the family medicine chest).
“Rock baby agen till Mummy goes to the well
for water, and then Mummy will make some salve
when Daddy has his dinner.”
She had to go a quarter of a mile to the well,
and, judging by the shadows, it was near dinner
time. She picked up her bucket and hurried off,
leaving little Bessie to console herself and her
baby brother. Bessie had learnt to be patient.
She knew that there would be no salve for her
sore foot until Daddy had his dinner, so she
rocked her baby brother to sleep, and began play-
ing with her rag doll.
“’E’s gone asleep, Mummy,” she whispered,
as her mother entered and thoughtlessly thumped
her bucket down on to a wooden stool, wakening
the child.
“Rock him, Bessie; quick! Daddy’s comm,
and the kittle’s not on.”
Bessie began to cry.
“ None of yer cryin’! One cranky kid’s enough,
without you j’inin’ in. Stop it, I tell yer !
d’y’ear ! ” the mother bawled—“or I’ll put yer
down the diggers’ hole.” Bessie suppressed her
sobs and began rocking the baby.
“Shut yer eyes, lovey, and go by-by,” she
coaxed ; but baby, thinking there was something
better to look at than the bark roof, lifted his
head. Bessi® did not notice him, and kept on
rocking. His head bumped against the side of the
cradle, and he began to roar his loudest.
“ That bloomin’ kid’s alwus ’owlin’ when a feller
comes ’orae to ’is dinner. What the ’ell s the
matter with’im ? ”
“ ’E’s bumped ’ees ’ed, Daddy,” replied Bessie.
“ I’ll give it to yer, me lady, for rocken ’im so
’ard ! ” said the mother, smacking Bessie on the
side of the face with her open hand and then pick-
ing up the howling kid.
“ I never done it, Mummy !” sobbed Bessie.
“Yes, yer did ! Rockin’ ’im too ’ard ! ”
“For two pins I’d give yer no dinner, me little
lady ! ” chipped in Daddy.
“ I’ll put hm in the collar ” (the horse’s collar),
said Mummy, “ and yer can just play with ’im till
me and Daddy have our dinner.”
Bessie played with him under the gum. She
thought it better than rocking him, and baby
himself preferred looking at the fowls to the bark
roof.
“ When y’ve done yer dinner, y’ better put some
more seed-wheat to soak in the bluestone water,
and feed them bloomin’ pigs, and don’t ferget to
give ’em water. The’ve been squealing all ther
mornin’,” said Daddy. “I dunno wot yer doin’
’arf yer time,” he muttered to himself.
Mummy hurried off, heedless of her little hungry
children and her promise to Bessie that she would
make salve for her foot.
It was nearly 3 o’clock when Mummy took
baby and gave little Bessie her dinner—a plate
of cold potatoes. Scarcely had she begun her meal
when her mother said, “’Urry up now, so as yer
can play with baby while I bake and get Daddy’s
tea ready ; and don’t let ’im go to sleep, either, or
’e’ll not sleep to-night.”
“Will night be long cornin’, Mummy?” in-
quired Bessie, already weary oMier day’s task.
“Never mind talkin’. ’Urry up, and mind this
kid,” said Mummy, angrily, as she planked the
baby down in the horse-collar, leaving Bessie to
mind him.
“ I alius got ter mind yer ! yer cranky little
debil ; and the night’ll nebber come! ” said
Bessie.
The rays of the setting sun were striking the
tree tops when Mummy took baby, fed him in the
cradle, and told Bessie to rock him to sleep.
“ Oome an’ ’ave yer tea, an’ go to bed outer the
road ! ” said her mother, when baby fell asleep.
While Daddy and Mummy talked about the
crops, the weather, and the pigs, a little girl, lying
on a bag of straw with her clothes for a pillow,
whispered in her dream, “’E’s gone asleep.
Mummy ! Carn’t I play ? ”
P.B.
